10059{_}838{_}"I shall refrain from criticising your conduct of this mill, Mr. Skeelty," he quietly observed, "nor shall I dictate what you may do with your money--provided you succeed in making any."
10059{_}839{_}The manager smiled broadly, as if the retort pleased him.
10059{_}840{_}"Give an' take, sir; that's my motto," he said.
10059{_}841{_}"But you prefer to take?"
10059{_}842{_}"I do," was the cheerful reply.
10059{_}843{_}"I'll take your paper, for instance--if it isn't too high priced."
10059{_}844{_}"In case it is, we will present you with a subscription," said Uncle John.
10059{_}845{_}"But that reminds me: as a part of our bargain I want you to allow my nieces, or any representative of the _Millville Tribune_, to take subscriptions among your workmen."
10059{_}846{_}Mr. Skeelty stared at him a moment.
10059{_}847{_}Then he laughed.
10059{_}848{_}"They're mostly foreigners, Mr. Merrick, who haven't yet fully mastered the English language.
10059{_}849{_}But," he added, thoughtfully, "a few among them might subscribe, if your country sheet contains any news of interest at all.
10059{_}850{_}This is rather a lonely place for my men and they get dissatisfied at times.
10059{_}851{_}All workmen seem chronically dissatisfied, and their women constantly urge them to rebellion.
10059{_}852{_}Already there are grumblings, and they claim they're buried alive in this forlorn forest.
10059{_}853{_}Don't appreciate the advantages of country life, you see, and I've an idea they'll begin to desert, pretty soon.
10059{_}854{_}Really, a live newspaper might do them good--especially if you print a little socialistic drivel now and then."
10059{_}855{_}Again he devoted a moment to thought, and then continued: "Tell you what I'll do, sir; I'll solicit the subscriptions myself, and deduct the price from the men's wages, as I do the cost of their other supplies.
10059{_}856{_}But the Company gets a commission for that, of course."
10059{_}857{_}"It's a penny paper," said Uncle John.
10059{_}858{_}"The subscription is only thirty cents a month."
10059{_}859{_}"Delivered?"
10059{_}860{_}"I suppose so."
10059{_}861{_}"Well, I'll pay you twenty cents, and keep the balance for commission.
10059{_}862{_}That's fair enough."
10059{_}863{_}"Very well, Mr. Skeelty.
10059{_}864{_}We're after subscriptions more than money, just now.
10059{_}865{_}Get all you can, at that rate."
10059{_}866{_}After signing a contract for the supply of electrical power, whereby he was outrageously robbed but the supply was guaranteed, Mr. Merrick and Arthur returned to the farm.
10059{_}867{_}"That man," said Louise's young husband, referring to the manager of the paper mill, "is an unmitigated scoundrel, sir."
10059{_}868{_}"I won't deny it," replied Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}869{_}"It occurs to me he is hiring those poor workmen at low wages and making a profit on all their living necessities, which he reserves the right of supplying from his own store.
10059{_}870{_}No wonder the poor fellows get dissatisfied."
10059{_}871{_}CHAPTER VII  THE SKETCH ARTIST   During the next three days so many things happened at Millville that the natives were in a panic of excitement.
10059{_}872{_}Not only was electricity brought from the paper mill, but a telegraph wire was run from Chazy Junction to Bob West's former storage shed and a telephone gang came along and placed a private wire, with long-distance connections, in the new newspaper office.
10059{_}873{_}The office itself became transformed--"as full o' winders as a hothouse!"
10059{_}874{_}exclaimed Peggy McNutt, with bulging eyes--and neat partitions were placed for the offices.
10059{_}875{_}There was no longer any secret as to the plans of the "nabobs"; it was generally understood that those terribly aggressive girls were going to inflict a daily paper on the community.
10059{_}876{_}Some were glad, and some rebelled, but all were excited.
10059{_}877{_}A perpetual meeting was held at Cotting's store to discuss developments, for something startling occurred every few minutes.
10059{_}878{_}"It's a outrage, this thing," commented young Skim Clark despondently.
10059{_}879{_}"They're tryin' to run mother out o' business--an' she a widder with me to look after!
10059{_}880{_}Most o' the business at the Emporium is done in newspapers an' magazines an' sich; so these gals thought they'd cut under an' take the business away from her."
10059{_}881{_}"Can't the Widder Clark sell the new paper, then?"
10059{_}882{_}asked the blacksmith.
10059{_}883{_}"I dunno.
10059{_}884{_}Hadn't thought o' that," said Skim.
10059{_}885{_}"But the price is to be jus' one cent, an' we've ben gittin' five cents fer all the outside papers.
10059{_}886{_}Where's the profit comin' from, on one cent, I'd like to know?
10059{_}887{_}Why, we make two or three cents on all the five cent papers."
10059{_}888{_}"As fer that," remarked the druggist, "we'll get a cheap paper--if it's any good--an' that's somethin' to be thankful for."
10059{_}889{_}"'Twon't be any good," asserted Skim.
10059{_}890{_}"Ma says so."
10059{_}891{_}But no one except McNutt was prepared to agree with this prediction.
10059{_}892{_}The extensive plans in preparation seemed to indicate that the new paper would be fully equal to the requirements of the populace.
10059{_}893{_}On Monday, when the news spread that two big freight cars had arrived at the Junction, and Nick Thorne began working three teams to haul the outfit to Millville, the rest of the town abandoned all business other than watching the arrival of the drays.
10059{_}894{_}Workmen and machinists arrived from the city and began unpacking and setting up the presses, type cases and all other paraphernalia, every motion being watched by eager faces that lined the windows.
10059{_}895{_}These workmen were lodged at the hotel, which had never entertained so many guests at one time in all its past history.
10059{_}896{_}The three girls, even more excited and full of awe than the townspeople, were at the office early and late, taking note of everything installed and getting by degrees a fair idea of the extent of their new plaything.
10059{_}897{_}"It almost takes my breath away, Uncle," said Patsy.
10059{_}898{_}"You've given the _Tribune_ such a splendid start that we must hustle to make good and prove we are worthy your generosity."
10059{_}899{_}"I sat up last night and wrote a poem for the first page of the first number," announced Louise earnestly.
10059{_}900{_}"Poems don't go on the first page," observed Patsy; "but they're needed to fill in with.
10059{_}901{_}What's it about, dear?"
10059{_}902{_}"It's called 'Ode to a Mignonette,'" answered Louise.
10059{_}903{_}"It begins this way:      "Wee brown blossom, humble and sweet,       Content on my bosom lying,      Who would guess from your quiet dress       The beauty there is lying           Under the rust?"
10059{_}904{_}"Hm," said Patsy, "I don't see as there's any beauty under the rust, at all.
10059{_}905{_}There's no beauty about a mignonette, anyhow, suspected or unsuspected."
10059{_}906{_}"She means 'fragrance,'" suggested Beth.
10059{_}907{_}"Change it to: 'The fragrance there is lying under the rust.'
10059{_}908{_}That'll fix it all right, Louise."
10059{_}909{_}"It doesn't seem right, even then," remarked Uncle John.
10059{_}910{_}"If the fragrance lies under the rust, it can't be smelt, can it?"
10059{_}911{_}"I did not anticipate all this criticism," said Louise, with an air of injured dignity.
10059{_}912{_}"None of the big publishing houses that returned my poems ever said anything mean about them; they merely said they were 'not available.'
10059{_}913{_}However, as this poem has not made a hit with the managing editor, I'll tear it up and write another."
10059{_}914{_}"Don't do that," begged Patsy.
10059{_}915{_}"Save it for emergencies.
10059{_}916{_}We've got to fill twenty-four columns every day, remember!"
10059{_}917{_}By Wednesday night the equipment was fully installed and the workmen departed, leaving only Jim McGaffey, an experienced pressman, and Lawrence Doane--familiarly called Larry--who was to attend to the electrotyping and "make-up."
10059{_}918{_}The press was of the best modern construction, and folded, cut and counted the papers automatically, with a capacity for printing three thousand copies an hour.
10059{_}919{_}"And at that rate," observed Patsy, "It will run off our regular edition in eight minutes."
10059{_}920{_}Aside from the newspaper press there were two "job" presses and an assortment of type for printing anything that might be required, from a calling card to a circus poster.
10059{_}921{_}A third man, who came from the city Thursday morning, was to take charge of the job printing and assist in the newspaper work.
10059{_}922{_}Three girls also arrived, pale-faced, sad-eyed creatures, who were expert typesetters.
10059{_}923{_}Uncle John arranged with Mrs. Kebble, the landlady at the hotel, to board all the "help" at moderate charge.
10059{_}924{_}It had been decided, after much consultation, to make the _Tribune_ a morning paper.
10059{_}925{_}At first it was feared this would result in keeping the girls up nights, but it was finally arranged that all the copy they furnished would be turned in by nine o'clock, and Miss Briggs, the telegraph editor, would attend to anything further that came in over the wires.
10059{_}926{_}The advantages of a morning edition were obvious.
10059{_}927{_}"You'll have all day to distribute a morning paper," Arthur pointed out, "whereas an evening paper couldn't get to your scattered subscribers until the next morning."
10059{_}928{_}Miss Briggs, upon whom they were to rely so greatly, proved to be a woman of tremendous energy and undoubted ability.
10059{_}929{_}She was thirty-five years of age and had been engaged in newspaper work ever since she was eighteen.
10059{_}930{_}Bright and cheerful, of even temper and shrewd comprehension, Miss Briggs listened to the eager explanations of the three girls who had undertaken this queer venture, and assured them she would assist in making a newspaper that would be a credit to them all.
10059{_}931{_}She understood clearly the conditions; that inexperience was backed by ample capital and unpractical ideas by unlimited enthusiasm.
10059{_}932{_}"This job may not last long," she told herself, "but while it does it will be mighty amusing.
10059{_}933{_}I shall enjoy these weeks in a quiet country town after the bustle of the big city."
10059{_}934{_}So here were seven regular employees of the _Millville Daily Tribune_ already secured and the eighth was shortly to appear.
10059{_}935{_}Preparations were well under way for a first edition on the Fourth of July and the office was beginning to hum with work, when one afternoon a girl strolled in and asked in a tired voice for the managing editor.
10059{_}936{_}She was admitted to Patsy's private room, where Beth and Louise were also sitting, and they looked upon their visitor in undisguised astonishment.
10059{_}937{_}She was young: perhaps not over twenty years of age.
10059{_}938{_}Her face bore marks of considerable dissipation and there was a broad scar underneath her right eye.
10059{_}939{_}Her hair was thin, straggling and tow-colored; her eyes large, deep-set and of a faded blue.
10059{_}940{_}The girl's dress was as queer and untidy as her personal appearance, for she wore a brown tailored coat, a short skirt and long, buttoned leggings.
10059{_}941{_}A round cap of the same material as her dress was set jauntily on the back of her head, and over her shoulder was slung a fiat satchel of worn leather.
10059{_}942{_}There was little that was feminine and less that was attractive about the young woman, and Patsy eyed her with distinct disfavor.
10059{_}943{_}"Tommy sent me here," said the newcomer, sinking wearily into a chair.
10059{_}944{_}"I'm hired for a month, on good behavior, with a chance to stay on if I conduct myself in a ladylike manner.
10059{_}945{_}I've been working on the _Herald_, you know; but there was no end of a row last week, and they fired me bodily.
10059{_}946{_}Any booze for sale in this town?"
10059{_}947{_}"It is a temperance community," answered Patsy, stiffly.
10059{_}948{_}"Hooray for me.
10059{_}949{_}There's a chance I'll keep sober.
10059{_}950{_}In that case you've acquired the best sketch artist in America."
10059{_}951{_}"Oh!
10059{_}952{_}Are you the artist, then?"
10059{_}953{_}asked Patsy, with doubtful intonation.
10059{_}954{_}"I don't like the word.
10059{_}955{_}I'm not a real artist--just a cartoonist and newspaper hack.
10059{_}956{_}Say, it's funny to see me in this jungle, isn't it?
10059{_}957{_}What joy I'll have in astonishing the natives!
10059{_}958{_}I s'pose a picture's a picture, to them, and Art an impenetrable mystery.
10059{_}959{_}What sort of stuff do you want me to turn out?"
10059{_}960{_}"I--I'm not sure you'll do," said Miss Doyle, desperately.
10059{_}961{_}"I--we--that is--we are three quite respectable young women who have under-taken to edit the _Millville Daily Tribune_, and the people we have secured to assist us are all--all quite desirable, in their way.
10059{_}962{_}So--; ahem!--so--"  "That's all right," remarked the artist composedly.
10059{_}963{_}"I don't know that I blame you.
10059{_}964{_}I can see very well the atmosphere is not my atmosphere.
10059{_}965{_}When is the next train back to New York?"
10059{_}966{_}"At four o'clock, I believe."
10059{_}967{_}"I'll engage a nice upholstered seat in the smoking car.
10059{_}968{_}But I've several hours to loaf, and loafing is my best stunt.
10059{_}969{_}Isn't this a queer start for girls like you?"
10059{_}970{_}looking around the "den" critically.
10059{_}971{_}"I wonder how you got the bug, and what'll come of it.
10059{_}972{_}It's so funny to see a newspaper office where everything is brand new, and--eminently respectable.
10059{_}973{_}Do you mind my lighting a cigarette?
10059{_}974{_}This sort of a deal is quite interesting to an old-timer like me; but perhaps I owe you an apology for intruding.
10059{_}975{_}I had a letter from Tommy and one from a big banker--Marvin, I guess his name is."
10059{_}976{_}She drew two letters from her satchel and tossed them on the desk before Patsy.
10059{_}977{_}"They're no good to me now," she added.
10059{_}978{_}"Where's your waste basket?"
10059{_}979{_}The managing editor, feeling embarrassed by the presence of the artist, opened the letters.
10059{_}980{_}The first was from Mr. Marvin, Uncle John's banker, saying:  "After much negotiation I have secured for you the best newspaper illustrator in New York, and a girl, too, which is an added satisfaction.
10059{_}981{_}For months I have admired the cartoons signed 'Het' in the New York papers, for they were essentially clever and droll.
10059{_}982{_}Miss Hewitt is highly recommended but like most successful artists is not always to be relied upon.
10059{_}983{_}I'm told if you can manage to win her confidence she will be very loyal to you."
10059{_}984{_}The other letter was from the editor of a great New York journal.
10059{_}985{_}"In giving you Hetty," he said, "I am parting with one of our strongest attractions, but in this big city the poor girl is rapidly drifting to perdition and I want to save her, if possible, before it is too late.
10059{_}986{_}She has a sweet, lovable nature, a generous heart and a keen intellect, but these have been so degraded by drink and dissipation that you may not readily discover them.
10059{_}987{_}My idea is that in a country town, away from all disreputable companionship, the child may find herself, and come to her own again.
10059{_}988{_}Be patient with her and help her all you can.
10059{_}989{_}Her wonderful talent will well repay you, even if you are not interested in saving one of God's creatures."
10059{_}990{_}Silently Patsy passed the letters to Beth and Louise.
10059{_}991{_}After reading them there was a new expression on the faces they turned toward Hetty Hewitt.
10059{_}992{_}"Forgive me," said Patsy, abruptly.
10059{_}993{_}"I--I think I misjudged you.
10059{_}994{_}I was wrong in saying what I did."
10059{_}995{_}"No; you were quite right."
10059{_}996{_}She sat with downcast eyes a moment, musing deeply.
10059{_}997{_}Then she looked up with a smile that quite glorified her wan face.
10059{_}998{_}"I'd like to stay, you know," she said humbly.
10059{_}999{_}"I'm facing a crisis, just now, and on the whole I'd rather straighten up.
10059{_}1000{_}If you feel like giving me a chance I--I'd like to see if I've any reserve force or whether the decency in me has all evaporated."
10059{_}1001{_}"We'll try you; and I'm sure you have lots of reserve force, Hetty," cried Patsy, jumping up impulsively to take the artist's soiled, thin hand in her own.
10059{_}1002{_}"Come with me to the hotel and I'll get you a room.
10059{_}1003{_}Where is your baggage?"
10059{_}1004{_}"Didn't bring it.
10059{_}1005{_}I wasn't sure I'd like the country, or that you'd care to trust me.
10059{_}1006{_}In New York they know me for what I'm worth, and I get lots of work and good advice--mixed with curses."
10059{_}1007{_}"We'll send for your trunk," said Patsy, leading the girl up the street.
10059{_}1008{_}"No; it's in hock.
10059{_}1009{_}But I won't need it.
10059{_}1010{_}With no booze to buy I can invest my earnings in wearing apparel.
10059{_}1011{_}What a picturesque place this is!
10059{_}1012{_}Way back in the primitive; no hint of those namby-pamby green meadows and set rows of shade trees that make most country towns detestable; rocks and boulders--boulders and rocks--and the scraggly pines for background.
10059{_}1013{_}The wee brook has gone crazy.
10059{_}1014{_}What do you call it?"
10059{_}1015{_}"Little Bill Creek."
10059{_}1016{_}"I'm going to stab it with my pencil.
10059{_}1017{_}Where it bumps the rocks it's obstinate and pig-headed; where it leaps the little shelves of slate it's merry and playful; where it sweeps silently between the curving banks it is sulky and resentful.
10059{_}1018{_}The Little Bill has moods, bless its heart!
10059{_}1019{_}Moods betoken character."
10059{_}1020{_}Patsy secured for Hetty a pleasant room facing the creek.
10059{_}1021{_}"Where will you work, at the office or here?"
10059{_}1022{_}she asked.
10059{_}1023{_}"In the open, I guess.
10059{_}1024{_}I'll run over the telegraph news to get a subject for the day's cartoon, and then take to the woods.
10059{_}1025{_}Let me know what other pictures you want and I'll do 'em on the run.
10059{_}1026{_}I'm a beast to work."
10059{_}1027{_}Arthur Weldon, in his capacity as advertising manager, wrote to all the national advertisers asking their patronage for the _Millville Daily Tribune_.
10059{_}1028{_}The letters were typewritten by the office stenographer on newly printed letterheads that Fitzgerald, the job printer, had prepared.
10059{_}1029{_}Some of the advertisers were interested enough in Arthur's novel proposition to reply with questions as to the circulation of the new paper, where it was distributed, and the advertising rates.
10059{_}1030{_}The voting man answered frankly that they had 27 subscribers already and were going to distribute 400 free copies every day, for a time, as samples, with the hope of increasing the subscription list.
10059{_}1031{_}"I am not sure you will derive any benefit at all from advertising in our paper," he added; "but we would like to have you try it, and you can pay us whatever you consider the results warrant."
10059{_}1032{_}To his astonishment the advertisements arrived, a great many from very prominent firms, who accepted his proposal with amusement at his originality and a desire to help the new venture along.
10059{_}1033{_}"Our square statement of facts has given us a good start," he told the girls.
10059{_}1034{_}"I'm really amazed at our success, and it's up to you to make a paper that will circulate and make trade for these trustful advertisers."
10059{_}1035{_}With the local merchants the results were less satisfying.
10059{_}1036{_}Bob West put in a card advertising his hardware business and Nib Corkins cautiously invested a half dollar to promote his drug store and stock of tarnished cheap jewelry; but Sam Cotting said everybody knew what he had for sale and advertising wouldn't help him any.
10059{_}1037{_}Arthur drove to Huntingdon with Louise and while the society editor picked up items her husband interviewed the merchants.
10059{_}1038{_}The Huntingdon people were more interested in the new paper than the Millville folk, and Arthur quoted such low prices that several advertisements were secured.
10059{_}1039{_}Two bright boys of this thriving village were also employed to ride over to Millville each morning, get a supply of _Tribunes_ and distribute a sample copy to every house in the neighborhood.
10059{_}1040{_}"Fitz" set up the "ads" in impressive type and the columns of the first edition began to fill up days before the Fourth of July arrived.
10059{_}1041{_}Louise had a story and two poems set in type and read over the proofs dozens of times with much pride and satisfaction, while Beth prepared an article on the history of baseball and the probable future of our national game.
10059{_}1042{_}They did not see much of their artist during the first days following her arrival, but one afternoon she brought Patsy a sketch and asked:  "Who is this?"
10059{_}1043{_}Patsy glanced at it and laughed gleefully.
10059{_}1044{_}It was Peggy McNutt, the fish-eyed pooh-bah of Millville, who was represented sitting on his front porch engaged in painting his wooden foot.
10059{_}1045{_}This was one of McNutt's recognized amusements.
10059{_}1046{_}He kept a supply of paints of many colors, and every few days appeared with his rudely carved wooden foot glistening with a new coat of paint and elaborately striped.
10059{_}1047{_}Sometimes it would be blue with yellow stripes, then green with red stripes, and anon a lovely pink decorated with purple.
10059{_}1048{_}One drawback to Peggy's delight in these transformations was the fact that it took the paint a night and a day to dry thoroughly, and during this period of waiting he would sit upon his porch with the wooden foot tenderly resting upon the rail--a helpless prisoner.
10059{_}1049{_}"Some folks," he would say, "likes pretty neckties; an' some wears fancy socks; but fer my part I'd ruther show a han'some foot ner anything.
10059{_}1050{_}It don't cost as much as wearin' socks an' neckties, an' it's more artistic like."
10059{_}1051{_}Hetty had caught the village character in the act of striping the wooden foot, and his expression of intense interest in the operation was so original, and the likeness so perfect, from the string suspenders and flannel shirt to the antiquated straw hat and faded and patched overalls, that no one would be likely to mistake the subject.
10059{_}1052{_}The sketch was entitled "The Village Artist," and Patsy declared they would run it on an inside page, just to make the Millville people aware of the "power of the press."
10059{_}1053{_}Larry made an etching of it and mounted the plate for a double column picture.
10059{_}1054{_}The original sketch Patsy decided to have framed and to hang it in her office.
10059{_}1055{_}CHAPTER VIII  THE MILLVILLE DAILY TRIBUNE   The first edition of the _Millville Daily Tribune_ certainly proved it to be a wonderful newspaper.
10059{_}1056{_}The telegraphic news of the world's doings, received and edited by the skillful Miss Briggs, was equal to that of any metropolitan journal; the first page cartoon, referring to the outbreak of a rebellion in China, was clever and humorous enough to delight anyone; but the local news and "literary page" were woefully amateurish and smacked of the schoolgirl editors who had prepared them.
10059{_}1057{_}Perhaps the Chazy County people did not recognize these deficiencies, for the new paper certainly created a vast amount of excitement and won the praise of nearly all who read it.
10059{_}1058{_}On the eventful night of the _Tribune's_ "first run" our girls were too eager to go home and await its appearance, so they remained at the office to see the birth of their enterprise, and as it was the night preceding the Fourth of July Uncle John gave an exhibition of fireworks in front of the newspaper office, to the delight of the entire population.
10059{_}1059{_}The girl journalists, however, were not so greatly interested in fireworks as in the birth of their fascinating enterprise.
10059{_}1060{_}Wearing long gingham aprons they hovered over the big table where the forms were being locked up, and watched anxiously every movement of the workmen.
10059{_}1061{_}It was exceedingly interesting to note how a column of the first page was left open until the last, so that copy "hot from the wire" of the very latest news might be added before going to press.
10059{_}1062{_}Finally, at exactly two o'clock, the forms were locked, placed upon the bed of the press, and McGaffey, a sour-faced individual whose chief recommendation was his ability as a pressman, began to make ready for the "run."
10059{_}1063{_}Outside the brilliantly lighted windows, which were left open for air, congregated a wondering group of the Millville people, many of whom had never been up so late before in all their lives.
10059{_}1064{_}But the event was too important to miss.
10059{_}1065{_}The huge, complicated press had already inspired their awe, and they were eager to "see it work" as it printed the new paper.
10059{_}1066{_}The girls tolerated this native curiosity with indulgent good humor and at midnight even passed out sandwiches to the crowd, a supply having been secured for the workmen.
10059{_}1067{_}These were accepted silently, and as they munched the food all kept their eyes fixed upon the magicians within.
10059{_}1068{_}There was a hitch somewhere; McGaffey muttered naughty words under his breath and plied wrenches and screwdrivers in a way that brought a thrill of anxiety, approaching fear, to every heart.
10059{_}1069{_}The press started half a dozen times, only to be shut down abruptly before it had printed a single impression.
10059{_}1070{_}McGaffey counseled with Larry, who shook his head.
10059{_}1071{_}Fitzgerald, the job printer, examined the machinery carefully and again McGaffey screwed nuts and regulated the press.
10059{_}1072{_}Then he turned on the power; the big cylinder revolved; the white paper reeled out like a long ribbon and with a rattle and thump the first copy of the _Millville Daily Tribune_ was deposited, cut and folded, upon the table placed to receive it.
10059{_}1073{_}Patsy made a rush for it, but before she could reach the table half a dozen more papers had been piled above it, and gathering speed the great press hummed busily and the pile of _Tribunes_ grew as if by magic.
10059{_}1074{_}Patsy grabbed the first dozen and handed them to Beth, for they were to be reserved as souvenirs.
10059{_}1075{_}Then, running back to the table, she seized a bunch and began distributing them to the watchers outside the window.
10059{_}1076{_}The natives accepted them eagerly enough, but could not withdraw their eyes from the marvelous press, which seemed to possess intelligence almost human.
10059{_}1077{_}Each of the three girl journalists now had a copy in hand, scanning it with boundless pride and satisfaction.
10059{_}1078{_}It realized completely their fondest hopes and they had good cause to rejoice.
10059{_}1079{_}Then Uncle John, who ought to have been in bed and sound asleep at this uncanny hour of night, came bouncing in, accompanied by Arthur Weldon.
10059{_}1080{_}Each made a dive for a paper and each face wore an expression of genuine delight.
10059{_}1081{_}The roar of the press made conversation difficult, but Mr. Merrick caught his nieces in his arms, by turn, and gave each one an ecstatic hug and kiss.
10059{_}1082{_}Suddenly the press stopped.
10059{_}1083{_}"What's wrong, McGaffey?"
10059{_}1084{_}demanded Patsy, anxiously.
10059{_}1085{_}"Nothing, miss.
10059{_}1086{_}Edition off, that's all."
10059{_}1087{_}"What!
10059{_}1088{_}the entire four hundred are printed?"
10059{_}1089{_}"Four twenty-five.
10059{_}1090{_}I run a few extrys."
10059{_}1091{_}And now a shriek of laughter came from the windows as the villagers, slowly opening the papers they held, came upon the caricature of Peggy McNutt.
10059{_}1092{_}The subject of the cartoon had, with his usual aggressiveness, secured the best "standing room" available, and his contemplative, protruding eyes were yet fixed upon the interior of the workroom.
10059{_}1093{_}But now, his curiosity aroused, he looked at the paper to see what his neighbors were laughing at, and his expression of wonder slowly changed to a broad grin.
10059{_}1094{_}He straightened up, looked triumphantly around the circle and exclaimed:  "By gum, folks, this 'ere paper's going to be a go!
10059{_}1095{_}I didn't take no stock in it till now, but them fool gals seem to know their business, an' I'll back 'em to the last ditch!"
10059{_}1096{_}CHAPTER IX  TROUBLE   Of course the girls exhausted their store of "effusions" on the first two or three papers.
10059{_}1097{_}A daily eats up "copy" very fast and the need to supply so much material began to bewilder the budding journalists.
10059{_}1098{_}There was not sufficient local news to keep them going, but fortunately the New York news service supplied more general news than they could possibly use, and, besides, Mr. Marvin, foreseeing this dilemma, had sent on several long, stout boxes filled with "plate matter," which meant that a variety of stories, poems, special articles and paragraphs of every sort had been made into stereotyped plates of column width which could be placed anywhere in the paper where a space needed to be filled.
10059{_}1099{_}This material, having been prepared by skilled writers, was of excellent character, so that the paper gained in its class of contents as the girlish contributions began to be replaced by "plates."
10059{_}1100{_}The nieces did not abandon writing, however, and all three worked sedulously to prepare copy so that at least one column of the Tribune each day was filled with notes from their pens.
10059{_}1101{_}Subscriptions came in freely during those first days, for farmers and villagers alike were proud of their local daily and the price was so low that no one begrudged the investment.
10059{_}1102{_}But Uncle John well knew that if every individual in the county subscribed, and the advertising patronage doubled, the income would fall far short of running expenses.
10059{_}1103{_}Saturday night, when the pay roll had to be met, the girls consulted together seriously.
10059{_}1104{_}In spite of the new subscriptions received, a deficiency must be supplied, and they quietly advanced the money from their private purses.
10059{_}1105{_}This was no great hardship, for each had an ample allowance from Uncle John, as well as an income from property owned in her own name.
10059{_}1106{_}"It's only about thirty dollars apiece," said Patsy.
10059{_}1107{_}"I guess we can stand that until--until more money begins coming in."
10059{_}1108{_}On Saturday evening there was an invasion of workmen from Royal, many of whom we're rough foreigners who came to Millville in search of excitement, as a relief from their week's confinement at the pine woods settlement at the mill.
10059{_}1109{_}Skeelty, who thought he knew how to manage these people, allowed every man, at the close of work on Saturday, to purchase a pint of whiskey from the company store, charging an exorbitant price that netted a huge profit.
10059{_}1110{_}There was no strong drink to be had at Millville, so the workmen brought their bottles to town, carousing on the way, and thought it amusing to frighten the simple inhabitants of the village by their rude shouts and ribald songs.
10059{_}1111{_}This annoyance had occurred several times since the establishment of the mill, and Bob West had protested vigorously to Mr. Skeelty for giving his men whiskey and turning them loose in a respectable community; but the manager merely grinned and said he must keep "the boys" satisfied at all hazards, and it was the business of the Millville people to protect themselves if the workmen became too boisterous.
10059{_}1112{_}On this Saturday evening the girls were standing on the sidewalk outside the printing office, awaiting the arrival of Arthur with the surrey, when a group of the Royal workmen appeared in the dim light, swaggering three abreast and indulging in offensive language.
10059{_}1113{_}Uncle John's nieces withdrew to the protection of the doorway, but a big bearded fellow in a red shirt discovered them, and, lurching forward, pushed his evil countenance in Patsy's face, calling to his fellows in harsh tones that he had "found a partner for a dance."
10059{_}1114{_}An instant later he received a swinging blow above the ear that sent him sprawling at full length upon the sidewalk, and a quiet voice said:  "Pardon me, ladies; it seemed necessary."
10059{_}1115{_}All three at once recognized the supposed tramp whom they had seen the morning of their arrival, but whom Uncle John had reported to be one of the bookkeepers at the paper mill.
10059{_}1116{_}The young fellow had no time to say more, for the downfall of their comrade brought a shout of rage from the group of workmen, numbering nearly a dozen, and with one accord they rushed upon the man who had dared champion the defenseless girls.
10059{_}1117{_}Beth managed to open the door of the office, through which Patsy and Louise slipped instantly, but the younger girl, always cool in emergencies, held the door ajar while she cried to the young man:  "Quick, sir--come inside!"
10059{_}1118{_}Really, he had no time to obey, just then.
10059{_}1119{_}With his back to the door he drove his fists at his assailants in a dogged, persistent way that felled three more of them before the others drew away from his stalwart bows.
10059{_}1120{_}By that time Larry and Fitzgerald, who had been summoned by Louise, rushed from the office armed with iron bars caught up at random, both eager for a fight.
10059{_}1121{_}The workmen, seeing the reinforcements, beat a retreat, carrying their sadly pommeled comrades with them, but their insulting language was not restricted until they had passed out of hearing.
10059{_}1122{_}Then the young man turned, bowed gravely to the girls, who had now ventured forth again, and without waiting to receive their thanks marched calmly down the street.
10059{_}1123{_}When Arthur reached home with the girls, Mr. Merrick was very indignant at his report of the adventure.
10059{_}1124{_}He denounced Skeelty in unmeasured terms and declared he would find a way to protect Millville from further invasion by these rough and drunken workmen.
10059{_}1125{_}There was no Sunday paper, so the girlish editors found the morrow a veritable day of rest.
10059{_}1126{_}They all drove to Hooker's Falls to church and returned to find that old Nora had prepared a fine chicken dinner for them.
10059{_}1127{_}Patsy had invited Hetty Hewitt, in whom she was now greatly interested, to dine with them, and to the astonishment of all the artist walked over to the farm arrayed in a new gown, having discarded the disreputable costume in which she had formerly appeared.
10059{_}1128{_}The new dress was not in the best of taste and its loud checks made dainty Louise shudder, but somehow Hetty seemed far more feminine than before, and she had, moreover, washed herself carefully and tried to arrange her rebellious hair.
10059{_}1129{_}"This place is doing me good," she confided to her girl employers, after dinner, when they were seated in a group upon the lawn.
10059{_}1130{_}"I'm getting over my nervousness, and although I haven't drank a drop stronger than water since I arrived.
10059{_}1131{_}I feel a new sort of energy coursing through my veins.
10059{_}1132{_}Also I eat like a trooper--not at night, as I used to, but at regular mealtime.
10059{_}1133{_}And I'm behaving quite like a lady.
10059{_}1134{_}Do you know, I wouldn't be surprised to find it just as amusing to be respectable as to--to be--the other thing?"
10059{_}1135{_}"You will find it far more satisfactory, I'm sure," replied Patsy encouragingly.
10059{_}1136{_}"What most surprises me is that with your talent and education you ever got into such bad ways."
10059{_}1137{_}"Environment," said Hetty.
10059{_}1138{_}"That's what did it.
10059{_}1139{_}When I first went to New York I was very young.
10059{_}1140{_}A newspaper man took me out to dinner and asked me to have a cocktail.
10059{_}1141{_}I looked around the tables and saw other girls drinking cocktails, so I took one.
10059{_}1142{_}That was where I turned into the rocky road.
10059{_}1143{_}People get careless around the newspaper offices.
10059{_}1144{_}They work under a constant nervous strain and find that drink steadies them--for a time.
10059{_}1145{_}By and by they disappear; others take their places, and they are never heard of again except in the police courts.
10059{_}1146{_}I knew a girl, society editor of a big paper, who drew her five thousand a year, at one time.
10059{_}1147{_}She got the cocktail habit and a week or so ago I paid her fine for getting pinched while intoxicated.
10059{_}1148{_}She was in rags and hadn't a red cent.
10059{_}1149{_}That set me thinking, and when Tommy fired me from his paper and said the best he could do was to get me a job in the country, it seemed as if my chance to turn over a new leaf had arrived.
10059{_}1150{_}I've turned it," she added, with a pathetic sigh; "but whether it'll stay turned, or not, is a question for the puzzle page."
10059{_}1151{_}"Haven't you a family to look after you--or for you to look after?"
10059{_}1152{_}asked Beth.
10059{_}1153{_}"No.
10059{_}1154{_}Brother and I were left orphans in a Connecticut town, and he went out West, to Chicago, and promised to send for me.
10059{_}1155{_}Must have forgot that promise, I guess, for I've never heard of Dan since.
10059{_}1156{_}I could draw pictures, so I went to New York and found a job.
10059{_}1157{_}Guess that's my biography, and it isn't as interesting as one of Hearst's editorials, either."
10059{_}1158{_}Hetty seemed pleased and grateful to note the frank friendliness of her girlish employers, in whom she recognized the admirable qualities she had personally sacrificed for a life of dissipation.
10059{_}1159{_}In the privacy of her room at the hotel she had read the first copy of the Millville Tribune and shrieked with laughter at the ingenuous editorials and schoolgirl essays.
10059{_}1160{_}Then she grew sober and thoughtful, envying in her heart the sweetness and simplicity so apparent in every line.
10059{_}1161{_}Here were girls who possessed something infinitely higher than journalistic acumen; they were true women, with genuine womanly qualities and natures that betrayed their worth at a glance, as do ingots of refined gold.
10059{_}1162{_}What would not this waif from the grim underworld of New York have given for such clear eyes, pure mind and unsullied heart?
10059{_}1163{_}"I don't know as I can ever swim in their pond," Hetty reflected, with honest regret, "but there's a chance I can look folks square in the eye again--and that wouldn't be so bad."
10059{_}1164{_}Monday morning, when Patsy, Louise and Beth drove to their office, Miss Briggs said nonchalantly:  "McGaffey's gone."
10059{_}1165{_}"Gone!
10059{_}1166{_}Gone where?"
10059{_}1167{_}asked Patsy.
10059{_}1168{_}"Back to New York.
10059{_}1169{_}Caught a freight from the Junction Saturday night."
10059{_}1170{_}"Isn't he coming back?"
10059{_}1171{_}inquired Beth.
10059{_}1172{_}"Here's a letter he left," said Miss Briggs.
10059{_}1173{_}They read it together.
10059{_}1174{_}It was very brief; "Climate don't suit me.
10059{_}1175{_}No excitement.
10059{_}1176{_}I've quit.
10059{_}1177{_}McGaffey."
10059{_}1178{_}"I suppose," said Patsy, with indignation, "he intended to go, all the while, and only waited for his Saturday pay."
10059{_}1179{_}Miss Briggs nodded.
10059{_}1180{_}She was at the telegraph instrument.
10059{_}1181{_}"What shall we do?"
10059{_}1182{_}asked Louise.
10059{_}1183{_}"Can anyone else work the press?"
10059{_}1184{_}"I'll find out," said Patsy, marching into the workroom.
10059{_}1185{_}Neither Fitz nor Larry would undertake to run the press.
10059{_}1186{_}They said the machine was so complicated it required an expert, and unless an experienced pressman could be secured the paper must suspend publication.
10059{_}1187{_}Here was an unexpected dilemma; one that for a time dazed them.
10059{_}1188{_}"These things always happen in the newspaper business," remarked Miss Briggs, when appealed to.
10059{_}1189{_}"Can't you telegraph to New York for another pressman?"
10059{_}1190{_}"Yes; but he can't get here in time," said Patsy.
10059{_}1191{_}"There's no Monday train to Chazy Junction, at all, and it would be Wednesday morning before a man could possibly arrive.
10059{_}1192{_}To shut down the paper would ruin it, for everyone would think we had failed in our attempt and it might take us weeks to regain public confidence."
10059{_}1193{_}"I know," said Miss Briggs, composedly.
10059{_}1194{_}"A paper never stops.
10059{_}1195{_}Somehow or other it always keeps going--even if the world turns somersaults and stands on its head.
10059{_}1196{_}You'll find a way, I'm sure."
10059{_}1197{_}But the bewildered girls had no such confidence.
10059{_}1198{_}They drove back to the farm to consult with Uncle John and Arthur.
10059{_}1199{_}"Let's take a look at that press, my dears," said Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}1200{_}"I'm something of a mechanic myself, or was in my young days, and I may be able to work this thing until we can get a new pressman."
10059{_}1201{_}"I'll help you," said Arthur.
10059{_}1202{_}"Anyone who can run an automobile ought to be able to manage a printing press."
10059{_}1203{_}So they went to the office, took off their coats and examined the press; but the big machine defied their combined intelligence.
10059{_}1204{_}Uncle John turned on the power.
10059{_}1205{_}The cylinder groaned, swung half around, and then the huge wooden "nippers" came down upon the table with a force that shattered them to kindlings.
10059{_}1206{_}At the crash Mr. Merrick involuntarily shut down the machine, and then they all stood around and looked gloomily at the smash-up and wondered if the damage was irreparable.
10059{_}1207{_}"Couldn't we print the paper on the job press?"
10059{_}1208{_}asked the little millionaire, turning to Fitzgerald.
10059{_}1209{_}"In sections, sir," replied Fitz, grinning.
10059{_}1210{_}"Half a page at a time is all we can manage, but we might be able to match margins so the thing could be read."
10059{_}1211{_}"We'll try it," said Uncle John.
10059{_}1212{_}"Do your best, my man, and if you can help us out of this bog you shall be amply rewarded."
10059{_}1213{_}Fitz looked grave.
10059{_}1214{_}"Never knew of such a thing being done, sir," he remarked; "but that's no reason it's impossible."
10059{_}1215{_}"'Twill be a horror of a make-up," added Larry, who did not relish his part in the experiment.
10059{_}1216{_}Uncle John put on his coat and went into the front office, followed by Arthur and the girls in dismal procession.
10059{_}1217{_}"A man to see the manager," announced Miss Briggs, nodding toward a quiet figure seated on the "waiting bench."
10059{_}1218{_}The man stood up and bowed.
10059{_}1219{_}It was the young bookkeeper from the paper mill, who had so bravely defended the girls on Saturday night.
10059{_}1220{_}Uncle John regarded him with a frown.
10059{_}1221{_}"I suppose Skeelty has sent you to apologize," he said.
10059{_}1222{_}"No, sir; Skeelty is not in an apologetic mood," replied the man, smiling.
10059{_}1223{_}"He has fired me."
10059{_}1224{_}"What for?"
10059{_}1225{_}"Interfering with his workmen.
10059{_}1226{_}The boys didn't like what I did the other night and threatened to strike unless I was put in the discard."
10059{_}1227{_}"And now?
10059{_}1228{_}asked Uncle John, looking curiously at the man.
10059{_}1229{_}"I'm out of work and would like a job, sir."
10059{_}1230{_}"What can you do?"
10059{_}1231{_}"Anything."
10059{_}1232{_}"That means nothing at all."
10059{_}1233{_}"I beg your pardon.
10059{_}1234{_}Let me say that I'm not afraid to tackle anything."
10059{_}1235{_}"Can you run a power printing press?"
10059{_}1236{_}"Yes, sir."
10059{_}1237{_}"Ever had any experience?"
10059{_}1238{_}The young man hesitated.
10059{_}1239{_}"I'm not sure," he replied slowly; "but I think I have."
10059{_}1240{_}This statement would not have been encouraging under ordinary circumstances, but in this emergency Uncle John accepted it.
10059{_}1241{_}"What is your name?"
10059{_}1242{_}he asked.
10059{_}1243{_}Another moment's hesitation.
10059{_}1244{_}"Call me Smith, please."
10059{_}1245{_}"First name?"
10059{_}1246{_}The man smiled.
10059{_}1247{_}"Thursday," he said.
10059{_}1248{_}All his hearers seemed astonished at this peculiar name, but Mr. Merrick said abruptly: "Follow me, Thursday Smith."
10059{_}1249{_}The man obeyed, and the girls and Arthur trotted after them back to the pressroom.
10059{_}1250{_}"Our pressman has deserted us without warning," explained Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}1251{_}"None of our other employees is able to run the thing.
10059{_}1252{_}If you can master it so as to run off the paper tonight, the job is yours."
10059{_}1253{_}Thursday Smith took off his jacket--a cheap khaki affair--and rolled up his sleeves.
10059{_}1254{_}Then he carefully looked over the press and found the damaged nippers.
10059{_}1255{_}Without a word he picked up a wrench, released the stub ends of the broken fingers, gathered the pieces in his hand and asked: "Where is there a carpenter shop?"
10059{_}1256{_}"Can you operate this press?"
10059{_}1257{_}asked Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}1258{_}"Yes, sir."
10059{_}1259{_}"The carpenter shop is a little shanty back of the hotel.
10059{_}1260{_}You'll find Lon Taft there."
10059{_}1261{_}Smith walked away, and Mr. Merrick drew a long breath of relief.
10059{_}1262{_}"That's good luck," he said.
10059{_}1263{_}"You may quit worrying, now, my dears."
10059{_}1264{_}"Are you sure he's a good pressman, Uncle?"
10059{_}1265{_}"No; but _he_ is sure.
10059{_}1266{_}I've an idea he wouldn't attempt the thing, otherwise."
10059{_}1267{_}Mr. Merrick returned to the farm, while Arthur drove Louise over to Huntingdon to gather items for the paper, and Patsy and Beth sat in the office arranging copy.
10059{_}1268{_}In an hour Smith came back with new nippers, which he fitted to the steel frame.
10059{_}1269{_}Then he oiled the press, started it going a few revolutions, to test its condition, and handled the machinery so dexterously and with such evident confidence that Larry nodded to Fitz and muttered, "He'll do."
10059{_}1270{_}McGaffey, knowing he was about to decamp, had not kept the press very clean; but Thursday Smith put in the afternoon and evening removing grease, polishing and rubbing, until the huge machine shone resplendent.
10059{_}1271{_}The girls went home at dinner time, but they sent Arthur to the office at midnight to see if the new pressman was proving capable.
10059{_}1272{_}The Tuesday morning _Tribune_ greeted them at the breakfast table, and the presswork was remarkably clean and distinct.
10059{_}1273{_}CHAPTER X  THURSDAY SMITH   In a day or so Mr. Merrick received a letter from Mr. Skeelty, the manager of the paper mill.
10059{_}1274{_}He said: "I understand you have employed one of my discharged workmen, who is named Thursday Smith.
10059{_}1275{_}My men don't want him in this neighborhood, and have made a strong protest.
10059{_}1276{_}I therefore desire you to discharge the fellow at once, and in case you refuse to accede to this reasonable demand I shall shut off your power."
10059{_}1277{_}Mr. Merrick replied: "Shut off the power and I'll sue you for damages.
10059{_}1278{_}My contract with you fully protects me.
10059{_}1279{_}Permit me a request in turn: that you mind your own business.
10059{_}1280{_}The _Millville Tribune_ will employ whomsoever it chooses."
10059{_}1281{_}Uncle John said nothing to the girls concerning this correspondence, nor did he mention it to the new pressman.
10059{_}1282{_}On Wednesday Larry and Fitz sent in their "resignations," to take effect Saturday night.
10059{_}1283{_}They told Patsy, who promptly interviewed them, that the town was altogether too slow for men accustomed to the city, but to Smith they admitted they feared trouble from the men at the mill.
10059{_}1284{_}"I talked with one of the mill hands last night," said Larry, "and they're up to mischief.
10059{_}1285{_}If you stay here, my boy, you'd better watch out, for it's you they're after, in the first place, and Skeelty has told 'em he wouldn't be annoyed if they wiped out the whole newspaper plant at the same time."
10059{_}1286{_}Thursday nodded but said nothing.
10059{_}1287{_}He began watching the work of the two men with comprehensive care.
10059{_}1288{_}When Mr. Merrick came down to the office during the forenoon to consult with his nieces about replacing the two men who had resigned, Smith asked him for a private interview.
10059{_}1289{_}"Come into the office," said Uncle John.
10059{_}1290{_}When the man found the three girl journalists present he hesitated, but Mr. Merrick declared they were the ones most interested in anything an employee of the paper might have to say to his principals.
10059{_}1291{_}"I am told, sir," Thursday began, "that the people at the mill have boycotted this paper."
10059{_}1292{_}"They've cancelled all their subscriptions," replied Beth; "but as they had not paid for them it won't hurt us any."
10059{_}1293{_}"It seems the trouble started through your employing me," resumed the young man; "so it will be best for you to let me go."
10059{_}1294{_}"Never!"
10059{_}1295{_}cried Mr. Merrick, firmly.
10059{_}1296{_}"Do you suppose I'll allow that rascal Skeelty to dictate to us for a single minute?
10059{_}1297{_}Not by a jug full!
10059{_}1298{_}And the reason the men dislike you is because you pounded some of them unmercifully when they annoyed my girls.
10059{_}1299{_}Where did you learn to use your fists so cleverly, Smith?"
10059{_}1300{_}"I don't know, sir."
10059{_}1301{_}"Well, you have earned our gratitude, and we're going to stand by you.
10059{_}1302{_}I don't mind a bit of a row, when I'm on the right side of an argument.
10059{_}1303{_}Do you?"
10059{_}1304{_}"Not at all, sir; but the young ladies--"  "They're pretty good fighters, too; so don't worry."
10059{_}1305{_}Thursday was silent a moment.
10059{_}1306{_}Then he said:  "Fitzgerald and Doane tell me they're going to quit, Saturday."
10059{_}1307{_}"It is true," replied Patsy.
10059{_}1308{_}"I'm sorry, for they seem good men and we may have trouble replacing them."
10059{_}1309{_}"They are not needed here, Miss Doyle," said Smith.
10059{_}1310{_}"There isn't a great deal of electrotyping to do, or much job printing.
10059{_}1311{_}More than half the time the two men are idle.
10059{_}1312{_}It's the same way with my own job.
10059{_}1313{_}Three hours a day will take care of the press and make the regular run.
10059{_}1314{_}If you will permit me, I am sure I can attend to all the work, unaided."
10059{_}1315{_}They looked at one another in amazement.
10059{_}1316{_}"How about the make-up?"
10059{_}1317{_}asked Uncle John.
10059{_}1318{_}"I can manage that easily, sir.
10059{_}1319{_}I've been watching the operation and understand it perfectly."
10059{_}1320{_}"And you believe you can do the work of three men?"
10059{_}1321{_}"Three men were unnecessary in a small plant like this, sir.
10059{_}1322{_}Whoever sent them to you did not understand very well your requirements.
10059{_}1323{_}I've been watching the compositors, too, and your three girls are one too many.
10059{_}1324{_}Two are sisters, and can set all the type very easily.
10059{_}1325{_}I recommend that you send the other back to New York."
10059{_}1326{_}They considered this advice seriously.
10059{_}1327{_}"I think Mr. Smith is right," observed Patsy.
10059{_}1328{_}"The girls have not seemed busy, at all, and spend most of their time laughing and talking together."
10059{_}1329{_}"It will cut down expenses a lot," said Beth, "and I'm sure we ought to be able to run this paper more economically than we have been doing."
10059{_}1330{_}Uncle John looked at the man thoughtfully.
10059{_}1331{_}"Where did you learn the printing business?"
10059{_}1332{_}he asked.
10059{_}1333{_}"I--I don't know, sir."
10059{_}1334{_}"What offices have you worked in?"
10059{_}1335{_}"I cannot tell you that, sir."
10059{_}1336{_}"You seem to answer all my questions with the statement that you 'don't know,'" asserted Mr. Merrick, with an annoyed frown.
10059{_}1337{_}"Is there any reason you should refuse to tell us of your former life?"
10059{_}1338{_}"None whatever, sir."
10059{_}1339{_}"Who are you, Smith?"
10059{_}1340{_}"I--I don't know, sir."
10059{_}1341{_}Mr. Merrick was getting provoked.
10059{_}1342{_}"This obstinacy is not likely to win our confidence," he said.
10059{_}1343{_}"Under the circumstances I think we ought to know something more about you, before we allow you to undertake so much responsibility.
10059{_}1344{_}You seem a bright, able young man, and I've no doubt you understand the work you're about to undertake, but if we have no knowledge of your antecedents you may cause us considerable future trouble."
10059{_}1345{_}Smith bowed his head and his cheeks flamed red.
10059{_}1346{_}"I have no knowledge of my antecedents to confide to you, sir," he said in a low voice.
10059{_}1347{_}Uncle John sighed regretfully and turned away, but Patsy looked at the man with new interest.
10059{_}1348{_}"Won't you please explain that a little more fully?"
10059{_}1349{_}she gently inquired.
10059{_}1350{_}"I am quite willing to tell all I know," said he; "but that is very little, I assure you.
10059{_}1351{_}Two years ago last May, on the morning of Thursday, the twenty-second, I awoke to find myself lying in a ditch beside a road.
10059{_}1352{_}Of my life previous to that time I have no knowledge whatever."
10059{_}1353{_}The three girls regarded him with startled eyes.
10059{_}1354{_}Uncle John turned from the window to examine the young man with new interest.
10059{_}1355{_}"Were you injured?"
10059{_}1356{_}he asked.
10059{_}1357{_}"My right ankle was sprained and I had a cut under my left eye--you can see the scar still."
10059{_}1358{_}"You have no idea how you came there?"
10059{_}1359{_}"Not the slightest.
10059{_}1360{_}I did not recognize the surrounding country; I had no clear impression as to who I was.
10059{_}1361{_}There was a farmhouse a quarter of a mile away; I limped to it and they gave me some breakfast.
10059{_}1362{_}I found I was fifty-six miles from New York.
10059{_}1363{_}The farmer had heard of no accident; there was no railway nearer than six miles; the highway was little used.
10059{_}1364{_}I told the good people my story and they suspected me of being drunk or crazy, but did not credit a single word I said."
10059{_}1365{_}"That was but natural," said Uncle John.
10059{_}1366{_}"After breakfast I took stock of myself.
10059{_}1367{_}In my pockets I found a twenty-dollar bill and some silver.
10059{_}1368{_}I wore a watch and chain and a ring set with a good-sized diamond.
10059{_}1369{_}My clothing seemed good, but the ditch had soiled it.
10059{_}1370{_}I had no hat, nor could the farmer find one when I sent him back to look for it.
10059{_}1371{_}My mind was not wholly a blank; I seemed to have a fair knowledge of life, and when the farmer mentioned New York the city seemed familiar to me.
10059{_}1372{_}But in regard to myself, my past history--even my name--I was totally ignorant.
10059{_}1373{_}All personal consciousness dated from the moment I woke up in the ditch."
10059{_}1374{_}"How wonderful!"
10059{_}1375{_}exclaimed Louise.
10059{_}1376{_}"And you haven't solved the mystery yet, after two years?"
10059{_}1377{_}asked Patsy.
10059{_}1378{_}"No, Miss Doyle.
10059{_}1379{_}I hired the farmer to drive me to the railway station, where I took the train to New York.
10059{_}1380{_}I seemed to know the city, but no recollection guided me to home or friends.
10059{_}1381{_}I went to a small hotel, took a room, and began to read all the newspapers, seeking to discover if anyone was reported missing.
10059{_}1382{_}The sight of automobiles led me to conceive the theory that I had been riding in one of those machines along a country road when something threw me out.
10059{_}1383{_}My head might have struck a stump or stone and the blow rendered me insensible.
10059{_}1384{_}Something in the nature of the thing, or in my physical condition, deprived me of all knowledge of the past.
10059{_}1385{_}Since then I have read of several similar cases.
10059{_}1386{_}The curious thing about my own experience was that I could find no reference to my disappearance, in any way, nor could I learn of any automobile accident that might account for it.
10059{_}1387{_}I walked the streets day after day, hoping some acquaintance would accost me.
10059{_}1388{_}I waited patiently for some impulse to direct me to my former haunts.
10059{_}1389{_}I searched the newspapers persistently for a clue; but nothing rewarded me.
10059{_}1390{_}"After spending all my money and the proceeds of my watch and diamond, I began to seek employment; but no one would employ a man without recommendations or antecedents.
10059{_}1391{_}I did not know what work I was capable of doing.
10059{_}1392{_}So finally I left the city and for more than two years I have been wandering from one part of the country to another, hoping that some day I would recognize a familiar spot.
10059{_}1393{_}I have done odd jobs, at times, but my fortunes went from bad to worse until of late I have become no better than the typical tramp."
10059{_}1394{_}"How did you secure employment as a book-keeper for Skeelty?"
10059{_}1395{_}asked Uncle John.
10059{_}1396{_}"I heard a new mill had started at Royal and walked up there to inquire for work.
10059{_}1397{_}The manager asked if I could keep books, and I said yes."
10059{_}1398{_}"Have you ever kept books before?"
10059{_}1399{_}"Not that I know of; but I did it very well.
10059{_}1400{_}I seemed to comprehend the work at once, and needed no instruction.
10059{_}1401{_}Often during these two years I have encountered similar curious conditions.
10059{_}1402{_}I sold goods in a store and seemed to know the stocks; I worked two weeks in a telegraph office and discovered I knew the code perfectly; I've shod horses for a country blacksmith, wired a house for electric lights and compounded prescriptions in a drug store.
10059{_}1403{_}Whatever I have undertaken to do I seem able to accomplish, and so it is hard for me to guess what profession I followed before my memory deserted me."
10059{_}1404{_}"You did not retain any position for long, it seems," remarked Uncle John.
10059{_}1405{_}"No; I was always impatient to move on, always hoping to arrive at some place so familiar that my lost memory would return to me.
10059{_}1406{_}The work I have mentioned was nearly all secured during the first year.
10059{_}1407{_}After I became seedy and disreputable in appearance people were more apt to suspect me and work was harder to obtain."
10059{_}1408{_}"Why did you come to Millville?"
10059{_}1409{_}asked Louise.
10059{_}1410{_}"You brought me here," he answered, with a smile.
10059{_}1411{_}"I caught a ride on your private car, when it left New York, not caring much where it might take me.
10059{_}1412{_}When I woke up the next morning the car was sidetracked at Chazy Junction, and as this is a section I have never before explored I decided to stay here for a time.
10059{_}1413{_}That is all of my story, I believe."
10059{_}1414{_}"Quite remarkable!"
10059{_}1415{_}declared Mr. Merrick, emphatically.
10059{_}1416{_}The girls, too, had been intensely interested in the strange recital.
10059{_}1417{_}"You seem educated," said Patsy thoughtfully; "therefore you must have come from a good family."
10059{_}1418{_}"That does not seem conclusive," replied Thursday Smith, deprecatingly, "although I naturally hope my family was respectable.
10059{_}1419{_}I have been inclined to resent the fact that none of my friends or relatives has ever inquired what became of me."
10059{_}1420{_}"Are you sure they have not?"
10059{_}1421{_}"I have watched the papers carefully.
10059{_}1422{_}In two years I have followed several clues.
10059{_}1423{_}A bricklayer disappeared, but his drowned body was finally found; a college professor was missing, but he was sixty years of age; a young man in New York embezzled a large sum and hid himself.
10059{_}1424{_}I followed that trail, although regretfully, but the real embezzler was caught the day I presented myself in his place.
10059{_}1425{_}Perhaps the most curious experience was in the case of a young husband who deserted his wife and infant child.
10059{_}1426{_}She advertised for him; he had disappeared about the time I had found myself; so I went to see her."
10059{_}1427{_}"What was the result?"
10059{_}1428{_}asked Beth.
10059{_}1429{_}"She said I was not her husband, but if he failed to come back I might take his place, provided I would guarantee to support her."
10059{_}1430{_}During the laugh that followed, Thursday Smith went back to his work and an animated discussion concerning his strange story followed.
10059{_}1431{_}"He seems honest," said Louise, "but I blame a man of his ability for becoming a mere tramp.
10059{_}1432{_}He ought to have asserted himself and maintained the position in which he first found himself."
10059{_}1433{_}"How?"
10059{_}1434{_}inquired Patsy.
10059{_}1435{_}"At that time he was well dressed and had a watch and diamond ring.
10059{_}1436{_}If he had gone to some one and frankly told his story he could surely have obtained a position to correspond with his personality.
10059{_}1437{_}But instead of this he wasted his time and the little capital he possessed in doing nothing that was sensible."
10059{_}1438{_}"It is easy for us to criticise the man," remarked Beth, "and he may be sorry, now, that he did not act differently.
10059{_}1439{_}But I think, in his place, I should have made the same attempt he did to unravel the mystery of his lost identity.
10059{_}1440{_}So much depended upon that."
10059{_}1441{_}"It's all very odd and incomprehensible," said Uncle John.
10059{_}1442{_}"I wonder who he can be."
10059{_}1443{_}"I suppose he calls himself Thursday because that was the day he first found himself," observed Patsy.
10059{_}1444{_}"Yes; and Smith was the commonest name he could think of to go with it.
10059{_}1445{_}The most surprising thing," added their uncle, "is the fact that a man of his standing was not missed or sought for."
10059{_}1446{_}"Perhaps," suggested Louise, "he had been insane and escaped from some asylum."
10059{_}1447{_}"Then how did he come to be lying in a ditch?"
10059{_}1448{_}questioned Patsy; "and wouldn't an escaped maniac be promptly hunted down and captured?"
10059{_}1449{_}"I think so," agreed Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}1450{_}"For my part, I'm inclined to accept the man's theory that it was an automobile accident."
10059{_}1451{_}"Then what became of the car, or of the others in it?"
10059{_}1452{_}"It's no use," said Beth, shaking her head gravely.
10059{_}1453{_}"If Thursday Smith, who is an intelligent young man, couldn't solve the mystery himself, it isn't likely we can do so."
10059{_}1454{_}"We know as much as he does, as far as that is concerned," said Patsy, "and our combined intelligence ought at least to equal his.
10059{_}1455{_}I'm sorry for the poor man, and wish we might help him to come to his own again."
10059{_}1456{_}They all agreed to this sentiment and while the girls attended to their editorial duties they had the amazing story of Thursday Smith uppermost in their minds.
10059{_}1457{_}When the last copy had been placed in the hands of Miss Briggs and they were driving to the farm--at a little after six o'clock--they renewed the interesting discussion.
10059{_}1458{_}Just before reaching the farm Hetty Hewitt came out of the wood just in front of them.
10059{_}1459{_}She was clothed in her short skirt and leggings and bore a fishing rod and a creel.
10059{_}1460{_}"What luck?"
10059{_}1461{_}asked Patsy, stopping the horse.
10059{_}1462{_}"Seven trout," answered the artist.
10059{_}1463{_}"I might have caught more, but the poor little creatures squirmed and struggled so desperately that I hadn't the heart to destroy any more of them.
10059{_}1464{_}Won't you take them home for Mr. Merrick's breakfast?"
10059{_}1465{_}Patsy looked at the girl musingly.
10059{_}1466{_}"Jump in, Hetty," she said; "I'm going to take you with us for the night.
10059{_}1467{_}The day's fishing has tired you; there are deep circles under your eyes; and that stuffy old hotel isn't home-like.
10059{_}1468{_}Jump in."
10059{_}1469{_}Hetty flushed with pleasure, but hesitated to accept the invitation.
10059{_}1470{_}"I--I'm not dressed for--"  "You're all right," said Beth, supporting her cousin's proposition.
10059{_}1471{_}"We'll lend you anything you need."
10059{_}1472{_}"Do come, Miss Hewitt," added Louise.
10059{_}1473{_}Hetty sighed, then smiled and finally climbed into the surrey.
10059{_}1474{_}"In New York," she said, as they started on, "I've sometimes hobnobbed with editors; but this is somewhat different."
10059{_}1475{_}"In what way?"
10059{_}1476{_}asked Patsy casually.
10059{_}1477{_}"You're not real journalists, you know, and--"  "Why aren't we journalists?"
10059{_}1478{_}asked Louise.
10059{_}1479{_}For a moment Hetty was puzzled how to reply.
10059{_}1480{_}"You are doing very good editorial work," she said mendaciously, "but, after all, you are only playing at journalism.
10059{_}1481{_}The real journalist--as I know him--is a Bohemian; a font of cleverness running to waste; a reckless, tender-hearted, jolly, careless ne'er-do-well who works like a Trojan and plays like a child.
10059{_}1482{_}He is very sophisticated at his desk and very artless when he dives into the underworld for rest and recreation.
10059{_}1483{_}He lives at high tension, scintillates, burns his red fire without discrimination and is shortly extinguished.
10059{_}1484{_}You are not like that.
10059{_}1485{_}You can't even sympathize with that sort of person.
10059{_}1486{_}But I can, for I'm cut from a remnant of the same cloth."
10059{_}1487{_}"Scintillate all you want to, Hetty," cried Patsy with a laugh; "but you're not going to be extinguished.
10059{_}1488{_}For we, the imitation journalists, have taken you under our wings.
10059{_}1489{_}There's no underworld at Millville, and the only excitement we can furnish just now is a night with us at the old farm."
10059{_}1490{_}"That," replied Hetty, "is indeed a real excitement.
10059{_}1491{_}You can't quite understand it, perhaps; but it's so--so very different from what I'm accustomed to."
10059{_}1492{_}Uncle John welcomed the girl artist cordially and under his hospitable roof the waif soon felt at ease.
10059{_}1493{_}At dinner the conversation turned upon Thursday Smith and his peculiar experience.
10059{_}1494{_}Beth asked Hetty if she knew the man.
10059{_}1495{_}"Yes," replied the girl; "I've seen him at the office and we've exchanged a word or two.
10059{_}1496{_}But he boards with Thorne, the liveryman, and not at the hotel."
10059{_}1497{_}"You have never seen him before you met him here?"
10059{_}1498{_}"Never."
10059{_}1499{_}"I wonder," said Louise musingly, "if he is quite right in his mind.
10059{_}1500{_}All this story may be an hallucination, you know."
10059{_}1501{_}"He's a very clever fellow," asserted Hetty, "and such a loss of memory is by no means so uncommon as you think.
10059{_}1502{_}Our brains are queer things--mine is, I know--and it doesn't take much to throw their machinery out of gear.
10059{_}1503{_}Once I knew a reporter who was worried and over-worked.
10059{_}1504{_}He came to the office one morning and said he was George Washington, the Commander of the Continental Army.
10059{_}1505{_}In all other ways he was sane enough, and we humored him and called him 'General.'
10059{_}1506{_}At the end of three months the idea quit him as suddenly as it had come on, and he was not only normal but greatly restored in strength of intellect through the experience.
10059{_}1507{_}Perhaps some of the overworked brain cells had taken a rest and renewed their energy.
10059{_}1508{_}It would not surprise me if some day Thursday Smith suddenly remembered who he was."
10059{_}1509{_}[Footnote: This anecdote is true.--_Author._]  "In the meantime," said Uncle John, "I'm going to make an effort to discover his identity."
10059{_}1510{_}"In what way, Uncle?"
10059{_}1511{_}asked Patsy.
10059{_}1512{_}"I'll set Fogerty, who is a clever detective, at work.
10059{_}1513{_}No man can disappear from his customary haunts without leaving some sort of a record behind him, and Fogerty may be able to uncover the mystery in a short time."
10059{_}1514{_}"Then we'll lose our pressman," declared Beth; "for I'm positive that Thursday Smith was a person of some importance in his past life."
10059{_}1515{_}CHAPTER XI  THE HONER'BLE OJOY BOGLIN   One morning while Patsy was alone in her office, busied over her work, the door softly opened and a curious looking individual stood before her.
10059{_}1516{_}He was thin in form, leathery skinned and somewhat past the middle age of life.
10059{_}1517{_}His clothing consisted of a rusty black Prince Albert coat, rusty trousers to match, which were carefully creased, cowhide shoes brilliant with stove polish, a tall silk hat of antiquated design, and a frayed winged collar decorated with a black tie on which sparkled a large diamond attached to a chain.
10059{_}1518{_}He had chin whiskers of a sandy gray color and small gray eyes that were both shrewd and suspicious in expression.
10059{_}1519{_}He stood in the doorway a moment, attentively eyeing the girl, while she in turn examined him with an amusement she could not quite suppress.
10059{_}1520{_}Then he said, speaking in a low, diffident voice:  "I'm lookin' for the editor."
10059{_}1521{_}"I am the editor," asserted Patsy.
10059{_}1522{_}"Really?"
10059{_}1523{_}"It is quite true."
10059{_}1524{_}He seemed disconcerted a moment, striving to regain his assurance.
10059{_}1525{_}Then he took out a well-worn pocketbook and from its depths abstracted a soiled card which, leaning forward, he placed carefully upon the table before Patsy.
10059{_}1526{_}She glanced at it and read: "Hon.
10059{_}1527{_}Ojoy Boglin, Hooker's Falls, Chazy County."
10059{_}1528{_}"Oh," said she, rather surprised; "are you Mr.
10059{_}1529{_}Boglin?"
10059{_}1530{_}"I am the Honer'ble Ojoy Boglin, miss," he replied, dwelling lovingly upon the "Honer'ble."
10059{_}1531{_}"I have not had the honor of your acquaintance," said she, deciding she did not like her visitor.
10059{_}1532{_}"What is your business, please?"
10059{_}1533{_}The Hon.
10059{_}1534{_}Ojoy coughed.
10059{_}1535{_}Then he suddenly remembered he was in the presence of a lady and took off his hat.
10059{_}1536{_}Next he slid slowly into the vacant chair at the end of the table.
10059{_}1537{_}"First," he began, "I want to compliment you on your new paper.
10059{_}1538{_}It's a good thing, and I like it.
10059{_}1539{_}It's what's been needed in these 'ere parts a long time, and it's talked about all over Chazy County."
10059{_}1540{_}"Thank you," said the editor briefly, for the praise was given in a perfunctory way that irritated her.
10059{_}1541{_}"The only other papers in this senatorial deestric', which covers three counties," continued the visitor, in impressive tones, "air weeklies, run by political mud-slingers that's bought up by the Kleppish gang."
10059{_}1542{_}"What is the Kleppish gang?"
10059{_}1543{_}she asked, wonderingly.
10059{_}1544{_}"The supporters o' that rascal, Colonel Kleppish, who has been occupyin' my berth for goin' on eight years," he said with fierce indignation.
10059{_}1545{_}"I fear I do not understand," remarked Patsy, really bewildered.
10059{_}1546{_}"What was your berth, which Colonel Kleppish has--has usurped?"
10059{_}1547{_}"See that 'Honer'ble' on the card?"
10059{_}1548{_}"I do."
10059{_}1549{_}"That means I were senator--state senator--which makes any common man honer'ble, accordin' to law, which it's useless to dispute.
10059{_}1550{_}I were elected fer this deestric', which covers three counties," he said proudly, "an' I served my country in that capacity."
10059{_}1551{_}"Oh, I see.
10059{_}1552{_}But you're not state senator now?"
10059{_}1553{_}"No; Kleppish beat me for the nomination, after I'd served only one term."
10059{_}1554{_}"Why?"
10059{_}1555{_}"Eh?
10059{_}1556{_}Why did he git the nomination?
10059{_}1557{_}'Cause he bought up the newspapers--the country weeklies--and set them to yellin' 'graft.'
10059{_}1558{_}He made 'em say I went into office poor, and in two years made a fortune."
10059{_}1559{_}"Did you?"
10059{_}1560{_}asked the girl.
10059{_}1561{_}He shuffled in his seat.
10059{_}1562{_}"I ain't used to talkin' politics with a girl," he admitted; "but seein' as you're the editor of this paper--a daily, by Jupe!--you've probably got a head on you and understand that a man don't get into office for his health.
10059{_}1563{_}There's a lot of bother in servin' your country, and a man oughter be well paid for it.
10059{_}1564{_}I did jest like the others do--like Kleppish is doin' right now--but the reg'lar voters don't understand politics, and when the howl went up about graft, backed by Kleppish's bought-up newspapers, they turned me down cold.
10059{_}1565{_}I've been eight years watchin' for a chance to get in again, an' now I've got it."
10059{_}1566{_}"This is very interesting, I'm sure," remarked Patsy; "but our paper doesn't go much into local politics, Mr. Boglin, and I'm very busy to-day."
10059{_}1567{_}"Honer'ble Ojoy Boglin," he said, correcting her; but he did not take the hint to leave.
10059{_}1568{_}Patsy picked up her pencil as if to resume her work, while he eyed her with a countenance baffled and uncertain.
10059{_}1569{_}Presently he asked:  "Has Kleppish got this paper too?"
10059{_}1570{_}"No," she coldly replied.
10059{_}1571{_}"I thought I'd likely head him off, you being so new.
10059{_}1572{_}See here, Editor--"  "I am Miss Doyle, sir."
10059{_}1573{_}"Glad to know you, Miss Doyle.
10059{_}1574{_}What I was about to remark is this: The election for senator comes up agin in September and I want this paper to pull for me.
10059{_}1575{_}Bein' as it's a daily it's got more power than all of Kleppish's weeklies put together, and if you work the campaign proper I'll win the nomination hands down.
10059{_}1576{_}This is a strong Republican deestric', and to git nominated on the Republican ticket is the same as an election.
10059{_}1577{_}So what I want is the nomination.
10059{_}1578{_}What do you say?"
10059{_}1579{_}Patsy glared at him and decided that as far as appearances went he was not a fit candidate for any office, however humble.
10059{_}1580{_}But she answered diplomatically:  "I will inquire into the condition of politics in this district, Mr. Boglin, and try to determine which candidate is the most deserving.
10059{_}1581{_}Having reached a decision, the _Millville Tribune_ will espouse the cause of the best man--if it mentions local politics at all."
10059{_}1582{_}The Hon.
10059{_}1583{_}Ojoy gave a dissatisfied grunt.
10059{_}1584{_}"That means, in plain words," he suggested, "that you'll give Kleppish a chance to bid against me.
10059{_}1585{_}But I need this paper, and I'm willin' to pay a big price for it.
10059{_}1586{_}Let Kleppish go, and we'll make our dicker right now, on a lib'ral basis.
10059{_}1587{_}It's the only way you can make your paper pay.
10059{_}1588{_}I've got money, Miss Doyle.
10059{_}1589{_}I own six farms near Hooker's Falls, which is in this county, and six hundred acres of good pine forest, and I'm director in the Bank of Huntingdon, with plenty of money out on interest.
10059{_}1590{_}Also I own half the stock in the new paper mill at Royal--"  "You do?"
10059{_}1591{_}she exclaimed.
10059{_}1592{_}"I thought Mr. Skeelty--"  "Skeelty's the head man, of course," he said.
10059{_}1593{_}"He came to me about the mill proposition and I went in with him.
10059{_}1594{_}I own all the forest around Royal.
10059{_}1595{_}Bein' manager, and knowin' the business, Skeelty stood out for fifty-one shares of stock, which is the controllin' interest; but I own all the rest, and the mill's makin' good money.
10059{_}1596{_}People don't know I'm in that deal, and of course this is all confidential and not to be talked about."
10059{_}1597{_}"Very well, sir.
10059{_}1598{_}But I fear you have mistaken the character of our paper," said Patsy quietly.
10059{_}1599{_}"We are quite independent, Mr. Boglin, and intend to remain so--even if we can't make the paper pay.
10059{_}1600{_}In other words, the _Millville Daily Tribune_ can't be bought."
10059{_}1601{_}He stared in amazement; then scratched his ear with a puzzled air.
10059{_}1602{_}"Such talk as that means somethin'," he asserted, gropingly, "but what it means, blamed if I know!
10059{_}1603{_}Newspapers never turn money down unless they're a'ready bought, or have got a grouch of their own....
10059{_}1604{_}Say!"
10059{_}1605{_}he suddenly cried, as an inspiration struck him, "you ain't got anything agin the mill at Royal, or agin Skeelty, have you?"
10059{_}1606{_}"I have, sir!"
10059{_}1607{_}declared Patsy, raising her head to frown discouragingly upon the Honer'ble Ojoy.
10059{_}1608{_}"Mr. Skeelty is acting in a very disagreeable manner.
10059{_}1609{_}He has not only boycotted our paper and refused to pay for the subscriptions he engaged, but I understand he is encouraging his workmen to annoy the Millville people, and especially this printing office."
10059{_}1610{_}"Well--durn--Skeelty!"
10059{_}1611{_}ejaculated Mr. Boglin, greatly discomposed by this statement.
10059{_}1612{_}"But I'll fix all that, Miss Doyle," he added, eagerly.
10059{_}1613{_}"Skeelty's my partner and he's got to do what I say or I'll make trouble for him.
10059{_}1614{_}You dicker with me for the support of your paper and I'll guarantee a hundred subscriptions from Royal and get you an apology from Skeelty and a promise he'll behave an' keep his men to home.
10059{_}1615{_}And all that's outside the price I'll agree to pay."
10059{_}1616{_}Patsy's eyes were full of scorn.
10059{_}1617{_}"I won't dicker with you an instant," she firmly declared.
10059{_}1618{_}"I don't know Colonel Kleppish, or what his character is, but I'm very sure he's the better man and that the people have made no mistake in electing him in your place.
10059{_}1619{_}No respectable candidate for office would attempt to buy the support of a newspaper, and I advise you to change the wording on your card.
10059{_}1620{_}Instead of 'Honorable' it should read 'Dishonorable' Ojoy Boglin.
10059{_}1621{_}Good day, sir!"
10059{_}1622{_}Mr. Boglin's face turned white with rage.
10059{_}1623{_}He half rose from his seat, but sat down again with a vicious snarl.
