10059{_}1624{_}"I've coaxed, so far, young woman," he said grimly, "but I guess it's time I showed my hand.
10059{_}1625{_}You'll either run this paper in my interest or I'll push Skeelty on to make the town too hot to hold you.
10059{_}1626{_}I've got power in this county, even if I ain't senator, and you'll feel that power if you dare oppose me.
10059{_}1627{_}Take your choice, girl--either to make good money out o' this campaign, or be run out of town, neck an' crop!
10059{_}1628{_}It's up to you to decide."
10059{_}1629{_}"In thirty seconds," said Patsy, her face as white as was Boglin's, "I shall ring this bell to summon my men to throw you out."
10059{_}1630{_}The Honer'ble Ojoy slowly rose and put on his hat.
10059{_}1631{_}"Look out!"
10059{_}1632{_}he said warningly.
10059{_}1633{_}"I will," snapped Patsy.
10059{_}1634{_}"This ain't the end of it, girl!"
10059{_}1635{_}"There are ten seconds left," she said.
10059{_}1636{_}He picked up his card, turned his back and walked out, leaving his opponent trembling betwixt agitation and righteous indignation.
10059{_}1637{_}A few moments later Bob West came in and looked at the girl editor curiously.
10059{_}1638{_}"Ojoy Boglin has been here," he said.
10059{_}1639{_}"The Honer'ble Ojoy, if you please," answered Patsy, with a laugh that bordered on hysteria.
10059{_}1640{_}The hardware man nodded, his eyes reading her face.
10059{_}1641{_}"You were quite right to turn him down," he asserted.
10059{_}1642{_}"It was the only thing to do," responded the girl, wondering how he knew.
10059{_}1643{_}"But Boglin is a dangerous man," resumed West.
10059{_}1644{_}"Look out for him.
10059{_}1645{_}Miss Doyle."
10059{_}1646{_}"Yes; he told me to do that, and I will," said she, more quietly.
10059{_}1647{_}"He is Skeelty's partner."
10059{_}1648{_}"And you're not afraid of him?"
10059{_}1649{_}"Why should I be, Mr.
10059{_}1650{_}West?"
10059{_}1651{_}He smiled.
10059{_}1652{_}"I'm justice of the peace here.
10059{_}1653{_}If there's a hint of trouble from Boglin or Skeelty, come directly to me."
10059{_}1654{_}"Thank you, Mr. West.
10059{_}1655{_}I will."
10059{_}1656{_}With this he nodded cheerfully and went away.
10059{_}1657{_}CHAPTER XII  MOLLY SIZER'S PARTY   The people of Chazy County were very proud of the _Millville Tribune_, the only daily paper in that section of the state.
10059{_}1658{_}It was really a very good newspaper, if small in size, and related the news of the day as promptly as the great New York journals did.
10059{_}1659{_}Arthur Weldon had not been very enthusiastic about the paper at any time, although he humored the girls by attending in a good-natured way to the advertising, hiring some of the country folk to get subscriptions, and keeping the books.
10059{_}1660{_}He was a young man of considerable education who had inherited a large fortune, safely invested, and therefore had no need, through financial necessity, to interest himself in business of any sort.
10059{_}1661{_}He allowed the girls to print his name as editor in chief, but he did no editorial work at all, amusing himself these delightful summer days by wandering in the woods, where he collected botanical specimens, or sitting with Uncle John on the lawn, where they read together or played chess.
10059{_}1662{_}Both the men were glad the girls were happy in their work and enthusiastic over the success of their audacious venture.
10059{_}1663{_}Beth was developing decided talent as a writer of editorials and her articles were even more thoughtful and dignified than were those of Patsy.
10059{_}1664{_}The two girls found plenty to occupy them at the office, while Louise did the reportorial work and flitted through Millville and down to Huntingdon each day in search of small items of local interest.
10059{_}1665{_}She grew fond of this work, for it brought her close to the people and enabled her to study their characters and peculiarities.
10059{_}1666{_}Her manner of approaching the simple country folk was so gracious and winning that they freely gave her any information they possessed, and chatted with her unreservedly.
10059{_}1667{_}Sometimes Louise would make her rounds alone, but often Arthur would join her for an afternoon drive to Huntingdon, and it greatly amused him to listen to his girl-wife's adroit manner of "pumping the natives."
10059{_}1668{_}About halfway to Huntingdon was the Sizer Farm, the largest and most important in that vicinity.
10059{_}1669{_}Old Zeke Sizer had a large family--five boys and three girls--and they were noted as quite the most aggressive and disturbing element in the neighborhood.
10059{_}1670{_}Old Zeke was rude and coarse and swore like a trooper, so his sons could not be expected to excel him in refinement.
10059{_}1671{_}Bill Sizer, the eldest, was a hard drinker, and people who knew him asserted that he "never drew a sober breath."
10059{_}1672{_}The other sons were all quarrelsome in disposition and many a free fight was indulged in among them whenever disputes arose.
10059{_}1673{_}They were industrious farmers, though, and the three girls and their mother worked from morning till night, so the farm prospered and the Sizers were reputed to be "well-off."
10059{_}1674{_}Molly, the eldest girl, had attracted Louise, who declared she was pretty enough to arrest attention in any place.
10059{_}1675{_}Indeed, this girl was a "raving beauty" in her buxom, countrified way, and her good looks were the pride of the Sizer family and the admiration of the neighbors.
10059{_}1676{_}The other two were bouncing, merry girls, rather coarse in manner, as might be expected from their environment; but Molly, perhaps fully conscious of her prettiness, assumed certain airs and graces and a regal deportment that brought even her big, brutal brothers to her feet in adoration.
10059{_}1677{_}The Sizers were among the first subscribers to the _Millville Tribune_ and whenever Louise stopped at the farmhouse for news the family would crowd around her, ignoring all duties, and volunteer whatever information they possessed.
10059{_}1678{_}For when they read their own gossip in the local column it gave them a sort of proprietary interest in the paper, and Bill had once thrashed a young clerk at Huntingdon for questioning the truth of an item the Sizers had contributed.
10059{_}1679{_}One day when Louise and Arthur stopped at the farm, Mollie ran out with an eager face to say that Friday was her birthday and the Sizers were to give a grand party to celebrate it.
10059{_}1680{_}"We want you to come over an' write it up, Mrs. Weldon," said the girl.
10059{_}1681{_}"They're comin' from twenty mile around, fer the dance, an' we've got the orchestry from Malvern to play for us.
10059{_}1682{_}Pop's goin' to spend a lot of money on refreshments an' it'll be the biggest blow-out Chazy County ever seen!"
10059{_}1683{_}"I think I can write up the party without being present, Mollie," suggested Louise.
10059{_}1684{_}"No; you come over.
10059{_}1685{_}I read once, in a novel, how an editor come to a swell party an' writ about all the dresses an' things--said what everybody wore, you know.
10059{_}1686{_}I'm goin' to have a new dress, an' if ever'thing's described right well we'll buy a lot of papers to send to folks we know in Connecticut."
10059{_}1687{_}"Well," said Louise, with a sigh, "I'll try to drive over for a little while.
10059{_}1688{_}It is to be Saturday, you say?"
10059{_}1689{_}"Yes; the birthday's Friday and the dance Saturday night, rain or shine.
10059{_}1690{_}An' you might bring the chief editor, your husband, an' try a dance with us.
10059{_}1691{_}It wouldn't hurt our reputation any to have you folks mingle with us on this festive occasion," she added airily.
10059{_}1692{_}They had a good laugh over this invitation when it was reported at Mr. Merrick's dinner table, and Patsy insisted that Louise must write up the party.
10059{_}1693{_}"It will be fun to give it a 'double head' and a big send-off," she said.
10059{_}1694{_}"Write it up as if it were a real society event, dear, and exhaust your vocabulary on the gowns.
10059{_}1695{_}You'll have to invent some Frenchy names to describe those, I guess, for they'll be wonders; and we'll wind up with a list of 'those present.'"
10059{_}1696{_}So on Saturday evening Arthur drove his wife over to the Sizer farm, and long before they reached there they heard the scraping of fiddles, mingled with shouts and boisterous laughter.
10059{_}1697{_}It was a prohibition district, to be sure, but old Sizer had imported from somewhere outside the "dry zone" a quantity of liquors more remarkable for strength than quality, and with these the guests had been plied from the moment of their arrival.
10059{_}1698{_}Most of them were wholly unused to such libations, so by the time Arthur and Louise arrived, the big living room of the farmhouse presented an appearance of wild revelry that was quite deplorable.
10059{_}1699{_}Molly welcomed them with wild enthusiasm and big Bill, her adoring brother, demanded in a loud voice if Arthur did not consider her the "Belle of Chazy County."
10059{_}1700{_}"They ain't a stunner in the state as kin hold a candle to our Molly," he added, and then with uncertain gait he left the "reporters" with the promise to "bring 'em a drink."
10059{_}1701{_}"Come, Louise," said Arthur, quietly, "let's get out of here."
10059{_}1702{_}He drew her to the door and as a dance was just starting they managed to escape without notice.
10059{_}1703{_}"What a disgraceful scene!"
10059{_}1704{_}cried Louise, when they were on their way home; "and to think of such a shocking carousal being held in good old Chazy County, where morals are usually irreproachable!
10059{_}1705{_}I shall not mention the affair in the _Tribune_ at all."
10059{_}1706{_}But Patsy, who had a managing editor's respect for news of any sort, combatted this determination and begged Louise to write up Molly Sizer's party without referring to its deplorable features.
10059{_}1707{_}"It isn't policy to offend the Sizers," she said, "for although they are coarse and common they have shown a friendly spirit toward the paper.
10059{_}1708{_}Moreover, the enmity of such people--which would surely result from our ignoring the birthday party--would keep us in hot water."
10059{_}1709{_}So Louise, though reluctantly, wrote up the party and the manuscript was sent over to Miss Briggs Sunday afternoon, so it would get a place in Monday morning's _Tribune_.
10059{_}1710{_}Uncle John had the paper at breakfast on Monday, and he gave an amused laugh as his eye caught the report of the Sizer party.
10059{_}1711{_}"This is a good one on you, Louise," he exclaimed.
10059{_}1712{_}"You say that Miss Molly, 'looking more lovely than ever in her handsome new gown, greeted her guests with a roughish smile.'"
10059{_}1713{_}"A what?"
10059{_}1714{_}demanded Louise, horrified.
10059{_}1715{_}"A 'roughish' smile."
10059{_}1716{_}"Oh; that's a mistake," she said, glancing at the item.
10059{_}1717{_}"What I said was a 'roguish' smile; but there's been a typographical error which Miss Briggs must have overlooked in reading the proof."
10059{_}1718{_}"Nevertheless," remarked Arthur, "the statement isn't far wrong.
10059{_}1719{_}Everything was rough, including the smiles, as far as I noted that remarkable gathering."
10059{_}1720{_}"But--see here!"
10059{_}1721{_}cried Patsy; "that's a dreadful mistake.
10059{_}1722{_}That spoils all the nice things you said about the girl, Louise.
10059{_}1723{_}I hope the Sizers won't notice it."
10059{_}1724{_}But the Sizers did, and were frantic with rage over what they deemed was a deliberate insult to Molly.
10059{_}1725{_}Several young men who had come from distances to attend the birthday party had stayed over Sunday at the farmhouse, where the revelry still continued in a fitful way, due to vain attempts to relieve racking headaches by further libations.
10059{_}1726{_}Monday morning found the dissipated crew still the guests of the Sizers, and when big Bill slowly spelled out the assertion made by the _Tribune_ that his sister had "a roughish smile" loud cries of indignation arose.
10059{_}1727{_}Molly first cried and then had hysterics and screamed vigorously; Bill swore vengeance on the _Millville Tribune_ and all connected with it, while the guests gravely asserted it was "a low-down, measly trick" which the Sizers ought to resent.
10059{_}1728{_}They all began drinking again, to calm their feelings, and after the midday dinner Bill Sizer grabbed a huge cowhide whip and started to Millville to "lick the editor to a standstill."
10059{_}1729{_}A wagonload of his guests accompanied him, and Molly pleaded with her brother not to hurt Mrs. Weldon.
10059{_}1730{_}"I won't; but I'll cowhide that fresh husband of hers," declared Bill.
10059{_}1731{_}"He's the editor--the paper says so--and he's the one I'm after!"
10059{_}1732{_}CHAPTER XIII  BOB WEST INTERFERES   It was unfortunate that at that time Thursday Smith had gone up the electric line toward Royal, to inspect it.
10059{_}1733{_}In the office were Patsy, Hetty Hewitt--who was making a drawing--Arthur Weldon, engaged upon his books, and finally, seated in an easy-chair from which he silently watched them work, old Bob West, the hardware man.
10059{_}1734{_}Louise and Beth had driven over to the Junction to write up an accident, one of the trainmen having caught his hand in a coupling, between two freight cars.
10059{_}1735{_}Bob West often dropped into the office, which was next door to his own place of business, but he was a silent man and had little to say on these visits.
10059{_}1736{_}In his early days he had wandered pretty much over the whole world, and he could relate some interesting personal adventures if he chose.
10059{_}1737{_}In this retired village West was the one inhabitant distinguished above his fellows for his knowledge of the world.
10059{_}1738{_}In his rooms over the store, where few were ever invited, he had a fine library of unusual books and a rare collection of curios gathered from foreign lands.
10059{_}1739{_}It was natural that such a man would be interested in so unique an experiment as the _Millville Tribune_, and he watched its conduct with curiosity but a constantly growing respect for the three girl journalists.
10059{_}1740{_}No one ever minded when he came into the office, nodded and sat down.
10059{_}1741{_}Sometimes he would converse with much freedom; at other times the old gentleman remained an hour without offering a remark, and went away with a brief parting nod.
10059{_}1742{_}It was West who first saw, through the window, the wagonload of men from the Sizer farm come dashing up the street at a gallop.
10059{_}1743{_}Instinctively, perhaps, he knew trouble was brewing, but he never altered his expression or his attitude, even when the wagon stopped at the printing office and the passengers leaped out.
10059{_}1744{_}In marched Bill Sizer at the head of his following, cowhide in hand.
10059{_}1745{_}Patsy, her face flushing scarlet, stood up and faced the intruders.
10059{_}1746{_}"Stand back, girl!"
10059{_}1747{_}cried Sizer in a fierce tone; "it's that coward editor I'm after," pointing his whip with trembling hand at Arthur.
10059{_}1748{_}"My sister Molly may be rough, an' hev a rough smile, but I'll be dinged ef I don't skin the man thet prints it in a paper!"
10059{_}1749{_}"Good fer you, Bill!"
10059{_}1750{_}murmured his friends, approvingly.
10059{_}1751{_}Arthur leaned back and regarded his accuser in wonder.
10059{_}1752{_}The big table, littered with papers, was between them.
10059{_}1753{_}"Come out o' there, ye measly city chap, an' take yer medicine," roared Bill, swinging his whip.
10059{_}1754{_}"I'll larn ye to come inter a decent neighborhood an' slander its women.
10059{_}1755{_}Come outer there!"
10059{_}1756{_}West had sat quietly observing the scene.
10059{_}1757{_}Now he inquired, in composed tones:  "What's the trouble, Bill?"
10059{_}1758{_}"Trouble?
10059{_}1759{_}Trouble, West?
10059{_}1760{_}Why, this lyin' scroundrel said in his paper thet our Molly had a rough smile.
10059{_}1761{_}That's the trouble!"
10059{_}1762{_}"Did he really say that?"
10059{_}1763{_}asked West.
10059{_}1764{_}"'Course he did.
10059{_}1765{_}Printed it in the paper, for all to read.
10059{_}1766{_}That's why I've come to cowhide the critter within an inch o' his life!"
10059{_}1767{_}"Good fer you, Bill!"
10059{_}1768{_}cried his friends, encouragingly.
10059{_}1769{_}"But--wait a moment!"
10059{_}1770{_}commanded West, as the maddened, half drunken young farmer was about to leap over the table to grasp his victim; "you're not going at this thing right, Bill Sizer."
10059{_}1771{_}"Why ain't I, Bob West?"
10059{_}1772{_}"Because," answered West, in calm, even tones, "this insult is too great to be avenged by a mere cowhiding.
10059{_}1773{_}Nothing but blood will wipe away the dreadful stain on your sister's character."
10059{_}1774{_}"Oh, Mr.
10059{_}1775{_}West!"
10059{_}1776{_}cried Patsy, horrified by such a statement.
10059{_}1777{_}"Eh?
10059{_}1778{_}Blood?"
10059{_}1779{_}said Bill, stupefied by the suggestion.
10059{_}1780{_}"Of course," returned West.
10059{_}1781{_}"You mustn't thrash Mr. Weldon; you must kill him."
10059{_}1782{_}A delighted chorus of approval came from Sizer's supporters.
10059{_}1783{_}"All right, then," said the bully, glaring around, "I--I'll kill the scandler!"
10059{_}1784{_}"Hold on!"
10059{_}1785{_}counselled West, seizing his arm.
10059{_}1786{_}"This affair must be conducted properly--otherwise the law might cause us trouble.
10059{_}1787{_}No murder, mind you.
10059{_}1788{_}You must kill Weldon in a duel."
10059{_}1789{_}"A--a what?
10059{_}1790{_}A duel!"
10059{_}1791{_}gasped Sizer.
10059{_}1792{_}"To be sure.
10059{_}1793{_}That's the way to be revenged.
10059{_}1794{_}Hetty," he added, turning to the artist, who alone of the observers had smiled instead of groaned at the old gentleman's startling suggestion, "will you kindly run up to my rooms and get a red leather case that lies under the shell cabinet?
10059{_}1795{_}Thank you, my dear."
10059{_}1796{_}Hetty was off like a flash.
10059{_}1797{_}During her absence an intense silence pervaded the office, broken only by an occasional hiccough from one of Mr. Sizer's guests.
10059{_}1798{_}Patsy was paralyzed with horror and had fallen back into her chair to glare alternately at Bob West and the big bully who threatened her cousin's husband.
10059{_}1799{_}Arthur was pale and stern as he fixed a reproachful gaze on the hardware merchant.
10059{_}1800{_}From Miss Briggs' little room could be heard the steady click-click of the telegraph instrument.
10059{_}1801{_}But the furious arrival of the Sizer party had aroused every inhabitant of Millville and with one accord they dropped work and rushed to the printing office.
10059{_}1802{_}By this time the windows were dark with groups of eager faces that peered wonderingly through the screens--the sashes being up--and listened to the conversation within.
10059{_}1803{_}While Hetty was gone not a word was spoken, but the artist was absent only a brief time.
10059{_}1804{_}Presently she reentered and laid the red leather case on the table before Bob West.
10059{_}1805{_}The hardware man at once opened it, displaying a pair of old-fashioned dueling pistols, with long barrels and pearl handles.
10059{_}1806{_}There was a small can of powder, some bullets and wadding in the case, and as West took up one of the pistols and proceeded to load it he said in an unconcerned voice:  "I once got these from an officer in Vienna, and they have been used in more than a score of duels, I was told.
10059{_}1807{_}One of the pistols--I can't tell which it is--has killed a dozen men, so you are going to fight with famous weapons."
10059{_}1808{_}Both Arthur and Bill Sizer, as well as the groups at the window, watched the loading of the pistols with fascinated gaze.
10059{_}1809{_}"Bob's a queer ol' feller," whispered Peggy McNutt to the blacksmith, who stood beside him.
10059{_}1810{_}"This dool is just one o' his odd fancies.
10059{_}1811{_}Much he keers ef they kills each other er not!"
10059{_}1812{_}"Mr. West," cried Patsy, suddenly rousing from her apathy, "I'll not allow this shameful thing!
10059{_}1813{_}A duel is no better than murder, and I'm sure there is a law against it."
10059{_}1814{_}"True," returned West, ramming the bullet into the second pistol; "it is quite irregular and--er--illegal, I believe.
10059{_}1815{_}Perhaps I shall go to jail with whichever of the duelists survives; but you see it is a point of honor with us all.
10059{_}1816{_}Molly Sizer has seemingly been grossly maligned in your paper, and the editor is responsible.
10059{_}1817{_}Are you a good shot, Bill?"
10059{_}1818{_}"I--I guess so," stammered Sizer.
10059{_}1819{_}"That's good.
10059{_}1820{_}Weldon, I hear, is an expert with the pistol."
10059{_}1821{_}Arthur did not contradict this statement, although he was positive he could not hit a barn at twenty yards.
10059{_}1822{_}"Now, then, are we ready?"
10059{_}1823{_}staid West, rising.
10059{_}1824{_}"Come with me, gentlemen."
10059{_}1825{_}"What ye goin' to do, Bob?"
10059{_}1826{_}asked Sizer, anxiously.
10059{_}1827{_}"I'll explain," replied the hardware man, leading the way to the street.
10059{_}1828{_}Everyone followed him and the crowd at the windows joined the group outside.
10059{_}1829{_}"Of course you mustn't shoot in the main street, for you might hit some one, or break windows; but back of this row of buildings is a lane that is perfectly clear.
10059{_}1830{_}You will stand back to back in the center of the block and then, at my word, you will each march to the end of the block and pass around the buildings to the lane.
10059{_}1831{_}As soon as you come in sight of one another you are privileged to fire, and I suppose Bill Sizer will try to kill you, Mr. Weldon, on the spot, and therefore you will try to kill him first."
10059{_}1832{_}"But--look a-here, Bob!"
10059{_}1833{_}cried Sizer; "it ain't right fer him to take a shot at me.
10059{_}1834{_}You said fer me to kill him, but ye didn't say nuth'n about _his_ shootin' at _me_."
10059{_}1835{_}"That's all right, Bill," returned West.
10059{_}1836{_}"You're in the right, and the right ought to win.
10059{_}1837{_}But you must give the man a chance for his life, you know."
10059{_}1838{_}"That weren't in the bargain."
10059{_}1839{_}"It is now, by the laws of dueling."
10059{_}1840{_}"He--he might shoot me," urged Bill.
10059{_}1841{_}"It isn't likely.
10059{_}1842{_}Although he's a dead shot, you have right on your side, and you must be sure to fire as soon as you get within good range.
10059{_}1843{_}It won't be considered murder; it will only be a duel, and the law will deal lightly with you."
10059{_}1844{_}"That's right, Bill," asserted one of Sizer's friends.
10059{_}1845{_}"Bob West's a justice o' the peace himself, an' he orter know."
10059{_}1846{_}"I do know," declared West gravely.
10059{_}1847{_}He placed Arthur Weldon and Bill Sizer back to back in the middle of the street and handed each a pistol.
10059{_}1848{_}"Now, then," said he, "you both understand the rules, which I have explained, and the spectators will bear witness that, whatever happens, this affair has been conducted in a regular manner, with no favor shown to either.
10059{_}1849{_}You are both brave men, and this duel will vindicate your honor.
10059{_}1850{_}If you are fortunate enough to survive, you will be heroes, and all your differences will be wiped off the slate.
10059{_}1851{_}But as one or both may fall, we, the citizens of Millville, hereby bid you a solemn and sad farewell."
10059{_}1852{_}Impressed by this speech, Sizer's friends began to shake hands with him.
10059{_}1853{_}"All ready!"
10059{_}1854{_}called West.
10059{_}1855{_}"One--two--three----go!"
10059{_}1856{_}At the word the two, back to back, started for the opposite ends of the little street, and at once the crowd made a rush between the buildings to gain the rear, where they might witness the shooting in the lane when the duelists met.
10059{_}1857{_}Arthur had been thinking seriously during these proceedings and had made up his mind it was in no degree his duty to be bored full of holes by a drunken countryman like Bill Sizer, just because there had been a typographical error in the _Millville Tribune_.
10059{_}1858{_}So, when he got to the end of the street, instead of turning into the lane he made for the farm, holding the long dueling pistol gingerly in his hand and trotting at a good pace for home.
10059{_}1859{_}Footsteps followed him.
10059{_}1860{_}In sudden panic he increased his run; but the other was faster.
10059{_}1861{_}A heavy hand grasped his shoulder and swung him around, while old Bob West, panting for Breath, exclaimed:  "Stop, you fool--stop!
10059{_}1862{_}The other one is running."
10059{_}1863{_}"The other one!"
10059{_}1864{_}echoed Arthur, wonderingly.
10059{_}1865{_}"Of course.
10059{_}1866{_}Bill Sizer was sure to run; he's a coward, as all bullies are.
10059{_}1867{_}Quick, Weldon, save the day and your reputation or I'll never stand your friend again."
10059{_}1868{_}Arthur understood now.
10059{_}1869{_}He turned and ran back faster than he had come, swung into the lane where the crowd was cautiously peering from the shelter of the buildings, and waving his pistol in a reckless way that made Bob West shudder, he cried out:  "Where is he?
10059{_}1870{_}Where's Sizer?
10059{_}1871{_}Why don't he show up and be shot, like a man?"
10059{_}1872{_}No Sizer appeared.
10059{_}1873{_}He was even then headed cross-lots for home, leaving his friends to bemoan his cowardice.
10059{_}1874{_}As for Arthur, the crowd gave him a cheer and condemned his opponent's conduct in no measured terms.
10059{_}1875{_}They were terribly disappointed by Big Bill's defection, for while not especially bloodthirsty they hated to see the impending tragedy turn out a farce.
10059{_}1876{_}In the printing office Patsy was laughing hysterically as her horror dissolved and allowed her to discover the comic phase of the duel.
10059{_}1877{_}She literally fell on Arthur's neck as he entered, but the next moment pushed him away to face the hardware merchant.
10059{_}1878{_}"I beg your pardon, Mr. West," said she with twinkling eyes.
10059{_}1879{_}"I suspected you of being a cold-blooded ruffian, when you proposed this duel; but I now see that you understand human nature better than the whole caboodle of us put together!
10059{_}1880{_}Arthur, thank Mr. West for saving you from a flogging."
10059{_}1881{_}"I do, indeed!"
10059{_}1882{_}said Arthur fervently.
10059{_}1883{_}CHAPTER XIV  THE DANGER SIGNAL   By this time the _Tribune_ had become the pride of all Millville, yet the villagers could not quite overcome their awe and wonder at it.
10059{_}1884{_}Also the newspaper was the pride of the three girl journalists, who under the tutelage of Miss Briggs were learning to understand the complicated system of a daily journal.
10059{_}1885{_}Their amateurish efforts were gradually giving way to more dignified and readable articles; Beth could write an editorial that interested even Uncle John, her severest critic; Louise showed exceptional talent for picking up local happenings and making news notes of them, while Patsy grabbed everything that came to her net--locals, editorials, telegraphic and telephone reports from all parts of the world--and skillfully sorted, edited and arranged them for the various departments of the paper.
10059{_}1886{_}It was mighty interesting to them all, and they were so eager each morning to get to work that they could scarcely devote the proper time to old Nora's famous breakfasts.
10059{_}1887{_}"We made a mistake.
10059{_}1888{_}Uncle," said Patsy to Mr. Merrick, "in starting the _Tribune_ in the wrong place.
10059{_}1889{_}In a few weeks we must leave it and go back to the city, whereas, had we established our paper in New York--"  "Then it never would have been heard of," interrupted practical Beth.
10059{_}1890{_}"In New York, Patsy dear, we would become the laughing stock of the town.
10059{_}1891{_}I shudder when I think what a countrified paper we turned out that first issue."
10059{_}1892{_}"But we are fast becoming educated," declared Patsy.
10059{_}1893{_}"I'm not ashamed of the _Tribune_ now, even in comparison with the best New York dailies."
10059{_}1894{_}Beth laughed, but Uncle John said judicially:  "For Millville, it's certainly a marvel.
10059{_}1895{_}I get the world news more concisely and more pleasantly from its four pages than when I wade through twenty or thirty of the big pages of a metropolitan newspaper.
10059{_}1896{_}You are doing famously, my dears.
10059{_}1897{_}I congratulate you."
10059{_}1898{_}"But we are running behind dreadfully," suggested Arthur, the bookkeeper, "even since Thursday Smith enabled us to cut down expenses so greatly.
10059{_}1899{_}The money that comes in never equals what we pay out.
10059{_}1900{_}How long can you keep this up, girls?"
10059{_}1901{_}They made no reply, nor did Uncle John discuss the financial condition of the newspaper.
10059{_}1902{_}He was himself paying some heavy expenses that did not appear on the books, such as the Associated Press franchise, the telegraph bills and the electric power; but he was quite delighted to take care of these items and regretted he had not assumed more of the paper's obligations.
10059{_}1903{_}He knew the expenses were eating big holes in the incomes of his three nieces, yet they never complained nor allowed their enthusiasm to flag.
10059{_}1904{_}Mr. Merrick, who had tested these girls in more ways than one, was watching them carefully, and fully approved their spirit and courage under such trying conditions.
10059{_}1905{_}Major Doyle, Patsy's father, when the first copy of the _Millville Tribune_ was laid on his desk in the city, was astounded at the audacity of this rash venture.
10059{_}1906{_}When he could command his temper to write calmly he sent a letter to Mr. Merrick which read: "Taken altogether, John, you're the craziest bunch of irresponsibles outside an asylum.
10059{_}1907{_}No wonder you kept this folly a secret from me until you had accomplished your nefarious designs.
10059{_}1908{_}The _Millville Daily Tribune_ is a corker and no mistake, for our Patsy's at the head of your lunatic gang.
10059{_}1909{_}I'll go farther, and say the paper's a wonder.
10059{_}1910{_}I believe it is the first daily newspaper published in a town of six inhabitants, that has ever carried the Associated Press dispatches, But, allow me to ask, why?
10059{_}1911{_}The lonely inhabitants of the desert of Chazy County don't need a daily--or a weekly--or a monthly.
10059{_}1912{_}A semi-annual would about hit their gait, and be more than they deserve.
10059{_}1913{_}So I've decided it's merely a silly way to spend money--and an easy way, too, I'll be bound.
10059{_}1914{_}Oblige me by explaining this incomprehensible eccentricity."
10059{_}1915{_}To this, a mild protest for the major, Uncle John replied: "Dear Major Doyle: Yours received.
10059{_}1916{_}Have you no business of your own to attend to?
10059{_}1917{_}Affectionately yours, John Merrick."
10059{_}1918{_}The major took the hint.
10059{_}1919{_}He made no further complaint but read the paper religiously every day, gloating over Patsy's name as managing editor and preserving the files with great care.
10059{_}1920{_}He really enjoyed, the _Millville Tribune_, and as his summer vacation was shortly due he anticipated with pleasure a visit to the farm and a peep at the workings of "our Patsy's" famous newspaper.
10059{_}1921{_}The other girls he ignored.
10059{_}1922{_}If Patsy was connected with the thing, her adoring parent was quite sure she was responsible for all the good there was in it.
10059{_}1923{_}The paper printed no mention of the famous duel.
10059{_}1924{_}But Hetty made a cartoon of it, showing the lane, with its fringe of spectators, Arthur Weldon standing manfully to await his antagonist and big Bill Sizer, in the distance, sprinting across the fields in the direction of home.
10059{_}1925{_}This cartoon was highly prized by those who had witnessed the adventure and Peggy McNutt pinned it on the wall of his real estate office beside the one Hetty had made of himself.
10059{_}1926{_}Bill Sizer promptly "stopped the paper," that being the only vengeance at hand, and when Bob West sent a boy to him demanding the return of the pistol, Bill dispatched with the weapon the following characteristic note, which he had penned with much labor:  "Bob west sir you Beet me out uv my Reeveng and Made me look like a bag uv Beens.
10059{_}1927{_}but I will skware this Thing sum da and yu and that edyter hed better Watch out.
10059{_}1928{_}i don't stand fer no Throwdown like that Wm.
10059{_}1929{_}Sizer."
10059{_}1930{_}However, the bully received scant sympathy, even from his most intimate friends, and his prestige in the community was henceforth destroyed.
10059{_}1931{_}Arthur did not crow, for his part.
10059{_}1932{_}He told the girls frankly of his attempt to run away and evade the meeting, which sensible intention was only frustrated by Bob West's interference, and they all agreed he was thoroughly justified.
10059{_}1933{_}The young man had proved to them his courage years before and none of the girls was disposed to accuse him of cowardice for not wishing to shoot or be shot by such a person as Bill Sizer.
10059{_}1934{_}A few days following the duel another incident occurred which was of a nature so startling that it drove the Sizer comedy from all minds.
10059{_}1935{_}This time Thursday Smith was the hero.
10059{_}1936{_}Hetty Hewitt, it seems, was having a desperate struggle to quell the longings of her heart for the allurements of the great city.
10059{_}1937{_}She had been for years a thorough Bohemienne, frequenting cafes, theatres and dance halls, smoking and drinking with men and women of her class and, by degrees, losing every womanly quality with which nature had generously endowed her.
10059{_}1938{_}But the girl was not really bad.
10059{_}1939{_}She was essentially nervous and craved excitement, so she had drifted into this sort of life because no counteracting influence of good had been injected into her pliable disposition.
10059{_}1940{_}None, that is, until the friendly editor for whom she worked, anticipating her final downfall, had sought to save her by sending her to a country newspaper.
10059{_}1941{_}He talked to the girl artist very frankly before she left for Millville, and Hetty knew he was right, and was truly grateful for the opportunity to redeem herself.
10059{_}1942{_}The sweet girl journalists with whom she was thrown in contact were so different from any young women she had heretofore known, and proved so kindly sympathetic, that Hetty speedily became ashamed of her wasted life and formed a brave resolution to merit the friendship so generously extended her.
10059{_}1943{_}But it was hard work at first.
10059{_}1944{_}She could get through the days easily enough by wandering in the woods and taking long walks along the rugged country roads; but in the evenings came the insistent call of the cafes, the cheap orchestras, vaudeville, midnight suppers and the like.
10059{_}1945{_}She strenuously fought this yearning and found it was growing less and less powerful to influence her.
10059{_}1946{_}But her nights were yet restless and her nerves throbbing from the effects of past dissipations.
10059{_}1947{_}Often she would find herself unable to sleep and would go out into the moonlight when all others were in bed, and "prowl around with the cats," as she expressed it, until the wee hours of morning.
10059{_}1948{_}Often she told Patsy she wished there was more work she could do.
10059{_}1949{_}The drawings required by the paper never occupied her more than a couple of hours each day.
10059{_}1950{_}Sometimes she made one of her cleverest cartoons in fifteen or twenty minutes.
10059{_}1951{_}"Can't I do something else?"
10059{_}1952{_}she begged.
10059{_}1953{_}"Let me set type, or run the ticker--I can receive telegrams fairly well--or even write a column of local comment.
10059{_}1954{_}I'm no journalist, so you'll not be envious."
10059{_}1955{_}But Patsy shook her head.
10059{_}1956{_}"Really, Hetty, there's nothing else you can do, and your pictures are very important to us.
10059{_}1957{_}Rest and enjoy yourself, and get strong and well.
10059{_}1958{_}You are improving wonderfully in health since you came here."
10059{_}1959{_}Often at midnight Hetty would wander into the pressroom and watch Thursday Smith run off the edition on the wonderful press, which seemed to possess an intelligence of its own, so perfectly did it perform its functions.
10059{_}1960{_}At such times she sat listlessly by and said little, for Thursday was no voluble talker, especially when busied over his press.
10059{_}1961{_}But a certain spirit of comradeship grew up between these two, and it was not unusual for the pressmen, after his work was finished and the papers were neatly piled for distribution to the carriers at daybreak, to walk with Hetty to the hotel before proceeding to his own lodgings in the little wing of Nick Thorne's house, which stood quite at the end of the street.
10059{_}1962{_}To be sure, the hotel adjoined the printing office, with only a vacant lot between, but Hetty seemed to appreciate this courtesy and would exchange a brief good night with Smith before going to her own room.
10059{_}1963{_}Afterward she not infrequently stole out again, because sleep would not come to her, and then the moon watched her wanderings until it dipped behind the hills.
10059{_}1964{_}On the night we speak of, Hetty had parted from Thursday Smith at one o'clock and crept into the hallway of the silent, barnlike hotel; but as soon as the man turned away she issued forth again and walked up the empty street like a shadow.
10059{_}1965{_}Almost to Thompson's Crossing she strolled, deep in thought, and then turned and retraced her steps.
10059{_}1966{_}But when she again reached the hotel she was wide-eyed as ever; so she passed the building, thinking she would go on to Little Bill Creek and sit by the old mill for a time.
10059{_}1967{_}The girl was just opposite the printing office when her attention was attracted by a queer grating noise, as if one of the windows was being pried up.
10059{_}1968{_}She stopped short, a moment, and then crept closer to the building.
10059{_}1969{_}Two men were at a side window of the pressroom, which they had just succeeded in opening.
10059{_}1970{_}As Hetty gained her point of observation one of the men slipped inside, but a moment later hastily reappeared and joined his fellow.
10059{_}1971{_}At once both turned and stole along the side of the shed directly toward the place where the girl stood.
10059{_}1972{_}Her first impulse was to run, but recollecting that she wore a dark gown and stood in deep shadow she merely flattened herself against the building and remained motionless.
10059{_}1973{_}The men were chuckling as they passed her, and she recognized them as mill hands from Royal.
10059{_}1974{_}"Guess that'll do the job," said one, in a low tone.
10059{_}1975{_}"If it don't, nothin' will," was the reply.
10059{_}1976{_}They were gone, then, stealing across the road and beating a hasty retreat under the shadows of the houses.
10059{_}1977{_}Hetty stood motionless a moment, wondering what to do.
10059{_}1978{_}Then with sudden resolve she ran to Thorne's house and rapped sharply at the window of the wing where she knew Thursday Smith slept.
10059{_}1979{_}She heard him leap from bed and open the blind.
10059{_}1980{_}"What is it?"
10059{_}1981{_}he asked.
10059{_}1982{_}"It's me, Thursday--Hetty," she said.
10059{_}1983{_}"Two men have just broken into the pressroom, through a window.
10059{_}1984{_}They were men from Royal, and they didn't steal anything, but ran away in great haste.
10059{_}1985{_}I--I'm afraid something is wrong, Thursday!"
10059{_}1986{_}Even while she spoke he was rapidly dressing.
10059{_}1987{_}"Wait!"
10059{_}1988{_}he called to her.
10059{_}1989{_}In a few moments he opened the door and joined her.
10059{_}1990{_}Without hesitation he began walking rapidly toward the office, and the girl kept step with him.
10059{_}1991{_}He asked no questions whatever, but us soon as she had led him to the open window he leaped through it and switched on an electric light.
10059{_}1992{_}An instant later he cried aloud, in a voice of fear:  "Get out, Hetty!
10059{_}1993{_}Run--for your life!"
10059{_}1994{_}"Run yourself, Thursday, if there's danger," she coolly returned.
10059{_}1995{_}But he shouted "Run--run--run!"
10059{_}1996{_}in such thrilling, compelling tones that the girl shrank away and dashed across the vacant lot to the hotel before she turned again in time to see Smith leap from the window and make a dash toward the rear.
10059{_}1997{_}He was carrying something--something extended at arms' length before him--and he crossed the lane and ran far into the field before stooping to set down his burden.
10059{_}1998{_}Now he was racing back again, running as madly as if a troop of demons was after him.
10059{_}1999{_}A flash cleft the darkness; a deep detonation thundered and echoed against the hills; the building against which Hetty leaned shook as if an earthquake had seized it, and Thursday Smith was thrown flat on his face and rolled almost to the terrified girl's feet, where he lay motionless.
10059{_}2000{_}Only the building saved her from pitching headlong too, but as the reverberations died away, to be followed by frantic screams from the rudely wakened population of Millville, Hetty sank upon her knees and turned the man over, so that he lay face up.
10059{_}2001{_}He opened his eyes and put up one hand.
10059{_}2002{_}Then he struggled to his feet, trembling weakly, and his white face smiled into the girl's anxious one.
10059{_}2003{_}"That was a close call, dear," he whispered; "but your timely discovery saved us from a terrible calamity.
10059{_}2004{_}I--I don't believe there is much harm done, as it is."
10059{_}2005{_}Hetty made no reply.
10059{_}2006{_}She was thinking of the moments he had held that deadly Thing in his hands, while he strove to save lives and property from destruction.
10059{_}2007{_}The inevitable crowd was gathering now, demanding in terrified tones what had happened.
10059{_}2008{_}Men, women and children poured from the houses in scant attire, all unnerved and fearful, crying for an explanation of the explosion.
10059{_}2009{_}"Keep mum, Hetty," said Smith, warningly.
10059{_}2010{_}"It will do no good to tell them the truth."
10059{_}2011{_}She nodded, realizing it was best the villagers did not suspect that an enemy of the newspaper had placed them all in dire peril.
10059{_}2012{_}"Dynamite?"
10059{_}2013{_}she asked in a whisper.
10059{_}2014{_}"Yes; a bomb.
10059{_}2015{_}But for heaven's sake don't mention it."
10059{_}2016{_}Suddenly a man with a lantern discovered a great pit in the field behind the lane and the crowd quickly surrounded it.
10059{_}2017{_}From their limited knowledge of the facts the explosion seemed unaccountable, but there was sufficient intelligence among them to determine that dynamite had caused it and dug this gaping hole in the stony soil.
10059{_}2018{_}Bob West glanced at the printing office, which was directly in line with the explosion; then he cast a shrewd look into the white face of Thursday Smith; but the old hardware merchant merely muttered under his breath something about Ojoy Boglin and shook his head determinedly when questioned by his fellow villagers.
10059{_}2019{_}Interest presently centered in the damage that had been done.
10059{_}2020{_}Many window panes were shattered and the kitchen chimney of the hotel had toppled over; but no person had been injured and the damage could easily be repaired.
10059{_}2021{_}While the excitement was at its height Thursday Smith returned to his room and went to bed; but long after the villagers had calmed down sufficiently to seek their homes Hetty Hewitt sat alone by the great pit, staring reflectively into its ragged depths.
10059{_}2022{_}Quaint and curious were the thoughts that puzzled the solitary girl's weary brain, but prominent and ever-recurring was the sentence that had trembled upon Thursday Smith's lips: "It was a close call, _dear_!"
10059{_}2023{_}The "close call" didn't worry Hetty a particle; it was the last word of the sentence that amazed her.
10059{_}2024{_}That, and a new and wonderful respect for the manliness of Thursday Smith, filled her heart to overflowing.
10059{_}2025{_}CHAPTER XV  A CLEVER IDEA   Neither Thursday nor Hetty allowed a word to escape concerning the placing of the bomb in the _Tribune_ office, but the explosion was public knowledge and many were bothering their heads to explain its meaning.
10059{_}2026{_}John Merrick, when he heard the news, looked very grave and glanced uneasily into the unconscious faces of his three beloved nieces.
10059{_}2027{_}A man of much worldly experience, in spite of his simple, ingenuous nature, the little man began carefully piecing together parts of the puzzle.
10059{_}2028{_}Thursday Smith's defense of the girl journalists, whereby he had severely pounded some of the workmen who had insulted them, had caused the man to be denounced by the colony at Royal.
10059{_}2029{_}Mr. Skeelty, the manager, had demanded that Smith be discharged by Mr. Mirrick, and being refused, had threatened to shut off the power from the newspaper plant.
10059{_}2030{_}Skeelty dared not carry out this threat, for fear of a lawsuit, but his men, who had urged the matter of Smith's discharge upon their manager, were of the class that seeks revenge at any cost.
10059{_}2031{_}At this juncture Ojoy Boglin, Skeelty's partner and the owner of all the pine forest around Royal, had become the enemy of the newspaper and was aware of the feeling among the workmen.
10059{_}2032{_}A word from Boglin, backed by Skeelty's tacit consent, would induce the men to go to any length in injuring the _Millville Tribune_ and all concerned in its welfare.
10059{_}2033{_}Considering these facts, Mr. Merrick shrewdly suspected that the dynamite explosion had been the work of the mill hands, yet why it was harmlessly exploded in a field was a factor that puzzled him exceedingly.
10059{_}2034{_}He concluded, from what information he possessed, that they had merely intended this as a warning, which if disregarded might be followed by a more serious catastrophe.
10059{_}2035{_}The idea that such a danger threatened his nieces made the old gentleman distinctly nervous.
10059{_}2036{_}There were ways to evade further molestation from the lawless element at the mill.
10059{_}2037{_}The Hon.
10059{_}2038{_}Ojoy could be conciliated; Thursday Smith discharged; or the girls could abandon their journalistic enterprise altogether.
10059{_}2039{_}Such alternatives were mortifying to consider, but his girls must be protected from harm at any cost.
10059{_}2040{_}While he was still considering the problem, the girls and Arthur having driven to the office, as usual, Joe Wegg rode over from Thompson's Crossing on his sorrel mare for a chat with his old friend and benefactor.
10059{_}2041{_}It was this same young man--still a boy in years--who had once owned the Wegg Farm and disposed of it to Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}2042{_}Joe was something of a mechanical genius and, when his father died, longed to make his way in the great world.
10059{_}2043{_}But after many vicissitudes and failures he returned to Chazy County to marry Ethel Thompson, his boyhood sweetheart, and to find that one of his father's apparently foolish investments had made him rich.
10059{_}2044{_}Ethel was the great-granddaughter of the pioneer settler of Chazy County--Little Bill Thompson--from whom the Little Bill Creek and Little Bill Mountain had been named.
10059{_}2045{_}It was he who first established the mill at Millville; so, in marrying a descendant of Little Bill Thompson, Joe Wegg had become quite the most important resident of Chazy County, and the young man was popular and well liked by all who knew him.
10059{_}2046{_}After the first interchange of greetings Joe questioned Mr. Merrick about the explosion of the night before, and Uncle John frankly stated his suspicions.
10059{_}2047{_}"I'm sorry," said Joe, "they ever started that mill at Royal Falls.
10059{_}2048{_}Most of the workmen are foreigners, and all of them rude and reckless.
10059{_}2049{_}They have caused our quiet, law-abiding people no end of trouble and anxiety already.
10059{_}2050{_}It is becoming a habit with them to haunt Millville on Saturday nights, when they are partly intoxicated, and they've even invaded some of the farmhouses and frightened the women and children.
10059{_}2051{_}I've talked to Bob West about it and he has promised to swear in Lon Taft and Seth Davis as special constables, to preserve order; but he admits we are quite helpless to oppose such a gang of rowdies.
10059{_}2052{_}I've also been to see Mr. Skeelty, to ask him to keep his men at home, but he answered gruffly that he had no authority over his employees except during working hours, and not much authority even then."
10059{_}2053{_}"Skeelty doesn't seem the right man to handle those fellows," observed Mr. Merrick thoughtfully; "but as he owns the controlling interest in his company, and Boglin is fully as unreasonable, we cannot possibly oust him from control.
10059{_}2054{_}If the men determined to blow up all Millville with dynamite I'm sure Skeelty would not lift a finger to prevent it."
10059{_}2055{_}"No; he's deathly afraid of them, and that's a fact," said Joe.
10059{_}2056{_}They sat in silence a while.
10059{_}2057{_}"Your report of Skeelty's threat to cut off your electric power," said young Wegg, "reminds me of a plan I've had in mind for some time.
10059{_}2058{_}I find I've too much time on my hands, Mr. Merrick, and I cannot be thoroughly happy unless I'm occupied.
10059{_}2059{_}Ethel's farms are let on shares and I'm a drone in the world's busy hive.
10059{_}2060{_}But we're anchored here at Millville, so I've been wondering what I could do to improve the place and keep myself busy.
10059{_}2061{_}It has seemed to me that the same rush of water in Little Bill Creek that runs the dynamos at Royal is in evidence--to a lesser extent--at the old milldam.
10059{_}2062{_}What would you think of my putting in an electric plant at the mill, and lighting both Millville and Huntingdon, as well as all the farmhouses?"
10059{_}2063{_}"Not a bad idea, Joe," said Uncle John approvingly.
10059{_}2064{_}"Electric lights have a civilizing influence," continued the young man.
10059{_}2065{_}"I'm quite sure all the farmers between here and Huntingdon would use them, at a reasonable price.
10059{_}2066{_}I can also run a line to Hooker's Falls, and one to Chazy Junction.
10059{_}2067{_}Plenty of poles can be cut from our pine forests and the wires will be the chief expense.
10059{_}2068{_}I may not make money, at first, but I'll play pretty nearly even and have something to do."
10059{_}2069{_}"Do you think you could furnish enough power for our printing office?"
10059{_}2070{_}asked Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}2071{_}"Yes; and a dozen factories, besides.
10059{_}2072{_}I've an idea the thing may bring factories to Millville."
10059{_}2073{_}"Then get at it, Joe, and build it quick.
10059{_}2074{_}I've a notion we shall have an open rupture with Skeelty before long."
10059{_}2075{_}Joe Wegg smiled.
10059{_}2076{_}"You're going to accuse me, sir, of asking advice after I've made up my mind," said he; "but the fact is, I have bought the mill of Silas Caldwell already.
10059{_}2077{_}He's been wanting to dispose of the property for some time."
10059{_}2078{_}"Good!"
10059{_}2079{_}exclaimed Uncle John.
10059{_}2080{_}"Also I--I've ordered a dynamo and machinery.
10059{_}2081{_}It all ought to be here in a few days."
10059{_}2082{_}"Better yet!"
10059{_}2083{_}cried Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}2084{_}"You've relieved my mind of a great weight, Joe."
10059{_}2085{_}"Now about Thursday Smith," said the young man.
10059{_}2086{_}"Don't you think it would be policy for you to let him go, Mr.
10059{_}2087{_}Merrick?"
10059{_}2088{_}"No."
10059{_}2089{_}"He's a clever fellow.
10059{_}2090{_}I can use him at my lighting plant."
10059{_}2091{_}"Thank you, Joe; but that wouldn't help any.
10059{_}2092{_}As long as he's in Millville he will be an object of vengeance to those anarchistic mill hands.
10059{_}2093{_}The only way to satisfy them in to drive Smith out of town, and--I'll be hanged if I'll do it!
10059{_}2094{_}He hasn't done anything wrong, and I'm interested in the fellow's curious history.
10059{_}2095{_}I've put his case in the hands of a famous New York detective--Fogerty--with instructions to discover who he is, and I can't let a lot of rowdies force me to abandon the man for no reasonable cause."
10059{_}2096{_}"Don't blame you, sir," said Joe.
10059{_}2097{_}"If it wasn't this Thursday Smith, some other would incur the hatred of the Royal workmen, and as they're disposed to terrorize us we may as well fight it out on this line as any other.
10059{_}2098{_}The whole county will stand by you, sir."
10059{_}2099{_}"The only thing I dread is possible danger to my girls."
10059{_}2100{_}"Keep 'em away from the office evenings," advised Joe.
10059{_}2101{_}"During the day they are perfectly safe.
10059{_}2102{_}If anything happens, it will be at night, and while the newspaper office may some time go flying skyward the girls will run no personal danger whatever."
10059{_}2103{_}"Maybe so, Joe.
10059{_}2104{_}How queer it is that such a condition should exist in Millville--a little forgotten spot in the very heart of civilization and the last place where one might expect excitement of this sort.
10059{_}2105{_}But I won't be cowed; I won't be driven or bullied by a pack of foreign hounds, I assure you!
10059{_}2106{_}If Skeelty can't discipline his men, I will."
10059{_}2107{_}In furtherance of which assertion, Mr. Merrick went to town and wired a message to the great Fogerty.
10059{_}2108{_}CHAPTER XVI  LOCAL CONTRIBUTION   We hear considerable of the "conventional people" of this world, but seldom meet with them; for, as soon as we begin to know a person, we discover peculiarities that quite remove him from the ranks of the conventional--if such ranks exist at all.
10059{_}2109{_}The remark of the old Scotch divine to his good wife: "Everybody's queer but thee and me, Nancy, and sometimes I think _thee_ a little queer," sums up human nature admirably.
10059{_}2110{_}We seldom recognize our own queerness, but are prone to mark the erratic temperaments of others, and this is rather more comfortable than to be annoyed by a consciousness of our personal deficits.
10059{_}2111{_}The inhabitants of a country town are so limited in their experiences that we generally find their personal characteristics very amusing.
10059{_}2112{_}No amount of scholastic learning could have rendered the Millville people sophisticated, for contact with the world and humanity is the only true educator; but, as a matter of fact, there was little scholastic learning among them, with one or two exceptions, and the villagers as a rule were of limited intelligence.
10059{_}2113{_}Every one was really a "character," and Uncle John's nieces, who all possessed a keen sense of humor, enjoyed the oddities of the Millvillites immensely.
10059{_}2114{_}A humorous situation occurred through a seemingly innocent editorial of Beth on authorship.
10059{_}2115{_}In the course of her remarks she said: "A prominent author is stated to have accumulated a large fortune by writing short stories for the newspapers and magazines.
10059{_}2116{_}He is said to receive ten cents a word, and this unusual price is warranted by the eager demand for his stories, of which the reading public is very fond.
10059{_}2117{_}However, the unknown author does not fare so badly.
10059{_}2118{_}The sum of from thirty to fifty dollars usually remitted for a short story pays the beginner a better recompense, for the actual time he is engaged upon the work, than any other occupation he might undertake."
10059{_}2119{_}This was seriously considered the morning it appeared in the _Tribune_ by Peggy McNutt and Skim Clark, as they sat in the sunshine on the former's little front porch.
10059{_}2120{_}Peggy had read it aloud in his laborious, halting way, and Skim listened with growing amazement.
10059{_}2121{_}"Thirty dollars!"
10059{_}2122{_}he cried; "thirty to fifty fer a short story!
10059{_}2123{_}Great Snakes, Peggy, I'm goin' into it."
10059{_}2124{_}"Heh?
10059{_}2125{_}Goin' into what?"
10059{_}2126{_}asked Peggy, raising his eyes from the paper.
10059{_}2127{_}"I kin write a story," declared Skim confidently.
10059{_}2128{_}"Ye kin, Skim?"
10059{_}2129{_}"It's a cinch, Peggy.
10059{_}2130{_}Mother keeps all the magazines an' paper novils, an' we allus reads 'em afore we sells 'em.
10059{_}2131{_}I've read the gol-durndest lot o' truck ye ever heard of, so I'm posted on stories in gen'ral.
10059{_}2132{_}I'll write one an' sell it to the _Millville Tribune_.
10059{_}2133{_}Do ye s'pose they'll give me the thirty, er the fifty, Peggy?"
10059{_}2134{_}"Anywheres between, they says.
10059{_}2135{_}But one feller gits ten cents a word.
10059{_}2136{_}Whew!"
10059{_}2137{_}"I know; but he's a big one, which I ain't--just now.
10059{_}2138{_}I'll take even the thirty, if I hev to."
10059{_}2139{_}"I would, Skim," advised Peggy, nodding approval.
10059{_}2140{_}"But make 'em put yer photygraf in the paper, besides.
10059{_}2141{_}Say, it'll be a big thing fer Millville to turn out a author.
10059{_}2142{_}I didn't think it were in you, Skim."
10059{_}2143{_}"Why, it hadn't struck me afore," replied the youth, modestly.
10059{_}2144{_}"I've ben hankerin' to make money, without knowin' how to do it.
10059{_}2145{_}I tell ye, Peggy, it pays to read the newspapers.
10059{_}2146{_}This one's give me a hint how to carve out a future career, an' I'll write a story as'll make them girl edyturs set up an' take notice."
10059{_}2147{_}"Make it someth'n' 'bout Injuns," suggested Peggy.
10059{_}2148{_}"I ain't read a Injun story fer years."
10059{_}2149{_}"No; they're out o' fashion," observed Skim loftily.
10059{_}2150{_}"What folks want now is a detective story.
10059{_}2151{_}Feller sees a hole in a fence an' says, 'Ha!
10059{_}2152{_}there's ben a murder!'
10059{_}2153{_}Somebody asks what makes him think so, an' the detective feller says, takin' out a magnifie-in' glass, 'Thet hole's a bullet-hole, an' the traces o' blood aroun' the edges shows the bullet went through a human body afore it went through the fence.'
10059{_}2154{_}'Then,' says some one, 'where's the body?'
10059{_}2155{_}'That,' says the detective, 'is what we mus' diskiver.'
10059{_}2156{_}So the story goes on to show how the body were diskivered an' who did the murderin'."
10059{_}2157{_}"By Jupe, thet's great!"
10059{_}2158{_}cried Peggy admiringly.
10059{_}2159{_}"Skim, ye're a wonder!"
10059{_}2160{_}"Ma allus said I were good fer somethin', but she couldn't tell what."
10059{_}2161{_}"It's story-writin'," declared Peggy "Say, Skim, I put ye onter this deal; don't I git a rake-off on thet fifty dollars?"
10059{_}2162{_}"Not a cent!"
10059{_}2163{_}said Skim indignantly.
10059{_}2164{_}"Ye didn't tell me to write a story; I said myself as I could do it.
10059{_}2165{_}An' I know where to use the money, Peggy, ev'ry dollar of it, whether it's thirty er fifty."
10059{_}2166{_}Peggy sighed.
10059{_}2167{_}"I writ a pome once," he said.
10059{_}2168{_}"Wonder ef they'd pay fer a pome?"
10059{_}2169{_}"What were it like?"
10059{_}2170{_}asked Skim curiously.
10059{_}2171{_}"It went someth'n' this way," said Peggy:          "I sigh         Ter fly         Up high         In the sky.
10059{_}2172{_}But my         Wings is shy,         So I mus' cry         Good-bye         Ter fly-             in'."
10059{_}2173{_}"Shoo!"
10059{_}2174{_}said Skim disdainfully.
10059{_}2175{_}"Thet ain't no real pome, Peggy."
10059{_}2176{_}"It makes rhymes, don't it?
10059{_}2177{_}All but the las' line."
10059{_}2178{_}"Mebbe it does," replied Skim, with assumption of superior wisdom; "but it don't mean nuth'n'."
10059{_}2179{_}"It would ef I got paid fer it," observed Peggy.
10059{_}2180{_}Skim went home to his mother's tiny "Emporium," took some note paper out of stock, opened a new bottle of ink and sat down at the sitting room table to write his story.
10059{_}2181{_}The Widow Clark looked in and asked what he meant by "squanderin' profits that way."
10059{_}2182{_}"Shet up, mar.
10059{_}2183{_}Gi' me elbow room," said her dutiful son.
10059{_}2184{_}"I'm writin' a fifty dollar story fer the _Tribune_."
10059{_}2185{_}"Fifty dollars!"
10059{_}2186{_}"Thirty, anyhow; mebbe fifty," replied Skim.
10059{_}2187{_}"What's a good name fer a detective, mar?"
10059{_}2188{_}The widow sat down and wiped her damp hands on her apron, looking upon her hopeful with an expression of mingled awe and pride.
10059{_}2189{_}"Kin ye do it, Skim?"
10059{_}2190{_}she asked softly.
10059{_}2191{_}"I s'pose I kin turn out one a day, by hard work," he said confidently.
10059{_}2192{_}"At thirty a day, the lowes' price, thet's a hunderd 'n' eighty a week, seven hunderd 'n' twenty a month, or over eight thousan' dollars a year.
10059{_}2193{_}I got it all figgered out.
10059{_}2194{_}It's lucky fer me the nabobs is rich, or they couldn't stan' the strain.
10059{_}2195{_}Now, mar, ef ye want to see yer son a nabob hisself, some day, jes' think up a good name fer a detective."
10059{_}2196{_}"Sherholmes Locke," she said after some reflection.
10059{_}2197{_}"No; this 'ere story's got ter be original.
10059{_}2198{_}I thought o' callin' him Suspectin' Algernon.
10059{_}2199{_}Detectives is allus suspectin' something."
10059{_}2200{_}"Algernon's high-toned," mused the widow.
10059{_}2201{_}"Let it go at that, Skim."
10059{_}2202{_}All that day and far into the evening he sat at his task, pausing now and then for inspiration, but most of the time diligently pushing his pen over the strongly lined note paper and hopelessly straying from the lines.
10059{_}2203{_}Meantime, Mrs. Clark walked around on tiptoe, so as not to disturb him, and was reluctant even to call him to his meals in the kitchen.
10059{_}2204{_}When Skim went to bed his story had got into an aggravating muddle, but during the next forenoon he managed to bring it to a triumphant ending.
10059{_}2205{_}"When I git used to the thing, mar," he said, "I kin do one a day, easy.
10059{_}2206{_}I had to be pertickler over this one, it bein' the first."
10059{_}2207{_}The widow read the story carefully, guessing at the words that were hopelessly indistinct.
10059{_}2208{_}"My!
10059{_}2209{_}but it's a thriller, Skim," she said with maternal enthusiasm; "but ye don't say why he killed the girl."
10059{_}2210{_}"That don't matter, so long's he did it."
10059{_}2211{_}"The spellin' don't allus seem quite right," she added doubtfully.
10059{_}2212{_}"I guess the spellin's as good as the readin'll be," he retorted, with evident irritation.
10059{_}2213{_}"I bet I spell as well as any o' the folks thet takes the paper."
10059{_}2214{_}"And some words I can't make out."
10059{_}2215{_}"Oh, the edytur'll fix that.
10059{_}2216{_}Say, air ye tryin' to queer my story, mar?
10059{_}2217{_}Do ye set up to know more'n I do about story writin'?"
10059{_}2218{_}"No," she said; "I ain't talented, Skim, an' you be."
10059{_}2219{_}"What I orter hev," he continued, reflectively, "is a typewriter.
10059{_}2220{_}When I git two er three hunderd ahead perhaps I'll buy one--secondhand."
10059{_}2221{_}"Kin ye buy one thet'll spell, Skim?"
10059{_}2222{_}she asked, as she made a neat roll of the manuscript and tied a pink hair ribbon around it.
10059{_}2223{_}Skim put on a collar and necktie and took his story across to the newspaper office.
10059{_}2224{_}"I got a conter-bution fer the paper," he said to Patsy, who asked him his business.
10059{_}2225{_}"What, something original, Skim?"
10059{_}2226{_}she asked in surprise.
10059{_}2227{_}"Ye've hit it right, Miss Doyle; it's a story."
10059{_}2228{_}"Oh!"
10059{_}2229{_}"A detective story."
10059{_}2230{_}"Dear me!
10059{_}2231{_}Then you'll have to see Mrs. Weldon, who is our literary editor."
10059{_}2232{_}Louise, who was sitting close by, looked up and held out her hand for the beribboned roll.
10059{_}2233{_}"I don't jes' know," remarked Skim, as he handed it across the table, "whether it's a thirty dollar deal, er a fifty."
10059{_}2234{_}Having forgotten Beth's editorial, Louise did not understand this remark, but she calmly unrolled Skim's manuscript and glanced at the scrawled heading with an amused smile.
10059{_}2235{_}"'Suspecting Algernon,'" she read aloud.
10059{_}2236{_}"'It were a dark and teedjus night in the erly springtime while the snow were falling soft over the moon litt lanskape.'
10059{_}2237{_}Why, Skim, how came you to write this?"
10059{_}2238{_}"It were the money," he said boldly.
10059{_}2239{_}"I kin do one a day like this, at thirty dollers apiece, an' never feel the wear an' tear."
10059{_}2240{_}Patsy giggled, but Louise stared with a wondering, puzzled expression at the crabbed writing, the misspelled words and dreadful grammar.
10059{_}2241{_}Indeed, she was a little embarrassed how to handle so delicate a situation.
10059{_}2242{_}"I'm afraid we cannot use your story, Mr. Clark," she said gently, and remembering the formula that usually accompanied her own rejected manuscripts she added: "This does not necessarily imply a lack of merit in your contribution, but is due to the fact that it is at present unavailable for our use."
10059{_}2243{_}Skim stared at her in utter dismay.
10059{_}2244{_}"Ye mean ye won't take it?"
10059{_}2245{_}he asked with trembling lips.
10059{_}2246{_}"We have so much material on hand, just now, that we cannot possibly purchase more," she said firmly, but feeling intensely sorry for the boy.
10059{_}2247{_}"It may be a good story--"  "It's the bes' story I ever heard of!"
10059{_}2248{_}declared Skim.
10059{_}2249{_}"But we have no place for it in the _Millville Tribune,_" she added, handing him back the roll.
10059{_}2250{_}Skim was terribly disappointed.
10059{_}2251{_}Never, for a single moment, had he expected "sech a throwdown as this."
10059{_}2252{_}"Seems to me like a bunco game," he muttered savagely.
10059{_}2253{_}"First ye say in yer blamed ol' paper a story's wuth thirty to fifty dollars, an' then when I bring ye a story ye won't pay a red cent fer it!"
10059{_}2254{_}"Stories," suggested Louise, "are of various qualities, depending on the experience and talent of the author.
10059{_}2255{_}An excellent story is often refused because the periodical to which it is offered is overstocked with similar material.
10059{_}2256{_}Such conditions are often trying, Skim; I've had a good many manuscripts rejected myself."
10059{_}2257{_}But the boy would not be conciliated.
10059{_}2258{_}"I'll send it to Munsey's, thet's what I'll do; an' then you'll be durn sorry," he said, almost ready to cry.
10059{_}2259{_}"Do," urged Louise sweetly.
10059{_}2260{_}"And if they print it, Mr. Clark, I'll agree to purchase your next story for fifty dollars."
10059{_}2261{_}"All right; the fifty's mine.
10059{_}2262{_}I got witnesses, mind ye!"
10059{_}2263{_}and he flounced out of the room like an angry schoolboy.
10059{_}2264{_}"Oh, Louise," exclaimed Patsy, reproachfully, "why didn't you let me see the thing?
10059{_}2265{_}It would have been better than a circus."
10059{_}2266{_}"Poor boy!"
10059{_}2267{_}said the literary editor, with a sigh.
10059{_}2268{_}"I didn't want to humiliate him more than I could help.
10059{_}2269{_}I wonder if he really will have the audacity to send it to Munsey's?"
10059{_}2270{_}And now the door opened to admit Peggy McNutt, who had been watching his chance to stump across to the printing office as soon as Skim left there.
10059{_}2271{_}For Peggy had reasoned, not unjustly, that if Skim Clark could make a fortune as an author he, Marshall McMahon McNutt, had a show to corral a few dollars in literature himself.
10059{_}2272{_}After lying awake half the night thinking it over, he arose this morning with the firm intention of competing with Skim for the village laurels.
10059{_}2273{_}He well knew he could not write a shuddery detective story, such as Skim had outlined, but that early poem of his, which the boy had seemed to regard so disdainfully, was considered by Peggy a rather clever production.
10059{_}2274{_}He repeated it over and over to himself, dwelling joyously on its perfect rhyme, until he was convinced it was a good poem and that Skim had enviously slandered it.
10059{_}2275{_}So he wrote it out in big letters on a sheet of foolscap and determined to offer it to "them newspaper gals."
10059{_}2276{_}"I got a pome, Miss Patsy," he said, with unusual diffidence, for he was by no means sure the "gals" would not agree with Skim's criticism.
10059{_}2277{_}"What!
10059{_}2278{_}Another contributor?"
10059{_}2279{_}she exclaimed playfully.
10059{_}2280{_}"Has the whole town suddenly turned literary, Peggy?"
10059{_}2281{_}"No; jest me 'n' Skim.
10059{_}2282{_}Skim says my pome's no good; but I sort o' like it, myself."
10059{_}2283{_}"Let me see it," said Patsy, ignoring this time the literary editor, who was glad to be relieved of the responsibility of disappointing another budding author.
10059{_}2284{_}Peggy handed over the foolscap, and Patsy eagerly read the "pome."
10059{_}2285{_}"Listen, Louise!
10059{_}2286{_}Listen, Beth!"
10059{_}2287{_}she called, delightedly.
10059{_}2288{_}"Here is certainly a real 'pome,' and on aviation--the latest fad:                 "'SKY HIGH         BY MARSHALL MCMAHON MCNUTT                of Millville   dealer in Real Estate Spring Chickens &c.                 1.
10059{_}2289{_}I sigh         Too fly         Up high         In the sky.
10059{_}2290{_}2.
10059{_}2291{_}But my         Wings air shy         And so I cry         A sad goodby         Too fly-             Ing.'"
10059{_}2292{_}A chorus of hilarious laughter followed the reading, and then Patsy wiped her eyes and exclaimed:  "Peggy, you are not only a poet but a humorist.
10059{_}2293{_}This is one of the best short poems I ever read."
10059{_}2294{_}"It's short 'cause I run out o' rhymes," admitted Peggy.
10059{_}2295{_}"But it's a gem, what there is of it."
10059{_}2296{_}"Don't, dear," remonstrated Louise; "don't poke fun at the poor man."
10059{_}2297{_}"Poke fun?
10059{_}2298{_}Why, I'm going to print that poem in the _Tribune_, as sure as my name's Patricia Doyle!
10059{_}2299{_}It's too good for oblivion."
10059{_}2300{_}"I dunno," remarked Peggy, uncertainly, "whether it's wuth fifty dollars, er about--"  "About forty-nine less," said Patsy.
10059{_}2301{_}"A poem of that length brings about fifty cents in open market, but I'll be liberal.
10059{_}2302{_}You shall have a whole dollar--and there it is, solid cash."
10059{_}2303{_}"Thank ye," returned Peggy, pocketing the silver.
10059{_}2304{_}"It ain't what I expected, but--"  "But what, sir?"
10059{_}2305{_}"But it's like findin' it, for I didn't expect nuth'n'.
10059{_}2306{_}I wish I could do more of 'em at the same price; but I did thet pome when I were young an' hed more ambition.
10059{_}2307{_}I couldn't think of another like it to save my neck."
10059{_}2308{_}"I am glad of that, Peggy.
10059{_}2309{_}One of this kind is all a paper dare print.
10059{_}2310{_}We mustn't get too popular, you know."
10059{_}2311{_}"I s'pose you'll print my name as the one what did it?"
10059{_}2312{_}he inquired anxiously.
10059{_}2313{_}"I shall print it just as it's written, advertisement and all."
10059{_}2314{_}She did, and Peggy bought two extra copies, at a cent apiece.
10059{_}2315{_}He framed all three and hung one in his office, one in the sitting room and a third in his bedroom, where he could see it the first thing when he wakened each morning.
10059{_}2316{_}His fellow villagers were very proud of him, in spite of the "knocking" of the Clarks.
10059{_}2317{_}Skim was deeply mortified that Peggy's "bum pome" had been accepted and his own masterly composition "turned down cold."
10059{_}2318{_}The widow backed her son and told all the neighbors that "Peggy never hed the brains to write thet pome, an' the chances air he stole it from the 'Malvern Weekly Journal.'
10059{_}2319{_}Them gal edyturs wouldn't know," she added scornfully; "they's as ignerunt as Peggy is, mostly."
10059{_}2320{_}A few days later McNutt entered the printing office with an air of great importance.
10059{_}2321{_}"Goodness me!
10059{_}2322{_}I hope you haven't done it again, Peggy," cried Patsy, in alarm.
10059{_}2323{_}"No; I got fame enough.
10059{_}2324{_}What I want is to hev the wordin' on my business cards changed," said he.
10059{_}2325{_}"What'll it cost?"
10059{_}2326{_}"What change do you wish made?"
10059{_}2327{_}asked Patsy, examining the sample card.
10059{_}2328{_}"Instead of 'Marshall McMahon McNutt, dealer in Real Estate an' Spring Chickens,' I want to make it read: 'dealer in Real Estate, Spring Chickens an' Poetry.'
10059{_}2329{_}What'll it cost.
10059{_}2330{_}Miss Patsy?"
10059{_}2331{_}"Nothing," she said, her eyes dancing; "We'll do that job free of charge, Peggy!"
10059{_}2332{_}CHAPTER XVII  THE PENALTIES OF JOURNALISM   Two strange men appeared in Millville--keen, intelligent looking fellows--and applied to Joe Wegg for jobs.
10059{_}2333{_}Having received a hint from Mr. Merrick, Joe promptly employed the strangers to prepare the old mill for the reception of the machinery for the lighting plant, and both of them engaged board at the hold.
10059{_}2334{_}"Thursday," said Hetty, as she watched the pressman that night, "there's a New York detective here--two of them, I think."
10059{_}2335{_}"How do you know?"
10059{_}2336{_}"I recognized one of them, who used to prowl around the city looking for suspicious characters.
10059{_}2337{_}They say they've come to work on the new electric plant, but I don't believe it."
10059{_}2338{_}Thursday worked a while in silence.
10059{_}2339{_}"Mr. Merrick must have sent for them," he suggested.
10059{_}2340{_}"Yes.
10059{_}2341{_}I think he suspects about the bomb."
10059{_}2342{_}"He ought to discharge me," said Thursday.
10059{_}2343{_}"No; he's man enough to stand by his guns.
10059{_}2344{_}I like Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}2345{_}He didn't become a millionaire without having cleverness to back him and I imagine he is clever enough to thwart Skeelty and all his gang."
10059{_}2346{_}"Perhaps I ought to go of my own accord," said Thursday.
10059{_}2347{_}"Don't do that.
10059{_}2348{_}When you've found a friend like Mr. Merrick, stick to him.
10059{_}2349{_}I imagine those detectives are here to protect you, as well as the printing plant.
10059{_}2350{_}It won't be so easy to set a bomb the next time."
10059{_}2351{_}Smith looked at her with a smile.
10059{_}2352{_}There was a glint of admiration in his eyes.
10059{_}2353{_}"You're not a bad sleuth yourself, Hetty," he remarked.
10059{_}2354{_}"No detective could have acted more wisely and promptly than you did that night."
10059{_}2355{_}"It was an accidental discovery, Thursday.
10059{_}2356{_}Sometimes I sleep."
10059{_}2357{_}That was a good deal of conversation for these two to indulge in.
10059{_}2358{_}Hetty was talkative enough, at times, and so was Thursday Smith, when the humor seized him; but when they were together they said very little.
10059{_}2359{_}The artist would stroll into the pressroom after the compositors had finished their tasks and watch the man make up the forms, lock them, place them on the press and run off the edition.
10059{_}2360{_}Then he would glance over the paper while Thursday washed up and put on his coat, after which he accompanied her to the door of her hotel and with a simple "good night" proceeded up the street to his own lodging.
10059{_}2361{_}There are surprises in the newspaper business, as our girl journalists were fast discovering.
10059{_}2362{_}It was a real calamity when Miss Briggs, who had been primarily responsible for getting the _Millville Daily Tribune_ into proper working order, suddenly resigned her position.
10059{_}2363{_}They had depended a great deal on Miss Briggs, so when the telegraph editor informed them she was going back to New York, they were positively bewildered by her loss.
10059{_}2364{_}Questions elicited the fact that the woman was nervous over the recent explosion and looked for further trouble from the mill hands.
10059{_}2365{_}She also suspected the two recent arrivals to be detectives, and the town was so small and so absolutely without police protection that she would not risk her personal safety by remaining longer in it.
10059{_}2366{_}"Perhaps I'm homesick," she added.
10059{_}2367{_}"It's dreadfully lonely here when I'm not at work, and for that reason I've tried to keep busy most of the time.
10059{_}2368{_}Really, I'm astonished to think I've stood this isolation so long; but now that my mind is made up, I'm going, and it is useless to ask me to remain."
10059{_}2369{_}They offered her higher wages, and Mr. Merrick himself had a long talk with her, but all arguments were unavailing.
10059{_}2370{_}"What shall we do, Thursday?"
10059{_}2371{_}asked Patsy in despair.
10059{_}2372{_}"None of us understands telegraphy."
10059{_}2373{_}"Hetty Hewitt does," he suggested.
10059{_}2374{_}"Hetty!
10059{_}2375{_}I'm afraid if I asked her to assume this work she also would leave us."
10059{_}2376{_}"No; she'll stay," he said positively.
10059{_}2377{_}"But she can't edit the telegraph news.
10059{_}2378{_}Suppose she took the messages, who would get the night news in shape for the compositors?
10059{_}2379{_}My uncle would not like to have me remain here until midnight, but even if he would permit it I have not yet mastered the art of condensing the dispatches and selecting just such items as are suitable for the _Tribune_."
10059{_}2380{_}"I'll do that, Miss Doyle," promised Smith.
10059{_}2381{_}"I've been paying especial attention to the work of Miss Briggs, for I had an idea she was getting uneasy.
10059{_}2382{_}And I can take all the day messages, too.
10059{_}2383{_}If Hetty will look after the wires evenings I can do the rest of the telegraph editor's work, and my own, too."
10059{_}2384{_}"Good gracious, Thursday!"
10059{_}2385{_}exclaimed Patsy; "you'll be running the whole paper, presently."
10059{_}2386{_}"No; I can't do the typesetting.
10059{_}2387{_}But if the Dwyer girls stick to their job--and they seem quite contented here--I'll answer for the rest of the outfit."
10059{_}2388{_}"I'm glad the Dwyer girls seem contented," she answered; "but I'm afraid to depend upon anyone now--except you."
10059{_}2389{_}He liked that compliment, but said nothing further.
10059{_}2390{_}After consulting with Louise and Beth, Patsy broached the subject to Hetty, and the artist jumped at the opportunity to do something to occupy her leisure time.
10059{_}2391{_}The work brought her in contact with Thursday Smith more than ever, and when Miss Briggs departed bag and baggage for New York, the paper suffered little through her defection.
