10059{_}2392{_}"Newspaper folk," remarked Major Doyle, who was now at the farm enjoying his vacation and worshipping at the shrine of the managing editor in the person of his versatile daughter, "are the most unreliable of any class in the world.
10059{_}2393{_}So I've often been told, and I believe it.
10059{_}2394{_}They come and go, by fits and starts, and it's a wonder the erratic rascals never put a paper out of business.
10059{_}2395{_}But they don't.
10059{_}2396{_}You never heard of a newspaper that failed to appear just because the mechanical force deserted and left it in the lurch.
10059{_}2397{_}By hook or crook the paper must be printed--and it always is.
10059{_}2398{_}So don't worry, mavourneen; when your sallow-faced artist and your hobo jack-of-all-trades desert you, there'll still be a way to keep the _Millville Tribune_ going, and therefore the world will continue to whirl on its axis."
10059{_}2399{_}"I don't believe Thursday will ever desert, and Hetty likes us too well to leave us in the lurch; but suppose those typesetters take a notion to flit?"
10059{_}2400{_}"Then," said matter-of-fact Beth, "we'll fill the paper with ready-made plate stuff and telegraph for more compositors."
10059{_}2401{_}"That's it," agreed the major, "Those people are always to be had.
10059{_}2402{_}But don't worry till the time comes.
10059{_}2403{_}As me grandfather, the commodore, once said: 'Never cross a bridge till ye come to it.'"
10059{_}2404{_}"It wasn't your grandfather who originated that remark," said Uncle John.
10059{_}2405{_}"It was, sir!
10059{_}2406{_}I defy you to prove otherwise."
10059{_}2407{_}"I'm not certain you ever had a grandfather; and he wasn't a commodore, anyhow."
10059{_}2408{_}"Sir!"
10059{_}2409{_}cried the major, glaring at his brother-in-law, "I have his commission, somewhere--laid away."
10059{_}2410{_}"Never mind," said Patsy, cheerfully, for these fierce arguments between her father and uncle--who were devotedly attached to one another--never disturbed her in the least, "the _Tribune's_ running smoothly just now, and the work is keeping us delightfully busy.
10059{_}2411{_}I think that never in my life have I enjoyed myself more than since I became a journalist."
10059{_}2412{_}"Is the thing paying dividends?"
10059{_}2413{_}inquired the major.
10059{_}2414{_}Arthur laughed.
10059{_}2415{_}"I've just been figuring up the last month's expenditures and receipts," said he.
10059{_}2416{_}"The first month didn't count, for we were getting started."
10059{_}2417{_}"And what's the result?"
10059{_}2418{_}asked the Major.
10059{_}2419{_}"Every paper we send out--for one cent--costs us eighty-eight cents to manufacture."
10059{_}2420{_}There was a painful silence for a time, broken by the major's suggestive cough.
10059{_}2421{_}"I hope," said the old soldier, solemnly, "that the paper's circulation is very small."
10059{_}2422{_}"The smallest of any daily paper in all the civilized word, sir," declared the bookkeeper.
10059{_}2423{_}"Of course," remarked Louise, with dignity; "that is what distinguishes it.
10059{_}2424{_}We did not undertake this publication to make money, and it does not cost us more than we are willing to pay for the exceptional experiences we are gaining."
10059{_}2425{_}The major raised his eyebrows; Arthur whistled softly; Uncle John smiled; but with one accord they dropped the disagreeable subject.
10059{_}2426{_}CHAPTER XVIII  OPEN WARFARE   Joe Wegg's machinery and dynamos arrived promptly and the electric plant was speedily installed at the old mill.
10059{_}2427{_}So energetically had the young man supervised his work that poles and wires were all in place as far up the road as Thompson's Crossing and a branch line run to the Wegg Farm, by the time the first test was made.
10059{_}2428{_}All Millville celebrated that first night when its streets shone resplendent under the glare of electric lights.
10059{_}2429{_}There was a public bonfire near the mill, speeches were made, and afterward Mr. Merrick served a free supper to the villagers, in the hall over Sam Cotting's General Store, where the girls assisted in waiting upon the guests, and everybody was happy and as hilarious as the fumes of good coffee could make them.
10059{_}2430{_}More speeches were made in the hall, and one of these was by Peggy McNutt, who had painted his wooden foot blue with red stripes in honor of the occasion.
10059{_}2431{_}He said, according to the report afterward printed in the Tribune:  "Feller Citizens!
10059{_}2432{_}This 'ere town's bloomin' like a new mown rose.
10059{_}2433{_}I'll bet anybody anything there ain't another town in Ameriky what's gone ahead like we hev in the past few months that's jest past.
10059{_}2434{_}(Applause.)
10059{_}2435{_}If I do say it myself, we're the mos'--eh--the mos'--eh--progressioning community in--in--this community.
10059{_}2436{_}Our community hes put out a daily paper what's a credit to--to--our community, especially the poetry; we've got a paper mill at Royal what makes paper fer New Yoruk; an' now, to cap the climate, our community hes lighted our community with 'lectric lights fit fer Lundon, New Yoruk, Canada or--or--or--our community.
10059{_}2437{_}(Laughter and cries of "Cut out the community, Peggy!")
10059{_}2438{_}No!
10059{_}2439{_}Never, feller citizens, will I cut out a community what's done so much fer our--our community.
10059{_}2440{_}If I do say it myself, the eyes of the com--of the world is upon us, an' I'm proud of the things that's ben did by our feller citizens, with my full approval, in this 'ere--this 'ere--er--community!"
10059{_}2441{_}(Cheers and a sandwich, which last offering was received by Mr. McNutt in his back hair as he turned to descend from the rostrum.)
10059{_}2442{_}Joe Wegg is reported to have said: "Neighbors, this electric plant is no plaything.
10059{_}2443{_}It is going to give you all better light, at no more cost to you than kerosene.
10059{_}2444{_}But it will do more than that: it will run machinery of all kinds better than steam will.
10059{_}2445{_}You've seen electricity running the newspaper press, and the same current has operated the big paper mills at Royal.
10059{_}2446{_}Here in this audience is a gentleman from Connecticut who has accepted my invitation to look over our village with a view to building a factory here, using the power I shall hereafter be able to furnish.
10059{_}2447{_}I am in correspondence with two other manufacturers, whom I hope to induce to locate in Millville.
10059{_}2448{_}(Enthusiastic cheers.)
10059{_}2449{_}Job Fisher, who used to live at Malvern, is planning to start a lumber mill, to cut the pine just north of here; so you see we are about to arouse from our long sleep and have a great future before us if we keep wide awake.
10059{_}2450{_}Another item of news merits your attention.
10059{_}2451{_}Bartlett has sold sixty acres of his farm to Dr. Adam Matthews, for many years a prominent physician of Boston, who is going to build a good house on the land and become a citizen of Millville.
10059{_}2452{_}We've always had to go to Huntingdon for a doctor, but now Dr. Matthews has promised to look after the health of the Millville people, although he has retired from city practice.
10059{_}2453{_}More people will come here from time to time, attracted by our enterprise and the rugged beauty of our county; real estate will become more valuable, trade will prosper and every one of the old inhabitants will find opportunities to make money."
10059{_}2454{_}(Great applause.)
10059{_}2455{_}A general discussion followed concerning the "doin's of Joe Wegg" and the prophecies he had made.
10059{_}2456{_}Opinion seemed divided as to whether the promised "boom" was desirable for Millville or not.
10059{_}2457{_}Some of the good villagers were averse to personal activity and feared the new order of things might disturb their comfort; in others a mild ambition had been awakened.
10059{_}2458{_}But while they feasted at Mr. Merrick's expense and gravely canvassed the situation, the newly installed electric lights suddenly failed.
10059{_}2459{_}Darkness fell upon the assemblage and there was an awed hush until Sam Cotting lighted the old reliable kerosene lamps.
10059{_}2460{_}Joe Wegg was as much astonished as anyone.
10059{_}2461{_}"There has been an accident to the machinery," he said to Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}2462{_}"I'll run over to the mill and see what has happened."
10059{_}2463{_}"I will go with you," said Arthur Weldon, and Major Doyle also decided to accompany the young man.
10059{_}2464{_}Uncle John and his three nieces remained in the hall, and Mr. Merrick took occasion to make a little speech in which he explained that a hitch in the working of the electric plant was liable to happen at first, but after a few days the dynamos could be fully depended upon.
10059{_}2465{_}He had scarcely finished this explanation when Arthur came running back into the hall in much excitement.
10059{_}2466{_}He approached Mr. Merrick and said in a low voice:  "The machinery is all right, sir.
10059{_}2467{_}Some one has cut the wires."
10059{_}2468{_}"Cut the wires!"
10059{_}2469{_}"Yes.
10059{_}2470{_}Joe thinks it's the work of the mill hands.
10059{_}2471{_}The wires are cut in all directions, and several of the men from Royal have been seen loitering around by Cox and Booth, the detectives."
10059{_}2472{_}The girls overheard this assertion, and Patsy exclaimed:  "I'm going to the office, to make sure our power hasn't been tampered with."
10059{_}2473{_}The meeting broke up at once and the villagers trooped out to investigate.
10059{_}2474{_}Mr. Merrick and Arthur walked with the girls to the printing office, where they found Thursday Smith and Hetty working by the light of tallow candles.
10059{_}2475{_}"The power is off," said Smith quietly.
10059{_}2476{_}"Then the wire from Royal has also been cut," said Patsy.
10059{_}2477{_}"What shall we do?
10059{_}2478{_}His paper must come out to-morrow morning, in spite of anything and everything!"
10059{_}2479{_}"Do you know who cut the wires?"
10059{_}2480{_}inquired Thursday.
10059{_}2481{_}"We think the mill hands must have done it."
10059{_}2482{_}"Not with Skeelty's consent, I'll be bound," said Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}2483{_}"The manager is too fearful of a damage suit to play any tricks."
10059{_}2484{_}"A cut wire may be repaired," suggested the pressman, and even as he spoke Joe Wegg came in, accompanied by the two detectives and the major.
10059{_}2485{_}"Cox has interviewed one of the workmen from Royal," said Joe, "and the fellow says there's a strike at the mill and everything is closed down.
10059{_}2486{_}Skeelty is barricaded in his office building, wild with fear, for the men have captured the company's store and helped themselves to the stock of liquors.
10059{_}2487{_}The man Cox spoke with, who seems to be a well disposed fellow, predicts all kinds of trouble, and perhaps rioting, before this thing is ended."
10059{_}2488{_}They listened to this report in amazement.
10059{_}2489{_}"I conjecture," said the major, "that the rascally manager has given his men too much leeway.
10059{_}2490{_}He's encouraged them in mischief until they've taken the bit between their teeth and turned against even their master.
10059{_}2491{_}I have no personal acquaintance with the villain, but I imagine it serves him right."
10059{_}2492{_}"But, dear me!"
10059{_}2493{_}cried Patsy, wringing her hands; "what'll become of the paper?
10059{_}2494{_}It's nearly ten o'clock now."
10059{_}2495{_}Thursday turned to Joe Wegg.
10059{_}2496{_}"Can't we connect our supply wire with your new plant, so as to use your power?"
10059{_}2497{_}he asked.
10059{_}2498{_}"Easily.
10059{_}2499{_}An hour's work will serve to make the connection.
10059{_}2500{_}But unless we watch the wire every minute those fellows will cut it again.
10059{_}2501{_}The town's full of the rascals, and they're not exactly sober, either."
10059{_}2502{_}"Watch the wire; that's the idea," said Uncle John.
10059{_}2503{_}"It's only a short distance to the mill, and I'm sure the villagers will volunteer for this duty."
10059{_}2504{_}"Of course," said Joe.
10059{_}2505{_}"Major Doyle, will you mount guard over my men at the dynamos, to see they're not interfered with, while I look after the wire?"
10059{_}2506{_}"Sure enough; it'll remind me of the old war times," said the major readily.
10059{_}2507{_}"Where is Arthur?"
10059{_}2508{_}asked Louise.
10059{_}2509{_}"We left him at the mill."
10059{_}2510{_}They left the office at once, Joe to get his line-men at work, and the major to join Weldon in guarding the dynamos.
10059{_}2511{_}One of the detectives went with Mr. Wegg, but the other, whose name was Booth, remained to guard the printing office.
10059{_}2512{_}Mr. Merrick now proposed that he take the girls home.
10059{_}2513{_}Patsy and Beth refused to leave until the emergency was past, when the major and Arthur could drive them to the farm, but Louise was tired and went with Uncle John in his buggy, the surrey being left for the rest of the party to use.
10059{_}2514{_}Arthur ran over for a moment to say everything was quiet at the mill and he did not think there would be any further trouble, and the report considerably reassured them.
10059{_}2515{_}CHAPTER XIX  A MERE MATTER OF REVENGE   Hetty and Thursday continued to work on the paper.
10059{_}2516{_}"We'll have everything ready by the time the line is connected," said the artist.
10059{_}2517{_}"Then it will be but a few moments' work to run off the edition."
10059{_}2518{_}Patsy and Beth held candles for them, for the electric lights had been cut off with the power; so, seeing them all busily engaged, Arthur Weldon decided to return to the mill to join the Major.
10059{_}2519{_}Booth sat in the front office, near the door, and in the darkness Arthur nearly stumbled over him.
10059{_}2520{_}"Going away, sir?"
10059{_}2521{_}asked the man.
10059{_}2522{_}"Yes; I'll see if I can be of any assistance at the mill."
10059{_}2523{_}"Be careful.
10059{_}2524{_}Those workmen have been drifting into town in squads, the last few minutes, and most of them are reckless with drink."
10059{_}2525{_}"I'll watch out," said Arthur.
10059{_}2526{_}In the middle of the road a group of mill hands conversed excitedly in some foreign tongue; but they paid no attention to Weldon as he passed them.
10059{_}2527{_}Others joined them, presently, and one began a harangue in a loud voice, to which they listened eagerly.
10059{_}2528{_}Then Bob West slipped across from the hardware store and ran against the detective in the doorway of the printing office.
10059{_}2529{_}"Who's this?"
10059{_}2530{_}he demanded, holding the man in a firm grip.
10059{_}2531{_}"Booth, sir."
10059{_}2532{_}"Good.
10059{_}2533{_}I could not recognize you in this darkness.
10059{_}2534{_}Are you armed?"
10059{_}2535{_}"Yes."
10059{_}2536{_}"Then you and I will defend this door.
10059{_}2537{_}Who is inside?"
10059{_}2538{_}"The pressman--Thursday Smith--and three of the girls."
10059{_}2539{_}"The compositors?"
10059{_}2540{_}"No; they've gone to the hotel.
10059{_}2541{_}Miss Doyle, Miss DeGraf, and--Hetty Hewitt."
10059{_}2542{_}West went into the hack room, which was faintly illumined by candles stuck here and there.
10059{_}2543{_}The girls and Smith were all bending over the imposing stone, where the forms of the paper were being made up.
10059{_}2544{_}"Here," said West, taking a revolver from his pocket and laying it on the table; "I'm afraid there may be an attack on this office in a few minutes, for I understand the language of those strikers and have been listening to them.
10059{_}2545{_}If any of the mill hands attempt to break into this room don't be afraid to shoot."
10059{_}2546{_}"Why should the men wish to attack us, sir?"
10059{_}2547{_}asked Patsy wonderingly.
10059{_}2548{_}"There are several reasons.
10059{_}2549{_}They're after Smith, for one thing.
10059{_}2550{_}They've an old grudge against him to settle.
10059{_}2551{_}Aside from the mere matter of revenge I overheard one of them telling his friends to smash the press and keep the paper from coming out, and Mr. Boglin would pay them well for the job."
10059{_}2552{_}Smith carelessly thrust the revolver into his hip pocket.
10059{_}2553{_}"The paper will come out if Mr. Wegg gives us the power," he said.
10059{_}2554{_}"Can you let me have a revolver, Mr.
10059{_}2555{_}West?"
10059{_}2556{_}asked Hetty.
10059{_}2557{_}"Could you use it?"
10059{_}2558{_}"I think so."
10059{_}2559{_}He looked at her a moment and then took a second revolver from his pocket.
10059{_}2560{_}"I've robbed my hardware stock," he said with a smile.
10059{_}2561{_}"But I advise you girls to keep your hands off the thing unless a crisis arises.
10059{_}2562{_}I don't imagine the gang will get past me and Booth at the entrance, but if any stragglers come your way Smith has authority to drive them back.
10059{_}2563{_}I'm justice of the peace, and I hereby appoint you all special officers of the law."
10059{_}2564{_}He said this lightly, fearing to alarm the girls unnecessarily, and then passed through the doorway and joined Booth at the front.
10059{_}2565{_}The telephone rang and Patsy answered it.
10059{_}2566{_}"How soon will the forms be ready?"
10059{_}2567{_}asked Arthur's voice.
10059{_}2568{_}"In ten minutes--perhaps five," she answered.
10059{_}2569{_}"We'll have the power on in ten minutes more.
10059{_}2570{_}Tell Smith not to lose an instant's time in running off the edition, for we don't know how long we can keep the line open.
10059{_}2571{_}The strikers are threatening us, even now."
10059{_}2572{_}"All right," called Patsy; "just give us the power for a few minutes, and we'll be through for to-night."
10059{_}2573{_}She went back to Thursday and reported.
10059{_}2574{_}"There may be a few typographical errors, and I'm afraid it's a bad make-up," he remarked; "but I'll have the thing on the press in five minutes."
10059{_}2575{_}With mallet and shooting-stick he tightened the quoins, then lifted the heavy iron frames filled with type and slid them onto the bed of the press.
10059{_}2576{_}They gave him all the light the flickering candles afforded as he adjusted the machinery, and all were bending over the press when a low, distant growl was heard, rising slowly to a frenzied shout.
10059{_}2577{_}A revolver popped--another--followed by wild cries from the street.
10059{_}2578{_}The girls grew a little pale, but Thursday Smith put his hand on the lever of the press and said:  "All right.
10059{_}2579{_}The moment they give us the current we're ready to run."
10059{_}2580{_}Patsy straightened up with a sigh of relief, then gave a low cry as the screens of the two windows of the pressroom were smashed in and through the openings men began to tumble into the room.
10059{_}2581{_}At once Hetty confronted them with leveled revolver and the sight caused them to hesitate.
10059{_}2582{_}"Out o' the way, you women!"
10059{_}2583{_}called a burly fellow who wore a green sweater and an oilskin hat; "we don't want to hurt you if we can help.
10059{_}2584{_}There's the one we're after!"
10059{_}2585{_}He pointed a finger at Thursday Smith.
10059{_}2586{_}"You can't have him," retorted Beth, half shielded behind the militant Hetty.
10059{_}2587{_}"This is private property, and you're trespassing.
10059{_}2588{_}Unless you go away at once you will suffer the consequences."
10059{_}2589{_}This defense seemed to surprise them, for they fell back a little toward the windows.
10059{_}2590{_}At that moment, with a low rumble, the press started, moving slowly at first but gradually acquiring speed.
10059{_}2591{_}The sight aroused the resentment of the invaders.
10059{_}2592{_}"Stop that press!"
10059{_}2593{_}yelled their spokesman excitedly.
10059{_}2594{_}"Stop it, Smith, or we'll put both you and the machine out of business."
10059{_}2595{_}Thursday paid no attention to anything but his press.
10059{_}2596{_}The huge cylinder of white paper was unrolling, passing under the platen and emerging at the other end as neatly folded copies of the Millville Daily Tribune.
10059{_}2597{_}With a roar of rage the big fellow leaped forward, but at the action a shot rang out and he fell headlong almost at the foot of the press.
10059{_}2598{_}Beth and Patsy turned their heads an instant to glance at Hetty.
10059{_}2599{_}The artist's face was white and set; her eyes sparkled brilliantly; she held the still smoking weapon in readiness for another shot.
10059{_}2600{_}But the men were awed by the fall of their leader.
10059{_}2601{_}They watched Beth leap to the platform beside Thursday Smith and draw his revolver from his pocket, where he had placed it.
10059{_}2602{_}Hetty's courage had inspired her, and Beth had handled pistols before.
10059{_}2603{_}The men read the determined eyes fixed upon them; they noted Smith's indifference to their threats.
10059{_}2604{_}The defenders of the press and pressman were only girls, but they were girls evidently not afraid to shoot.
10059{_}2605{_}No advance was made and the tableau was dramatic.
10059{_}2606{_}Smith watched his press with undivided attention and it clattered away at full speed until the frail building shook with its powerful, steady motion.
10059{_}2607{_}Then suddenly it began to slow down.
10059{_}2608{_}The power was off, and the machine came to an abrupt stop.
10059{_}2609{_}Thursday stepped from the platform and looked at the index of the counter.
10059{_}2610{_}"Four hundred and sixty-three.
10059{_}2611{_}Twenty-two short, Miss Doyle," he announced.
10059{_}2612{_}"That'll do, Thursday."
10059{_}2613{_}He came to her side, then, facing the sullen, glowering group of mill hands.
10059{_}2614{_}"Boys," said he, "it won't do you any good to interfere with us to-night.
10059{_}2615{_}The paper for to-morrow morning is already printed, and Ojoy Boglin isn't a big enough man to stop it, now or ever.
10059{_}2616{_}Better go back to Royal and settle your troubles with Skeelty, for if you stay here the citizens of Millville are in the mood to shoot you down like dogs."
10059{_}2617{_}They stood undecided a moment, but the argument had evidently struck home.
10059{_}2618{_}"What's the matter with Harris?"
10059{_}2619{_}asked one, pointing to the motionless form of the man in the green sweater.
10059{_}2620{_}"Is he dead?"
10059{_}2621{_}"I suppose so," answered Thursday coolly; but he stooped to examine Hetty's victim, rolling him over so that his face was upward.
10059{_}2622{_}"No; he isn't hurt much, I'm sorry to say.
10059{_}2623{_}The bullet glanced off his forehead and stunned him, that's all.
10059{_}2624{_}Take the brute, if you want him, and go."
10059{_}2625{_}They obeyed in silence.
10059{_}2626{_}Several stepped forward and raised the unconscious Harris, bearing him to the window, where they passed him to those without.
10059{_}2627{_}Then they also retreated through the windows and the room was cleared.
10059{_}2628{_}Only then did Hetty and Beth venture to lower their weapons.
10059{_}2629{_}"Oh, dear!"
10059{_}2630{_}cried Patsy, in a low, agitated voice; "I'm so glad you didn't kill him, Hetty."
10059{_}2631{_}"I'm not," returned the artist doggedly.
10059{_}2632{_}"He deserved death, at the least, and by killing him I'd have cheated the gallows."
10059{_}2633{_}Then she glanced around at the horrified faces of her friends and burst into tears.
10059{_}2634{_}CHAPTER XX  DEFENDING THE PRESS   In the front room Bob West and the detective were having a busy time.
10059{_}2635{_}At the first rush they each fired a shot over the heads of the mob, merely to let them know the place was guarded.
10059{_}2636{_}In the darkness it was impossible for the strikers to tell how many armed men confronted them, so they fell back a little, but formed a cordon around the entire building.
10059{_}2637{_}From the printing office to the old mill was a distance of only a few hundred feet, and every able-bodied inhabitant of Millville except Peggy McNutt and Sara Cotting--who had discreetly disappeared at the first sign of danger--was assisting Joe Wegg to protect the electric cable he was trying to connect.
10059{_}2638{_}The men from Royal were scattered all along the line, peering through the dim light to discover a vulnerable point of attack but deterred from interfering by the determination of the stalwart defenders.
10059{_}2639{_}Mobs are invariably cowardly, and this one, composed of the lowest strata of mixed American and foreign laborers, was no exception to the general rule.
10059{_}2640{_}However, when word was finally passed along from the mill that the dynamo was running and supplying power to the printing press, a howl of rage went up and a sudden rush was made for the line, the attack concentrating at one point.
10059{_}2641{_}The defenders promptly grouped themselves in front of the threatened pole and Seth Davis, the blacksmith, wielding a heavy sledge hammer, did valiant service, clearing a space around him with little difficulty.
10059{_}2642{_}Joe Wegg, Arthur Weldon, Cox the detective, Lon Taft, Nick Thome and even little Skim Clark were all in the melee, fighting desperately for time to enable Thursday Smith to work his press, using whatever cudgels they had been able to pick up to keep the assailants from the pole.
10059{_}2643{_}Slowly, however, they were forced back by superior numbers until finally one of the mill hands clambered up the pole and cut the wire.
10059{_}2644{_}"Never mind," said Arthur to Joe, as they retreated fighting toward the printing office; "I think they've had time to run off the edition, provided Smith was ready with the forms."
10059{_}2645{_}The mob was by this time in an ugly mood and the nearer Joe and Arthur edged toward the printing office the more numerous their enemies became.
10059{_}2646{_}The Millville people were getting rather the worst of the scrimmage when out rushed Thursday Smith, swinging a stout iron bar he had taken from the press, and with this terrible weapon he struck out so vigorously that the diversion in their favor enabled the retreating villagers to gain the office, where Booth and Bob West fired several shots that effectually checked the mob.
10059{_}2647{_}"Stand back, ye villains!"
10059{_}2648{_}cried a loud voice, as Major Doyle marched calmly down the road from the mill; "how dare ye interfere with a gentleman?"
10059{_}2649{_}One of the leaders confronted him menacingly.
10059{_}2650{_}The major slapped his face with the flat of his hand and then kicked the fellow in the shins.
10059{_}2651{_}"Didn't I say to get out o' my way?"
10059{_}2652{_}he roared, and to the surprise of everyone--even the major, perhaps--they fell hack and allowed him to walk leisurely into the printing office.
10059{_}2653{_}Having succeeded in their primary attempt to cut the wire, and finding the determined band of defenders more dangerous than they had thought, the workmen retreated in the direction of Royal, where there was more to be gained by rioting than in Millville.
10059{_}2654{_}When at last the town was clear of them, Arthur, who was considerably battered and bruised but pleased with the triumphant ending of the adventure, drove the girls and the major to the farm.
10059{_}2655{_}They urged Hetty to accompany them, but she declared she was not a bit nervous and preferred to sleep at the hotel.
10059{_}2656{_}"I think the trouble is over for to-night," said West, and all agreed with him.
10059{_}2657{_}Cox and Booth decided to sleep in the printing office, and after the girls had driven away with their escorts and the villagers had dispersed to their homes, Thursday put on his coat and walked to the hotel with Hetty.
10059{_}2658{_}"All that row was about me," he remarked disconsolately.
10059{_}2659{_}"But they didn't get you," said Hetty, triumph in her voice.
10059{_}2660{_}"No."
10059{_}2661{_}He did not mention her bravery, or the loyal support of Beth and Patsy, but after a moment he added: "I'm not worth defending."
10059{_}2662{_}"How do you know?"
10059{_}2663{_}asked Hetty.
10059{_}2664{_}"It occurs to me, Mr. Smith, that you are as much a stranger to yourself as to us."
10059{_}2665{_}"That is true."
10059{_}2666{_}"And in emergencies you are not averse to defending others.
10059{_}2667{_}Of course Miss DeGraf and her cousin wanted the paper printed, at all hazards.
10059{_}2668{_}I don't blame them for that; but I--"  She hesitated.
10059{_}2669{_}"You simply stood by a comrade.
10059{_}2670{_}Thank you, Hetty."
10059{_}2671{_}"Good night, Thursday."
10059{_}2672{_}"Will you be able to sleep to-night?"
10059{_}2673{_}"I'm going straight to bed.
10059{_}2674{_}The rumpus has quieted my nerves."
10059{_}2675{_}"Good night, then."
10059{_}2676{_}In the early morning Mr. Merrick was awakened by a red glare that flooded his bedroom.
10059{_}2677{_}Going to the window he found the sky at the north full of flame.
10059{_}2678{_}He threw on his bathrobe and went to the door of Arthur Weldon's room, arousing the young man with a rap on the panels.
10059{_}2679{_}"The settlement at Royal is burning," he reported.
10059{_}2680{_}Arthur came out, very weary and drowsy, for he had not been asleep long and the strenuous work of the night had tired him.
10059{_}2681{_}"Let it burn," he said, glancing through a window at the lurid light of the conflagration.
10059{_}2682{_}"We couldn't be of any use going over there and, after all, it isn't our affair to relieve Skeelty."
10059{_}2683{_}Then he told Uncle John of the riot in the village, for the old gentleman had been sound asleep when the party returned to the farm.
10059{_}2684{_}"The blaze is the work of those crazy strikers, I suppose," said Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}2685{_}"It looks from here as if they had set fire to their own homes, as well as to the paper mills and office and store buildings.
10059{_}2686{_}It will be fortunate if the forest does not also burn."
10059{_}2687{_}"Don't worry, sir," advised Arthur.
10059{_}2688{_}"We'll discover the extent of the fire by daylight.
10059{_}2689{_}For my part, I'm going back to bed, and it will be well for you to follow my example."
10059{_}2690{_}"Another item for the paper," whispered a soft voice, and there was Patsy beside them at the window.
10059{_}2691{_}Mr. Merrick sighed.
10059{_}2692{_}"I had no idea so much excitement could possibly happen at Millville," said he.
10059{_}2693{_}"If this keeps on we'll have to go back to New York for quiet.
10059{_}2694{_}But let us get to bed, my dear, for to-morrow is likely to be a busy day for us all."
10059{_}2695{_}CHAPTER XXI  THE COMING OF FOGERTY   The homeless mill hands flocked to Chazy Junction next day, from whence a freight train distributed them over other parts of the country.
10059{_}2696{_}The clearing at Royal Falls was now a heap of charred embers, for every one of the cheap, rough-board buildings had been consumed by the fire.
10059{_}2697{_}Skeelty had watched the destruction of his plant with feelings of mingled glee and disgust.
10059{_}2698{_}He was insured against loss, and his rash workmen, who had turned upon him so unexpectedly, had accidentally settled the strike and their own future by starting the fire during their drunken orgies.
10059{_}2699{_}There being no longer a mill to employ them they went elsewhere for work, rather glad of the change and regretting nothing.
10059{_}2700{_}As for the manager, he stood to lose temporary profits but was not wholly displeased by the catastrophe.
10059{_}2701{_}Transportation of his manufactured products had been so irregular and undefendable that even while he watched the blaze he determined to rebuild his plant nearer the main line of a railway, for many such locations could be found where the pine was as plentiful as here.
10059{_}2702{_}At dawn he entered the hotel at Millville with his arms full of books and papers which he had succeeded in saving from the fire, and securing a room went directly to bed.
10059{_}2703{_}It was afternoon when he awoke and after obtaining a meal he strolled out into the village and entered the newspaper office.
10059{_}2704{_}"Here's an item for your paper," he said to Patsy, who was busy at her desk.
10059{_}2705{_}"The mills at Royal will never be rebuilt, and Millville has lost the only chance it ever had of becoming a manufacturing center.
10059{_}2706{_}The whole settlement, which belonged to Boglin and myself, went up in smoke, and I'm willing to let it go at that.
10059{_}2707{_}I shall collect the insurance, make myself good, and if anything's left over, that fool Boglin is welcome to it.
10059{_}2708{_}I admit I made a mistake in ever allowing him to induce me to build at Royal.
10059{_}2709{_}Boglin owned the land and I used his money, so I gave up to him; but I'm through with the _honer'ble_ ass now.
10059{_}2710{_}Put it all in the paper; it'll make him feel good.
10059{_}2711{_}You might add that I'm taking the evening train for New York, shaking the dust of your miserable village from my feet for good and all."
10059{_}2712{_}"Thank you, sir," said Patsy, brightly; "the Millville people will appreciate their good luck, I'm sure."
10059{_}2713{_}Skeelty hung around the town for awhile, sneering at the new electric light plant and insolently railing at any of the natives who would converse with him.
10059{_}2714{_}Then he hired Nick Thorne to drive him over to Chazy Junction, and that was the last Millville ever saw of him.
10059{_}2715{_}During this day Joe Wegg's men succeeded in repairing all the wires which had been tampered with and in making a proper and permanent connection of the cable to the printing office.
10059{_}2716{_}That evening the village was again brilliantly lighted and thereafter the big dynamos whirled peacefully and without interruption.
10059{_}2717{_}The girls had a busy day, as Uncle John had predicted, for all the exciting incidents of the evening and night before had to be written up and the next day's paper teemed with "news" of a character to interest all its readers.
10059{_}2718{_}Beth's editorial declared the neighborhood well rid of the paper mill, which had been of little advantage but had caused no end of annoyance because of the rough and mischievous character of the workmen employed.
10059{_}2719{_}In this statement nearly everyone agreed with her.
10059{_}2720{_}Several had been wounded in the riot of the eventful evening, but none seriously injured.
10059{_}2721{_}The workmen took away their damaged comrades and Lon Taft drove over to Huntingdon and had his head sewed up by the doctor.
10059{_}2722{_}Other villagers suffered mere bruises, but all who engaged in the fight posed as heroes and even Peggy McNutt, who figured as "not present," told marvelous tales of how he had worsted seven mill hands in a stand-up fight, using only his invincible fists.
10059{_}2723{_}The following forenoon the liveryman at the Junction brought to Millville a passenger who had arrived by the morning train--a quiet, boyish-looking man with a shock of brick-red hair and a thin, freckled face.
10059{_}2724{_}He was driven directly to the Merrick farm, where Uncle John received him cordially, but with surprise, and at once favored the new arrival with a long interview in his private room.
10059{_}2725{_}The girls, who had not yet gone to the office, awaited somewhat impatiently the result of this conference, for they already knew the red-headed youth to be the great Fogerty--admitted by even his would-be rivals, the king of New York detectives.
10059{_}2726{_}Also they knew that Uncle John had employed him some time ago to ferret out the mystery of the identity of Thursday Smith, and the fact of Fogerty's presence indicated he had something to report.
10059{_}2727{_}However, when Mr. Merrick came out of the private room his usually cheery countenance wore a troubled expression.
10059{_}2728{_}Fogerty was invariably placid and inscrutable, so no explanation could be gleaned from his demeanor.
10059{_}2729{_}"Ready for town, my dears?"
10059{_}2730{_}asked Uncle John.
10059{_}2731{_}"Yes; the surrey is waiting," answered Louise.
10059{_}2732{_}"Then go along, and Fogerty and I will join you at the office presently.
10059{_}2733{_}I want to confer with the major and Arthur before--before taking any steps to--"  "What's the news, Uncle?"
10059{_}2734{_}demanded Patsy, impatiently.
10059{_}2735{_}"You shall know in good time."
10059{_}2736{_}"Who is Thursday Smith?"
10059{_}2737{_}"By and by, dear.
10059{_}2738{_}Don't bother me now.
10059{_}2739{_}But that reminds me; you are to say nothing to--to--Thursday about Mr. Fogerty's arrival.
10059{_}2740{_}Treat him--Thursday, you know--just as you have always done, for the present, at least.
10059{_}2741{_}Whatever we determine on in regard to this man, during our conference, we must not forget that he has acted most gallantly since he came to Millville.
10059{_}2742{_}We really owe him a debt of gratitude."
10059{_}2743{_}With this somewhat incomprehensible statement the girls were forced to content themselves.
10059{_}2744{_}Feeling quite helpless, they drove to the office and left the men to settle the fate of Thursday Smith.
10059{_}2745{_}The "pressman" was now the man-of-all-work about the modest but trim little publishing plant.
10059{_}2746{_}He attended to whatever job printing came in, made the etchings from Hetty's drawings, cast the stereotypes, made up the forms and operated the press.
10059{_}2747{_}But aside from this mechanical work Smith took the telegraphic news received by Hetty, edited and condensed it and wrote the black-letter headings over the various items.
10059{_}2748{_}All this, with a general supervision over the girl compositors, kept the man busy from daybreak to midnight.
10059{_}2749{_}In spite of this, the Tribune was essentially a "girls' paper," since Thursday Smith was the only man employed on it--not counting the "dummy" editor, Arthur Weldon, who did nothing but keep the books, and found this not an arduous task.
10059{_}2750{_}Hetty, at Miss Briggs' desk, attended the telegraph instrument and long-distance telephone, receiving news over both wires, and still found time to draw her daily cartoons and additional humorous sketches which she "worked in" whenever the mood seized her.
10059{_}2751{_}The typesetting was done by the Dwyer sisters--a colorless pair but quite reliable--while the reportorial and editorial work was divided between Louise, Beth and Patsy, none of whom shirked a single duty.
10059{_}2752{_}Indeed, they had come to love this work dearly and were enthusiastic over the _Tribune_, which they fondly believed was being watched with envious admiration by all the journalistic world.
10059{_}2753{_}This belief was not wholly due to egotism.
10059{_}2754{_}Their "exchanges," both city and country, had shown considerable interest in the "Millville Experiment," as they called it, and only a few days before the leading journal of a good-sized city had commented at length on the "girls' newspaper" and, after indulging in some humorous remarks, concluded quite seriously with the statement that "its evident sincerity, clean contents and typographical neatness render the _Millville Daily Tribune_ worthy a better setting than the somnolent country village whose census is too low to be officially recorded."
10059{_}2755{_}"But that's all right," said Patsy, smiling at the praise; "we'd never have dared to start a newspaper anywhere else, because a journal that will do for Millville might not make a hit if it bumped against experienced competition."
10059{_}2756{_}"We were woefully ignorant when we began, a few weeks ago," commented Beth, glancing with pride at her latest editorial, which she thought had caught the oracular tone of the big city newspapers.
10059{_}2757{_}"And we're not expert journalists, even yet," added Louise, with a sigh.
10059{_}2758{_}"We've improved, to be sure; but I imagine there is still lots of room for improvement."
10059{_}2759{_}"One trouble," said Patsy, "is that every inhabitant of Millville wants to see his or her name in print every day, whether he or she has done anything worthy of publication or not.
10059{_}2760{_}If the name isn't printed, we've made an enemy; and, if it is, the paper is sure to suffer more or less ridicule."
10059{_}2761{_}"That is quite true, my dear," responded Louise, the reporter.
10059{_}2762{_}"I've said everything, about every one of them, that has ever happened, or threatened to happen, since we started the paper, and it is driving me crazy to discover anything more about these stupid natives that will do to print."
10059{_}2763{_}Hetty had overheard this conversation and now looked up with a smile.
10059{_}2764{_}"Has your 'local happenings' column been prepared for to-morrow, Mrs.
10059{_}2765{_}Weldon?"
10059{_}2766{_}she inquired.
10059{_}2767{_}"No; I'm about to start out to unearth some items," replied Louise, wearily.
10059{_}2768{_}"Let me do it for you.
10059{_}2769{_}I've an hour or so to spare and I won't need to leave my desk," suggested the artist.
10059{_}2770{_}"It is my duty, you know, Hetty, and I've no right to evade it."
10059{_}2771{_}"Evade it for to-day.
10059{_}2772{_}Go home and rest.
10059{_}2773{_}I'll do your column for to-morrow, and after the vacation you can tackle the thrilling situations with better courage."
10059{_}2774{_}"Thank you, Hetty.
10059{_}2775{_}But I won't go home.
10059{_}2776{_}I'll wait here to see Fogerty."
10059{_}2777{_}"Fogerty!"
10059{_}2778{_}exclaimed the artist, with a start of surprise.
10059{_}2779{_}"Do you mean the detective?"
10059{_}2780{_}"Yes," said Louise, regretting she had inadvertently mentioned the name.
10059{_}2781{_}"But what is there now to detect?"
10059{_}2782{_}asked Hetty suspiciously.
10059{_}2783{_}"Our troubles seem ended with the burning of the mill and the flitting of Skeelty and his workmen."
10059{_}2784{_}Louise hardly knew how to reply; but Patsy, who trusted the queer girl artist, said quite frankly:  "There remains the mystery of Thursday Smith to fathom, you know."
10059{_}2785{_}Hetty flushed and an indignant look swept over her face.
10059{_}2786{_}"What right has anyone to solve that mystery?"
10059{_}2787{_}she asked defiantly.
10059{_}2788{_}"Isn't that Thursday Smith's own business?"
10059{_}2789{_}"Perhaps," returned Patsy, somewhat amused; "but Smith hasn't been able to discover who he is--or was, rather--and seems really anxious to know."
10059{_}2790{_}Hetty bent over her desk for a time.
10059{_}2791{_}Then she looked up and her thin features were white and drawn with anxiety.
10059{_}2792{_}"When you discover who Thursday Smith is," said she, "the Millville Tribune will lose its right bower."
10059{_}2793{_}"Why?"
10059{_}2794{_}"Before his accident, or whatever it was that made him lose his memory, he was an unusual man, a man of exceptional ability.
10059{_}2795{_}You know that."
10059{_}2796{_}"We are all inclined to admit it," answered Patsy.
10059{_}2797{_}"But what then?"
10059{_}2798{_}"Men of ability," declared Hetty slowly, "are of two classes: the very successful, who attain high and honorable positions, or the clever scoundrels who fasten themselves like leeches on humanity and bleed their victims with heartless unconcern.
10059{_}2799{_}What will you gain if you unmask the past of Thursday Smith?
10059{_}2800{_}You uncover a rogue or a man of affairs, and in either case you will lose your pressman.
10059{_}2801{_}Better leave the curtain drawn, Miss Doyle, and accept Thursday Smith as he is."
10059{_}2802{_}There was so much good sense in this reasoning that all three girls were impressed and began to regret that Uncle John had called Fogerty to untangle the skein.
10059{_}2803{_}But it was now too late for such repentance and, after all, they were curious to discover who their remarkable employee really was.
10059{_}2804{_}Even while the awkward silence that had fallen upon the group of girls continued, the door opened to admit Uncle John, Fogerty, Major Doyle and Arthur Weldon.
10059{_}2805{_}Except for the detective they were stern-faced and uncompromising.
10059{_}2806{_}CHAPTER XXII  UNMASKED   Quintus Fogerty was as unlike the typical detective as one could imagine.
10059{_}2807{_}Small in size, slight and boyish, his years could not readily be determined by the ordinary observer.
10059{_}2808{_}His face was deeply furrowed and lined, yet a few paces away it seemed the face of a boy of eighteen.
10059{_}2809{_}His cold gray eyes were persistently staring but conveyed no inkling of his thoughts.
10059{_}2810{_}His brick-red hair was as unkempt as if it had never known a comb, yet the attire of the great detective was as fastidiously neat as if he had dressed for an important social function.
10059{_}2811{_}Taken altogether there was something mistrustful and uncanny about Fogerty's looks, and his habit of eternally puffing cigarettes rendered his companionship unpleasant.
10059{_}2812{_}Yet of the man's professional ability there was no doubt; Mr. Merrick and Arthur Weldon had had occasion to employ him before, with results that justified their faith in him.
10059{_}2813{_}The detective greeted the young ladies with polite bows, supplemented by an aimless compliment on the neatness of their office.
10059{_}2814{_}"Never would have recognized it as a newspaper sanctum," said he in his thin, piping voice.
10059{_}2815{_}"No litter, no stale pipes lying about, no cursing and quarreling, no excitement whatever.
10059{_}2816{_}The editorial room is the index to the workshop; I'll see if the mechanical department is kept as neatly."
10059{_}2817{_}He opened the door to the back room, passed through and closed it softly behind him.
10059{_}2818{_}Mr. Merrick made a dive for the door and followed Fogerty.
10059{_}2819{_}"What's the verdict, Arthur?"
10059{_}2820{_}asked Louise curiously.
10059{_}2821{_}"Why, I--I believe the verdict isn't rendered yet," he hastily replied, and followed Mr. Merrick into the pressroom.
10059{_}2822{_}"Now, then," cried Patsy, grabbing the major firmly, "you'll not stir a step, sir, until you tell us the news!"
10059{_}2823{_}"What news, Patricia?"
10059{_}2824{_}Inquired the old gentleman blandly.
10059{_}2825{_}"Who was Thursday Smith?"
10059{_}2826{_}"The identical individual he is now," said the Major.
10059{_}2827{_}"Don't prevaricate, sir!
10059{_}2828{_}Who was he?
10059{_}2829{_}What did he do?
10059{_}2830{_}What is his right name?"
10059{_}2831{_}"Is it because you are especially interested in this man, my dear, or are ye simply consumed with feminine curiosity?"
10059{_}2832{_}"Be good, Daddy!
10059{_}2833{_}Tell us all about it," said Patsy coaxingly.
10059{_}2834{_}"The man Thursday, then, was likely enough the brother of Robinson Crusoe's man Friday."
10059{_}2835{_}"Major, you're trifling!"
10059{_}2836{_}"Or mayhap an ex-president of the United States, or forby the senator from Oklahoma.
10059{_}2837{_}Belike he was once minister to Borneo, an' came home in a hurry an' forgot who he was.
10059{_}2838{_}But John Merrick will be wanting me."
10059{_}2839{_}He escaped and opened the door.
10059{_}2840{_}Then, with his hand on the knob, he turned and added:  "Why don't ye come in, me journalistic investigators, and see the fun for yerselves?
10059{_}2841{_}I suspect there's an item in store for ye."
10059{_}2842{_}Then he went in, and they took the hint and entered the pressroom in a fluttering group.
10059{_}2843{_}Fogerty stood with his hands in his pockets intently watching the Dwyer girls set type, while at his elbow Mr. Merrick was explaining in a casual voice how many "m's" were required to make a newspaper column.
10059{_}2844{_}In another part of the long room Arthur Weldon was leaning over a table containing the half-empty forms, as if critically examining them.
10059{_}2845{_}Smith, arrayed in overalls and jumper, was cleaning and oiling the big press.
10059{_}2846{_}"A daily newspaper," said the major, loudly, as he held up a warning finger to the bevy of nieces, behind whom Hetty's pale face appeared, "means a daily grind for all concerned in it.
10059{_}2847{_}There's no vacation for the paper, no hyphens, no skipping a day or two if it has a bad cold; it's the tyrant that leads its slaves by the nose, metaphorically, and has no conscience.
10059{_}2848{_}Just as regularly as the world rolls 'round the press rolls out the newspaper, and human life or death makes little difference to either of the revolutionists."
10059{_}2849{_}While he spoke the Major led the way across the room to the stereotyping plant, which brought his party to a position near the press.
10059{_}2850{_}Smith glanced at them and went on with his work.
10059{_}2851{_}It was not unusual to have the pressroom thus invaded.
10059{_}2852{_}Presently Fogerty strolled over, smoking his eternal cigarette, and stood watching the pressman, as if interested in the oiling of the complicated machine.
10059{_}2853{_}Smith, feeling himself under observation, glanced up again in an unconcerned way, and as he faced the detective Fogerty gave a cleverly assumed start and exclaimed:  "Good God!"
10059{_}2854{_}Instantly Thursday Smith straightened up and looked at the man questioningly.
10059{_}2855{_}Fogerty stretched out his hand and said, as if in wonder:  "Why, Melville, old man, what are you doing here?
10059{_}2856{_}We wondered what had become of you, all these months.
10059{_}2857{_}Shake hands, my boy!
10059{_}2858{_}I'm glad I've found you."
10059{_}2859{_}Smith leaned against the press and stared at him with dilated eyes.
10059{_}2860{_}Everyone in the room was regarding the scene with intense but repressed excitement.
10059{_}2861{_}"What's wrong, Harold?"
10059{_}2862{_}continued Fogerty, as if hurt by the other's hesitation to acknowledge their acquaintance.
10059{_}2863{_}"You haven't forgotten me, have you?
10059{_}2864{_}I'm McCormick, you know, and you and I have had many a good time together in the past."
10059{_}2865{_}Smith passed his hand across his forehead with a dazed gesture.
10059{_}2866{_}"What name did you call me, sir?"
10059{_}2867{_}he asked.
10059{_}2868{_}"Melville; Harold Melville, of East Sixty-sixth street.
10059{_}2869{_}I'm sure I'm right.
10059{_}2870{_}There can't be two like you in the world, you know."
10059{_}2871{_}Thursday Smith stepped down from the platform and with a staggering gait walked to a stool, on which he weakly sank.
10059{_}2872{_}He wiped the beads of perspiration from his forehead and looked at Fogerty with a half frightened air.
10059{_}2873{_}"And you--are--McCormick?"
10059{_}2874{_}he faltered.
10059{_}2875{_}"Of course."
10059{_}2876{_}Smith stared a moment and then shook his head.
10059{_}2877{_}"It's no use," he said despairingly; "I can't recall a single memory of either Harold Melville or--or his friend McCormick.
10059{_}2878{_}Pardon me, sir; I must confess my mind is absolutely blank concerning all my life previous to the last two years.
10059{_}2879{_}Until this moment I--I could not recall my own name."
10059{_}2880{_}"H'm," muttered Fogerty; "you recall it now, don't you?"
10059{_}2881{_}"No.
10059{_}2882{_}You tell me my name is Melville, and you seem to recognize me as a man whom you once knew.
10059{_}2883{_}I accept your statement in good faith, but I cannot corroborate it from my own knowledge."
10059{_}2884{_}"That's queer," retorted Fogerty, his cold eyes fixed upon the man's face.
10059{_}2885{_}"Let me explain, please," said Smith, and related his curious experience in practically the same words he had employed when confiding it to Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}2886{_}"I had hoped," he concluded, "that if ever I met one who knew me formerly, or heard my right name mentioned, my memory would come back to me; but in this I am sorely disappointed.
10059{_}2887{_}Did you know me well, sir?"
10059{_}2888{_}"Pretty well," answered the detective, after a slight hesitation.
10059{_}2889{_}"Then tell me something about myself.
10059{_}2890{_}Tell me who I was."
10059{_}2891{_}"Here--in public?"
10059{_}2892{_}asked Fogerty, with a suggestive glance at the spectators, who had involuntarily crowded nearer.
10059{_}2893{_}Smith flushed, but gazed firmly into the faces surrounding him.
10059{_}2894{_}"Why not?"
10059{_}2895{_}he returned.
10059{_}2896{_}"These young ladies and Mr. Merrick accepted me without knowledge of my antecedents.
10059{_}2897{_}They are entitled to as full an explanation as--as I am."
10059{_}2898{_}"You place me, Melville, in a rather embarrassing position," declared Fogerty.
10059{_}2899{_}"This is a queer case--the queerest in all my experience.
10059{_}2900{_}Better let me post you in a private interview."
10059{_}2901{_}Smith trembled a bit, from nervousness; but he persisted in his demand.
10059{_}2902{_}"These people are entitled to the truth," said he.
10059{_}2903{_}"Tell us frankly all you know about me, and do not mince words--whatever the truth may be."
10059{_}2904{_}"Oh, it's not so bad," announced the detective, with a shrug; "or at least it wouldn't be in New York, among your old aristocratic haunts.
10059{_}2905{_}But here, in a quiet country town, among these generous and simple-hearted folks who have befriended you, the thing is rather difficult to say."
10059{_}2906{_}"Say it!"
10059{_}2907{_}commanded Smith.
10059{_}2908{_}"I will.
10059{_}2909{_}Many New Yorkers remember the firm of Melville & Ford, the cleverest pair of confidence men who ever undertook to fleece the wealthy lambs of the metropolis."
10059{_}2910{_}"Confidence men!"
10059{_}2911{_}gasped Smith, in a voice of horror.
10059{_}2912{_}"Yes, putting it mildly.
10059{_}2913{_}You were both jolly good fellows and made a host of friends.
10059{_}2914{_}You were well-groomed, rode in automobiles, frequented good clubs and had a stunning establishment on Sixty-sixth street where you entertained lavishly.
10059{_}2915{_}You could afford to, for there was where you fleeced your victims.
10059{_}2916{_}But it wasn't so very bad, as I said.
10059{_}2917{_}You chose the wealthy sons of the super-rich, who were glad to know such popular men-about-town as Harold Melville and Edgar Ford.
10059{_}2918{_}When one set of innocents had been so thoroughly trimmed that they compared notes and began to avoid you, you had only to pick up another bunch of lambs, for New York contains many distinct flocks of the species.
10059{_}2919{_}As they could afford to lose, none of them ever complained to the police, although the Central Office had an eye on you and knew your methods perfectly.
10059{_}2920{_}"Finally you made a mistake--or rather Ford did, for he was not as clever as you were.
10059{_}2921{_}He brought an imitation millionaire to your house; a fellow who was putting up a brazen front on the smallest sort of a roll.
10059{_}2922{_}You won his money and he denounced you, getting away with a pack of marked cards for evidence.
10059{_}2923{_}At this you both took fright and decided on a hasty retreat.
10059{_}2924{_}Gathering together your plunder--which was a royal sum, I'm convinced--you and Ford jumped into a motor car and--vanished from New York.
10059{_}2925{_}"The balance of your history I base on premise.
10059{_}2926{_}Ford has been located in Chicago, where, with an ample supply of money, he is repeating his New York operations; but Harold Melville has never been heard of until this day.
10059{_}2927{_}I think the true explanation is easily arrived at.
10059{_}2928{_}Goaded by cupidity--and perhaps envy of your superior talents--Ford took advantage of the situation and, finding the automobile speeding along a deserted road, knocked you on the head, tumbled you out of the car, and made off with your combined winnings.
10059{_}2929{_}The blow had the effect--not so uncommon as you think--of destroying your recollection of your past life, and you have for two years been wandering in total ignorance of what caused your affliction."
10059{_}2930{_}During this recital Smith sat with his eyes eagerly fixed upon the speaker's face, dwelling upon every word.
10059{_}2931{_}At the conclusion of the story he dropped his face in his hands a moment, visibly shuddering.
10059{_}2932{_}Then again he looked up, and after reading the circle of pitying faces confronting him he bravely met Mr. Merrick's eyes.
10059{_}2933{_}"Sir," he said in a voice that faltered in spite of his efforts to render it firm, "you now know who I am.
10059{_}2934{_}When I first came to you I was a mere irresponsible hobo, a wandering tramp who had adopted the name of Thursday Smith because he was ignorant of his own, but who had no cause to be ashamed of his manhood.
10059{_}2935{_}To-day I am discovered in my true guise.
10059{_}2936{_}As Harold Melville, the disreputable trickster, I am not fit to remain in your employ--to associate with honest men and women.
10059{_}2937{_}You will forgive my imposition, I think, because you know how thoroughly ignorant I was of the truth; but I will impose upon you no longer.
10059{_}2938{_}I am sorry, sir, for I have been happy here; but I will go, thanking you for the kindly generosity that prompted you to accept me as I seemed to be, not as I am."
10059{_}2939{_}He rose, his face showing evidence of suffering, and bowed gravely.
10059{_}2940{_}Hetty Hewitt walked over and stood by his side, laying her hand gently upon his arm.
10059{_}2941{_}But Thursday Smith did not know John Merrick very well.
10059{_}2942{_}The little gentleman had silently listened, observing meanwhile the demeanor of the accused, and now he smiled in his pleasant, whimsical way and caught Smith's hand in both his own.
10059{_}2943{_}"Man, man!"
10059{_}2944{_}he cried, "you're misjudging both me and yourself, I don't know this fellow Melville.
10059{_}2945{_}You don't know him, either.
10059{_}2946{_}But I do know Thursday Smith, who has won my confidence and by his manly acts, and I'll stand by him through thick and thin!"
10059{_}2947{_}"I am Harold Melville--the gambler--the confidence man."
10059{_}2948{_}"You're nothing of the sort, you're just Thursday Smith, and no more responsible for Harold Melville than I am."
10059{_}2949{_}"Hooray!"
10059{_}2950{_}exclaimed Patsy Doyle enthusiastically.
10059{_}2951{_}"Uncle's right, Thursday.
10059{_}2952{_}You're our friend, and the mainstay of the _Millville Daily Tribune_.
10059{_}2953{_}We shall not allow you to desert us just because you've discovered that your--your--ancestor--wasn't quite respectable."
10059{_}2954{_}"That's it, exactly," asserted Beth.
10059{_}2955{_}"It's like hearing a tale of an ancestor, Thursday, or of some member of your family who lived before you.
10059{_}2956{_}You cannot be responsible, in any way, for another man's wickedness."
10059{_}2957{_}"As I look at it," said Louise reflectively, "you are just two years old, Thursday, and innocent of any wrongdoing before that day you first found yourself."
10059{_}2958{_}"There's no use our considering Melville at all," added Uncle John cheerfully.
10059{_}2959{_}"I'm sorry we ever heard of him, except that in one way it clears up a mystery.
10059{_}2960{_}Thursday Smith, we like you and trust you.
10059{_}2961{_}Do not doubt yourself because of this tale.
10059{_}2962{_}I'll vouch for your fairness and integrity.
10059{_}2963{_}Forget Melville, who has never really existed so far as any of us are concerned; be yourself, and count on our friendship and regard, which Thursday Smith has fairly won."
10059{_}2964{_}Hetty was crying softly, her cheek laid against Thursday's sleeve.
10059{_}2965{_}The man stood as if turned to stone, but his cheeks were flushed, his eyes sparkling, and his head proudly poised.
10059{_}2966{_}Fogerty lighted a fresh cigarette, watching the scene with an imperturbable smile.
10059{_}2967{_}Suddenly Smith awoke to life.
10059{_}2968{_}He half turned, looked wonderingly at Hetty, and then folded her thin form in his arms and pressed a kiss on her forehead.
10059{_}2969{_}Fogerty coughed.
10059{_}2970{_}Uncle John jerked out his handkerchief and blew his nose like a bugle call.
10059{_}2971{_}The major's eyes were moist, for the old soldier was sympathetic as a child.
10059{_}2972{_}But Patsy, a little catch in her voice, impulsively put her arms around the unashamed pair and murmured: "I'm so glad, Hetty!
10059{_}2973{_}I'm so glad, Thursday!
10059{_}2974{_}But--dear me--aren't we going to have any paper to-morrow morning?"
10059{_}2975{_}That relieved the tension and everybody laughed.
10059{_}2976{_}Thursday released Hetty and shook Uncle John's hand most gratefully.
10059{_}2977{_}Then they all wanted to shake hands, and did until it came to Fogerty's turn.
10059{_}2978{_}But now Smith drew back and looked askance at the detective.
10059{_}2979{_}"I do not know you, Mr. McCormick," he said with dignity.
10059{_}2980{_}"My name's not McCormick; it's Fogerty," said the other, without malice.
10059{_}2981{_}"I was simply testing your memory by claiming to be an old friend.
10059{_}2982{_}Personally I never knew Harold Melville, but I'm mighty glad to make Thursday Smith's acquaintance and will consider it an honor if you'll shake my hand."
10059{_}2983{_}Smith was too happy to refuse.
10059{_}2984{_}He took Fogerty's hand.
10059{_}2985{_}CHAPTER XXIII  THE JOURNALISTS ABDICATE   Mr. Merrick told Thursday Smith, in an apologetic way, how he had hired Fogerty to unravel the mystery of his former life, and how the great detective had gone to work so intelligently and skillfully that, with the aid of a sketch Hetty had once made of the pressman, and which Mr. Merrick sent on, he had been able to identify the man and unearth the disagreeable details of his history.
10059{_}2986{_}Thursday was too humble, by this time, and too grateful, besides, to resent Uncle John's interference.
10059{_}2987{_}He admitted that, after all, it was better he should know the truth.
10059{_}2988{_}"I've nothing to bother me now but the future," he said, "and with God's help I mean to keep the name of Thursday Smith clean and free from any reproach."
10059{_}2989{_}After the interview he went about his duties as before and Hetty sat down at her desk and took the telegraphic news that came clicking over the wire as if nothing important in her life had occurred.
10059{_}2990{_}But the girl journalists were all excitement and already were beginning to plan the things they might do to Make Hetty and Thursday happier.
10059{_}2991{_}Cox and Booth had gone away and Mr. Merrick thanked Fogerty for his skillful service and gave him a fat check.
10059{_}2992{_}"It's a mighty interesting case, sir," declared the detective, "and I'm as glad as any of you that it has ended so comfortably.
10059{_}2993{_}Whatever Melville might have been--and his record is a little worse than I related it--there's no doubt of Thursday Smith's honesty.
10059{_}2994{_}He's a mighty fine fellow, and Fate played a proper trick when she blotted out his unscrupulous mind and left him as innocent as an unborn babe.
10059{_}2995{_}He will do well in his new life, I'm sure, and that girl of his, Hetty Hewitt--I've know of her reckless ways for years--has also redeemed herself and turned out a regular brick!
10059{_}2996{_}All of which, Mr. Merrick is unusual in real life, more's the pity, and therefore it makes even a cold-blooded detective feel good to witness it."
10059{_}2997{_}Mr. Merrick smiled benignantly and Fogerty drove over to the Junction to catch his train.
10059{_}2998{_}After luncheon, Patsy, while arranging her galley proofs, inquired of Louise for the local column.
10059{_}2999{_}"Hetty said she'd attend to it," was the reply; "but we are all upset to-day and things are at sixes and sevens."
10059{_}3000{_}"The column is all prepared, Miss Doyle," announced Hetty.
10059{_}3001{_}"Where is it?"
10059{_}3002{_}"Thursday has made it ready for the press.
10059{_}3003{_}It's--illustrated," she confessed.
10059{_}3004{_}"I'd rather you wouldn't see it until the paper is out, if you can trust me."
10059{_}3005{_}"To be sure," said Patsy.
10059{_}3006{_}"That's one responsibility I'm relieved of, anyhow."
10059{_}3007{_}The paper was a bit uneven in appearance next morning, but when Patsy came down to breakfast she found both Uncle John and the major roaring with laughter over Hetty's locals.
10059{_}3008{_}The first item stated that "Mrs. Thorne took tea at Sam Cotting's last evening," (the Cottings being notoriously inhospitable) and the picture showed Mrs. Thorne, a sour-faced woman, departing from the store with a package of tea.
10059{_}3009{_}Then came the announcement that "Eph Hildreth got shot at West's hardware store," and there was a picture of West weighing out a pound of buckshot for his customer.
10059{_}3010{_}The next item said: "Our distinguished fellow citizen, Marshall Peggy McNutt, was discovered unconscious on his front porch at 3 p.m." The drawing of McNutt was one of the best of the series.
10059{_}3011{_}It was his habit to "snooze" in an easy chair on his porch every afternoon, and Hetty depicted the little man with both feet--meat and wood--on the rail, his mouth open and eyes shut, while lusty snores were indicated by radiating lines and exclamation points.
10059{_}3012{_}The Widow Clark's cow occupied the next square, being tethered to a stake while Skim approached the animal with pail and milking-stool.
10059{_}3013{_}Below the drawing were the words: "Mr. Skimton Clark, cowward."
10059{_}3014{_}A few other local hits were concluded by a picture of Hon.
10059{_}3015{_}Ojoy Boglin shaking his fist at Mr. Skeelty, who held a package of money in his grasp labeled "insurance."
10059{_}3016{_}Below was the simple legend: "O Joy!"
10059{_}3017{_}The artist's cleverness became the subject of conversation at the breakfast table, and Arthur remarked:  "You won't be able to hold Hetty in Millville long.
10059{_}3018{_}Her talent enables her to draw big salaries in New York and it isn't likely she will consent to bury herself in this little town."
10059{_}3019{_}"I'm not so sure," said Patsy.
10059{_}3020{_}"If we can hold Thursday Smith we can hold Hetty, you know."
10059{_}3021{_}"We won't need to hold either of them for long," observed Beth; "for in another three weeks or so we must leave here and return to the city, when of course the _Millville Daily Tribune_ must suspend publication."
10059{_}3022{_}"I've been thinking of that," said Uncle John.
10059{_}3023{_}"So have I," declared Patsy.
10059{_}3024{_}"For a long time I was puzzled what to do, for I hated dreadfully to kill our dear _Tribune_ after we've made it such a nice paper.
10059{_}3025{_}Yet I knew very well we couldn't stay here all winter and run it.
10059{_}3026{_}But last night I had an inspiration.
10059{_}3027{_}Thursday will marry Hetty, I suppose, and they can both stay here and run the Tribune.
10059{_}3028{_}They are doing most of the work now.
10059{_}3029{_}If Uncle John agrees, we will sell out to them on 'easy terms.'"
10059{_}3030{_}"Good gracious, Patsy!"
10059{_}3031{_}chuckled the major, "wherever can the poor things borrow money to keep going?
10059{_}3032{_}Do you want to load onto an innocent bride an' groom the necessity of meeting a deficit of a couple of hundred dollars every week?"
10059{_}3033{_}Patsy's face fell.
10059{_}3034{_}"They have no money, I know," she said, "except what they earn."
10059{_}3035{_}"And their wages'll be cut off when they begin hiring themselves," added the major.
10059{_}3036{_}"No; you can't decently thrust such an incubus on Hetty and Thursday--or on anyone else.
10059{_}3037{_}You've been willing to pay the piper for the sake of the dance, but no one else would do it."
10059{_}3038{_}"Quite true," agreed Arthur.
10059{_}3039{_}"The days of the _Millville Tribune_ are numbered."
10059{_}3040{_}"Let us not settle that question just yet," proposed Mr. Merrick, who had been deep in thought.
10059{_}3041{_}"I'll consider Patsy's proposition for awhile and then talk with Thursday.
10059{_}3042{_}The paper belongs to the girls, but the outfit is mine, and I suppose I may do what I please with it when my nieces retire from journalism."
10059{_}3043{_}Even the major could not demur at this statement and so the conversation dropped.
10059{_}3044{_}During the next few days Uncle John visited the printing office several times and looked over the complete little plant with speculative eyes.
10059{_}3045{_}Then one day he made a trip to Malvern, thirty miles up the railway line from the Junction, where a successful weekly paper had long been published.
10059{_}3046{_}He interviewed the editor, examined the outfit critically, and after asking numerous questions returned to Millville in excellent spirits.
10059{_}3047{_}Then he invited Thursday Smith and Hetty to dine at the farm on Saturday evening, which was the one evening in the week they were free, there being no Sunday morning paper.
10059{_}3048{_}Thursday had bought a new suit of clothes since he came to the _Tribune_, and Hetty, after much urging, finally prevailed upon him to accept the invitation.
10059{_}3049{_}When the young man appeared at the farm he wore his new suit with an air of perfect ease that disguised its cheapness, and it was noticed that he seemed quite at home in the handsome living-room, where the party assembled after dinner.
10059{_}3050{_}"I am in search of information, Thursday," said Uncle John in his pleasant way.
10059{_}3051{_}"Will you permit me to question you a bit?"
10059{_}3052{_}"Certainly, sir."
10059{_}3053{_}"And you, Hetty?"
10059{_}3054{_}"Ask anything you like, sir."
10059{_}3055{_}"Thank you.
10059{_}3056{_}To begin with, what are your future plans?
10059{_}3057{_}I understand, of course, you are to be married; but--afterward?"
10059{_}3058{_}"We haven't considered that as yet, sir," replied Thursday thoughtfully.
10059{_}3059{_}"Of course we shall stay with the _Tribune_ as long as you care to employ our services; but--"  "Well?"
10059{_}3060{_}"I have been given to understand the young ladies plan to return to New York at the end of September, and in that case of course the paper will suspend."
10059{_}3061{_}"My nieces will be obliged to abandon journalism, to be sure," said Mr. Merrick; "but I see no reason why the paper should suspend.
10059{_}3062{_}How would you and Hetty like to remain in Millville and run it?"
10059{_}3063{_}Both Thursday and Hetty smiled, but it was the man who answered;  "We cannot afford such a luxury, sir."
10059{_}3064{_}"Would you care to make your future home in Millville?"
10059{_}3065{_}"Oh, yes!"
10059{_}3066{_}exclaimed Hetty.
10059{_}3067{_}"I love the quaint little town dearly, and the villagers are all my friends.
10059{_}3068{_}I'm sure Thursday doesn't care to go back to New York, where--where Harold Melville once lived.
10059{_}3069{_}But, as he truly says, we couldn't make a living with the _Tribune_, even if you gave us the use of the plant."
10059{_}3070{_}"Let us see about that," said Uncle John.
10059{_}3071{_}"I will admit, in advance, that a daily paper in such a place is absurd.
10059{_}3072{_}None of us quite understood that when we established the _Tribune_.
10059{_}3073{_}My nieces thought a daily the only satisfactory sort of newspaper, because they were used to such, but it did not take long to convince me--and perhaps them--that in spite of all our efforts the _Millville Daily Tribune_ would never thrive.
10059{_}3074{_}It is too expensive to pay its own way and requires too much work to be a pleasant plaything.
10059{_}3075{_}Only unbounded enthusiasm and energy have enabled my clever nieces to avoid being swamped by the monster their ambition created."
10059{_}3076{_}"That," said Patsy, with a laugh, "is very clearly and concisely put, my dear Uncle."
10059{_}3077{_}"It was never intended to be a permanent thing, anyhow," continued Mr. Merrick; "yet I must express my admiration for the courage and talent my nieces have displayed in forcing a temporary success where failure was the logical conclusion.
10059{_}3078{_}Shortly, however, they intend to retire gracefully from the field of journalism, leaving me with a model country newspaper plant on my hands.
10059{_}3079{_}Therefore it is I, Thursday and Hetty, and not my nieces, who have a proposition to place before you.
10059{_}3080{_}"While a daily paper is not appropriate in Millville, a weekly paper, distributed throughout Chazy County, would not only be desirable but could be made to pay an excellent yearly profit.
10059{_}3081{_}Through the enterprise of Joe Wegg, Millville is destined to grow rapidly from this time on, and Chazy County is populous enough to support a good weekly paper, in any event.
10059{_}3082{_}Therefore, my proposition is this: To turn the plant over to Mr. and Mrs. Thursday Smith, who will change the name to the _Millville Weekly Tribune_ and run it as a permanent institution.
10059{_}3083{_}Your only expense for labor will be one assistant to set type and do odd jobs, since you are so competent that you can attend to all else yourselves.
10059{_}3084{_}We will cut out the expensive news service we have heretofore indulged in and dispense with the private telegraph wire.
10059{_}3085{_}Joe Wegg says he'll furnish you with what power you need free of all charge, because the paper will boost Millville's interests, with which his own interests are identified.
10059{_}3086{_}Now, then, tell me what you think of my proposal."
10059{_}3087{_}Hetty and Thursday had listened attentively and their faces proved they were enthusiastic over the idea.
10059{_}3088{_}They said at once they would be glad to undertake the proposition.
10059{_}3089{_}"However," said Thursday, after a little reflection, "there are two things that might render our acceptance impossible.
10059{_}3090{_}I suppose you will require rent for the outfit; but for a time, until we get well started, we could not afford to pay as much as you have a right to demand."
10059{_}3091{_}"I have settled on my demands," replied Mr. Merrick, "and hope you will agree to them.
10059{_}3092{_}You must pay me for the use of the outfit twenty per cent of your net profits, over and above all your operating and living expenses.
10059{_}3093{_}When this sum has reimbursed me for my investment, the outfit will belong to you."
10059{_}3094{_}Thursday Smith looked his amazement.
10059{_}3095{_}"That seems hardly business-like, sir," he protested.
10059{_}3096{_}"You are right; but this isn't entirely a business deal.
10059{_}3097{_}You are saving my nieces the humiliation of suspending the paper they established and have labored on so lovingly.
10059{_}3098{_}Moreover, I regard you and Hetty as friends whom I am glad to put in the way of a modest but--I venture to predict--a successful business career.
10059{_}3099{_}What is your second objection?"
10059{_}3100{_}"I heard Mr. West say the other day that he would soon need the building we occupy to store his farm machinery in."
10059{_}3101{_}"True; but I have anticipated that.
10059{_}3102{_}I have completed plans for the erection of a new building for the newspaper, which will be located on the vacant lot next to the hotel.
10059{_}3103{_}I purchased the lot a long time ago.
10059{_}3104{_}The new building, for which the lumber is already ordered, will be a better one than the shed we are now in, and on the second floor I intend to have a cozy suite of rooms where you and Hetty can make a home of your own.
10059{_}3105{_}Eh?
10059{_}3106{_}How does that strike you, my children?"
10059{_}3107{_}Their faces were full of wonder and delight.
10059{_}3108{_}"The new building goes with the outfit, on the same terms," continued Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}3109{_}"That is I take one-fifth of your net profits for the whole thing."
10059{_}3110{_}"But, sir," suggested Thursday, "suppose no profits materialize?"
10059{_}3111{_}"Then I have induced you to undertake a poor venture and must suffer the consequences, which to me will be no hardship at all.
10059{_}3112{_}In that case I will agree to find some better business for you, but I am quite positive you will make a go of the _Millville Weekly Tribune_."
10059{_}3113{_}"I think so, too, Mr. Merrick, or I would not accept your generous offer," replied Smith.
10059{_}3114{_}"What do you think, Hetty?"
10059{_}3115{_}"The idea pleases me immensely," she declared.
10059{_}3116{_}"It is a splendid opportunity for us, and will enable us to live here quietly and forget the big outside world.
10059{_}3117{_}New York has had a bad influence on both you and me, Thursday, and here we can begin a new life of absolute respectability."
10059{_}3118{_}"When do you intend to be married?"
10059{_}3119{_}asked Patsy.
10059{_}3120{_}"We have scarcely thought of that, as yet, for until this evening we did not know what the future held in store for us."
10059{_}3121{_}"Couldn't you arrange the wedding before we leave?"
10059{_}3122{_}asked Beth.
10059{_}3123{_}"It would delight us so much to be present at the ceremony."
10059{_}3124{_}"I think we owe the young ladies that much, Thursday," said Hetty, after a brief hesitation.
10059{_}3125{_}"Nothing could please me better," he asserted eagerly.
10059{_}3126{_}So they canvassed the wedding, and Patsy proposed they transfer the paper to Thursday and Hetty--to become a weekly instead of a daily--in a week's time, and celebrate the wedding immediately after the second issue, so as to give the bridal couple a brief vacation before getting to work again.
10059{_}3127{_}Neither of them wished to take a wedding trip, and Mr. Merrick promised to rush the work on the new building so they could move into their new rooms in the course of a few weeks.
10059{_}3128{_}CHAPTER XXIV  A CHEERFUL BLUNDER   "We would like to ask your advice about one thing, sir," said Thursday Smith to Mr. Merrick, a little later that same evening.
10059{_}3129{_}"Would it be legal for me to marry under the name of Thursday Smith, or must I use my real name--Harold Melville?"
10059{_}3130{_}Uncle John could not answer this question, nor could the major or Arthur.
10059{_}3131{_}Hetty and her fianc had both decided to cling to the name of Thursday Smith thereafter, and they disliked to be married under any other--especially the detestable one of Harold Melville.
10059{_}3132{_}"An act of legislature would render your new name legal, I believe," said Mr. Merrick; "but such an act could not be passed until after the date you have planned to be married."
10059{_}3133{_}"But if it was made legal afterward it wouldn't matter greatly," suggested the major.
10059{_}3134{_}"I do not think it matters at all," asserted Hetty.
10059{_}3135{_}"It's the man I'm marrying, not his name.
10059{_}3136{_}I don't much care what he calls himself."
10059{_}3137{_}"Oh, but it must be legal, you know!"
10059{_}3138{_}exclaimed Patsy.
10059{_}3139{_}"You don't care now, perhaps, but you might in the future.
10059{_}3140{_}We cannot be certain, you know, that Thursday is entirely free from his former connection with Harold Melville."
10059{_}3141{_}"Quite true," agreed the major.
10059{_}3142{_}"Then," said Smith, with evident disappointment, "I must use the hateful name of Melville for the wedding, and afterward abandon it for as long as possible."
10059{_}3143{_}The nieces were greatly pleased with Uncle John's arrangement, which relieved them of the newspaper and also furnished Thursday and Hetty, of whom they had grown really fond, with a means of gaining a livelihood.
10059{_}3144{_}Millville accepted the new arrangement with little adverse comment, the villagers being quite satisfied with a weekly paper, which would cost them far less than the daily had done.
10059{_}3145{_}Everyone was pleased to know Thursday Smith had acquired the business, for both he and Hetty had won the cordial friendship of the simple-hearted people and were a little nearer to them than "the nabob's girls" could ever be.
10059{_}3146{_}Preparations were speedily pushed forward for the wedding, which the nieces undertook to manage themselves, the prospective bride and groom being too busy at the newspaper office to devote much attention to the preliminaries of the great event.
10059{_}3147{_}The ceremony was to take place at the farmhouse of Mr. Merrick, and every inhabitant of Millville was invited to be present.
10059{_}3148{_}The minister would drive over from Hooker's Falls, and the ceremony was to be followed by a grand feast, for which delicacies were to be imported from New York.
10059{_}3149{_}The girls provided a complete trousseau for Hetty, as their wedding present, while Arthur and the major undertook to furnish the new apartments, which were already under construction.
10059{_}3150{_}Uncle John's gift was a substantial check that would furnish the newly married couple with modest capital to promote their business or which they could use in case of emergencies.
10059{_}3151{_}It was the very day before the wedding that Fogerty gave them so great and agreeable a surprise that Uncle John called it "Fogerty's Wedding Present" ever afterward.
10059{_}3152{_}In its physical form it was merely a telegram, but in its spiritual and moral aspect it proved the greatest gift Thursday and Hetty were destined to receive.
10059{_}3153{_}The telegram was dated from New York and read as follows:  "Harold Melville just arrested here for passing a bogus check under an assumed name.
10059{_}3154{_}Have interviewed him and find he is really Melville, so Thursday Smith must be some one else, and doubtless a more respectable character.
10059{_}3155{_}Shall I undertake to discover his real identity?"
10059{_}3156{_}Uncle John let Thursday and Hetty answer this question, and their reply was a positive "no!"
10059{_}3157{_}"The great Fogerty made such a blunder the first time," said Hetty, who was overjoyed at the glorious news, "that he might give poor Thursday another dreadful scare if he tackled the job again.
10059{_}3158{_}Let the mystery remain unfathomable."
10059{_}3159{_}"But, on the contrary, my dear, Fogerty might discover that Thursday was some eminent and good man--as I am firmly convinced is the truth," suggested Mr. Merrick.
10059{_}3160{_}"He's that right now," asserted Hetty.
10059{_}3161{_}"For my part, I prefer to know nothing of his former history, and Thursday says the present situation thoroughly contents him."
10059{_}3162{_}"I am more than contented," said Thursday, with a happy smile.
10059{_}3163{_}"Hetty has cured me of my desire to wander, and no matter what I might have been in the past I am satisfied to remain hereafter a country editor."
